THE CALUMET...
in the palmy days of the North American Indians was a symbol of peace.
The fragrant weed—crude, but still fragrant was appreciated just as much
by the savage of that day as
CAMEO CKSARETTCS
are by men of taste in our day
THE BED PAGE .
About the middle of last century there was
born to a decent Scottish laboring family a
son whom they called Robert. His surname
was Burns, and he lived to write verses—
many verses ; and very many verses have
been written about him. He uses his own
name as a rhyme in the Dumfries election
ballad—
O, that my e’en were flowing burns—
but he did his countrymen grave injury by
using up the rhyme as a name. Had he
called himself Gurns, or Murns, or Durns, it
would have been far easier to make verses
about him ; and an immense burden would
have been lifted from the pen of a nation
which rhymes, as it jokes, ‘ wi’ deeficulty.’
* * * *
But a graver injury has been done by
Burns’s countrymen to Burns himself. He
was a capable fellow, and he probably knew
pretty nearly how good or how bad his verses
were. Further, he knew pretty nearly how
much he had borrowed from the verses of
others. The average Scotchman does not
know how good or how bad Burns’s verses
are, and he does not know how much Burns
plagiarised. So he frequently says, even in
his sober moments, that Burns is the greatest
poet that the world has seen—which is non-
sense, as Burns would tell him were Burns
alive. The effect of such over-praise is to
arouse antagonists who unduly depreciate,
and in the strife between bias and anti-bias
the truth usually escapes through a cracked
argument and is lost altogether.
* * * *
Burns was just a lively, hearty fellow,
with a knack of rhyming, a lot of sense and
humor, and some susceptibility to pathetic
appeals. But he had not a first-rate poetic
brain ; he was perhaps not sufficiently cul-
tured or sufficiently meditative for a poet;
and he was physically too healthy to rise to
exalted planes of imagination. His verses
are the product not of genius, but of ener-
getic, exuberant talent.
* * * *
You can divide writers into two extreme
classes—those with bad-quality brains and
good-quality bodies, and those with good-
quality brains and bad-quality bodies. The
man with the first-class brain has never
energy enough to make the most of it ; he
produces by fits and starts, but what he
produces makes a mark. The man with the
first-class body has never a brain to corres-
pond (that is, he has a brain to correspond ;
he has probably a healthy brain ; and good
writing comes from a brain unhealthy, over-
stimulated) ; he produces much, and nothing
of value. Between the extremes lies every
variety of mean. And the statement applies
in all fields of artistic achievement.
* * * *
Burns was of the Shakespeare type—the
type of extense, not incense, energy—the
type with a fair brain and enormous force—
with an engine requiring 100 h.p. and a
boiler supplying 300, or 500, or 1000 h.p.
The liver of such men is a physiological
poem better than any they ever gave to
literature. What beautiful bile Shakespeare
must have had !
* * * *
But Burns was not wise: he blew
off steam in all directions : he was a
Man Who Couldn’t Keep Still. And so
susceptible ! The Muse is jealous of a wife—
and Burns asked her to live in a harem ! The
girls liked Bums, too, at least as well as he
liked them. No wonder—he was a fine-
looking fellow ; he had ‘ the look and the
leg ’ which Droz’s aunt demands, and ‘ the
spirit of a man, not of a louse,’ and ‘ a fine,
takin’ way wi’ the sex ’ —‘ that sex the most
amiable of the works of God.’
Their tricks an’ craft hae put me daft,
They’ve taen me in, an’ a’ that ;
But clear your decks, an’ here’s the Sex !
I like the jads for a’ that.
His verse had charms only second to those
of his person—the Kilmarnock volume saved
Mrs. Dunlop (so she said), from ‘ the mad-
house or the grave.’ But the lass who, as
Burns declares, first drew song from the
chaos of his breast, was not satisfied with
woful ballads to her eyebrow. And think of
all the lasses that came after ! If Burns had
been half as good a man he might have been
twice as good a poet. He reverses Jean
Ingelow’s case : her poems are her legiti-
mate children, his illegitimate children are
his poems.
* * * *
And Burns was a type of his nation. The
Scotch are the least poetical of the nations
allied as Great Britain and Ireland, but they
have the highest illegitimacy rate. Two and
two make four.
* * * •
Burns amuses and interests, but he can
rarely elevate or inspire anyone save a
Scotchman. Burns inspires a Scotchman
less because he is a poet than because he is
Scotch. ‘ For the people who like that kind
of thing, that kind of thing is precisely what
those people like.’ Burns seems to stand for
the shrewd common-sense, the droll mother-
wit of his nation. He fits his unimaginative
countrymen, and they fit him. The average
Scotchman is not impressed by poetry.
While the poet’s eye is rolling in a fine
frenzy, the Scotchman is calculating what
is the value of the frenzy at sixty days’
sight, and wondering if he can secure a dis-
count on the strength of auld lang syne. In
business hours, anyway.
* * * *
But the Scotchman is impressed by Scotch-
ness. Burns appeals to hereditary patriotism,
to fond memories of childhood, to ancient
honored customs. And his Doric stirs the
Scotchman’s soul—or brain, rather—evoking
the lively impressions of youth in all their
vigor. His homely verse suits homely minds;
and his lack of novelty and subjective stimu-
lus makes him all the more acceptable to
people who want, not new or elevated ideas,
but their own old, ordinary ideas set in new
and attractive form. That is, most of Burns’s
verses are rhymed commonplace, and there-
fore popular. If they were miracles of
ethereal exaltation, like Shelley’s, they would
be unpopular, and poetry.
* * * »
Burns was an earth-born, not a heaven-
born poet: he was stimulated by earthy
hopes, fears, joys, sorrows : his verses are
earthy—he never soars to the empyrean, but
always hovers round the local scene, girl,
hypocrite, topic, legend. Burns is the
Scotch Villon, and Villon the Scotch
Burns: no one in literature has ever loved
women or hated priests in quite the same
way as these two men ; and honest hearts
naturally cleave to them. But it is one
thing to say Burns was a fine fellow : an-
other to say he was a fine poet. Burns
the man is always more worthy of interest
and admiration than Burns the poet: it is
the man in his poetry that compels affection.
He appeals to every man’s manhood : he
hated shams and lies; he loved drink and
the lasses ; his words were bonds, his oaths
round Doric ones, his thoughts pure messen-
gers sent straight from heaven—but it was
Venus’s heaven, not Mary’s.
* * * *
The Burns of the popular Scottish imagin-
ation is a kind of fetish, which every Scottish
editor has whitewashed until hardly a bit of
the divinity’s true skin is visible. Burns the
drunkard. Bums the rake, Burns the writer
of bawdy songs, is carefully toned down in
his own supposed interest and for the sake
of the alleged morals of his nation. His
editors’ injunction is the photographer’s
‘ Look pleasant ! ’ and they make him look
pleasant by as far as possible divesting him
of character and individuality. But—shock-
ing to relate ! —there has been recently pub-
lished an edition of Burns which not only
puts no whitewash on, but even takes some
off! Great has been the consternation in
the camps of orthodoxy. Yet Henley and
Henderson still appreciate at a little
more than their worth the universal claims
of the poet. The criticisms are still
screwed up a peg or two because of their
reference. The ‘ Centenary ’ editors are
influenced in spite of themselves by the
Burns mana —by the thought present all
the time at the back of their heads
that they are dealing with England's envy
and Scotia’s pride. But their illusion
seems the least that is possible to
Scotchmen. They call the lap-dog for
which Burns wrote an epitaph ‘ a lit-
tle beast’; they talk of ‘the cad in
Burns O Sacred Haggis !—and expose a
billet-seeking democrat writing slushy verses
in honor of Lord President Dundas. For
though Burns was a sturdy, independent
fellow, he had an inherited respect for titles
and dignities. He crawled unwillingly,
maybe ; yet sometimes he did crawl in fact
and in rhyme, liberally greasing patrons or
possible patrons from excess of gratitude or
in hope of favors.
* * * *
The chief value of the ‘ Centenary ’ edition
lies in the evidence it provides for exactly
estimating Burns’s rank as a poet. It has
always been known or guessed that he was
scarcely ever a completely original writer ;
but detailed proof was hard to come at.
Burns’s friends and relations, with intent to
heighten his reputation, deliberately de-
stroyed many of the old ballads from which
he borrowed; some of his material was
lost in the process of years ; some,
probably, is still suppressed by the holders ;
and some has remained unknown in various
collections. By dint of brains and industry,
Henley and Henderson have placed Burns’s
plagiarisms more clearly before the world
than ever these were placed before. They
were particularly fortunate in discovering in
the British Museum the Herd MS., of which
Burns made large use in compiling the songs
attributed t© him. And the conclusion justi-
fies the long-standing suspicion that Burns is
BURNS largely because the average reader
doesn’t bother about notes and commentaries.
* * * *
The ‘ Centenary ’ Burns is issued in four
volumes, of which three are published. In
the first two volumes —containing the Kil-
marnock book, the additional Edinburgh
poems, and posthumous pieces—there are
doubtfully ten lines that reach the elevation
of thought and style demanded from the
best poetry ; and two-thirds of the matter
is worthless in the abstract, and only
valuable or interesting because penned by
Burns the man. The remaining third dis-
plays Burns in his true poetic colors —the
most genuine Burns there is—employing his
astonishing energy in describing homely
scenes like * Hallowe’en,’ or in dashing off
humorous pictures like ‘ Tam O’Shanter.’
Burns’s mind was very alert and shrewd
within its range, but its range was singularly
narrow. His lack of imagination kept him
from soaring far from his facts—he is always
a tethered goat grazing in an objective circle.
It is true that Burns’s virile heat often simu-
lates imagination, and songs like ‘ Corn Rigs ’
—husks which Burns built round others’
kernels—may be classed as poetry ; but not
with the highest poetry.
* • * *
This early Burns is the most original
Burns there is, but Burns was never original.
His earlier poems are Fergusson plus his own
vitality ; his later poems represent his own
skill and taste plus the vitality of a hundred
unknown balladists. You can parallel nearly
every masterpiece of Burns with a master-
piece similar in kind, and as good in degree
(though not always in extent) by Tannahill,
or Hogg, or Ramsay, or Cunningham, or
half-a-dozen others. Burns never put more
humor in five verses than Alexander Ross
put into ‘ Woo’d, and Married, and a’.’ He
never put as much pathos in a ballad as
William Hamilton put into ‘The Braes of
Yarrow,’or Allan Cunningham into ‘She’s
Gane to Dwall in Heaven.’ He never put
more tender sentiment in a song than Hogg
put into ‘When the Kye Comes Hame.’
‘ The Siller Gun ’ is as good a bit of descrip-
tive verse as any but two or three by Burns.
Certainly no Scottish poet approaches Burns
as a satirist. But his characteristic virtue is
that he blew others’ sparks to fire. By dint
of his versatile energy he summed up and
excelled the achievements of a dozen poets,
on a dozen lines. He ploughed with other
men’s ploughs in many ways, in many fields,
and often cut the deepest furrow of all.
Other poets show different sides of the Scot-
tish nation : Burns is like Shakespeare—he
comprehends all sides. And his irresistible
vitality dings his poems into the universal
mind.
* * » •
The early Burns survives as proverbs sur-
vive—because his language is a pithy, vigor-
ous concentration of what every Scotchman
thinks and feels, and continually needs and
wishes to express. The later Burns survives
in virtue of the ability with which he milked
other men’s cows into his own bucket. The
songs attributed to Burns have always been
considered the most intrinsically poetic part
of his literary legacy. Yet the slightest con-
sideration would have shown how unlikely it
was that Burns should write verse in his
fresh and ardent youth and poetry in his
weary and embittered maturity. As a
matter of fact nearly all that is best in
‘ the songs of Burns ’ never belonged to
Burns at all was never conceived by
him, never written by him. Burns the
songster was an inspired vamper, tinker,
mender, and little more. For the most
part he never claimed to be anything more.
He was employed, or employed himself,
to supply words to collections of Scottish
airs—just as a pantomime-writer supplies
‘ words and songs ’ —and he got them some-
times out of his memory, sometimes by re-
search like Walter Scott’s among auld wives
and out-of-the-way neighborhoods, some-
times from his head. Sometimes he im-
proved his originals; sometimes he didn’t ;
but the words were vamped to suit the airs,
and that was the main point. The
collections for which he wrote were
in essence musical, not literary ; and
the words were no more meant to be
criticised alone than a frame is meant to be
criticised without a picture.
* * ♦ »
But throughout the work, almost with-
out exception, the old balladists show
by far the livelier imagination and the
firmer touch. Burns’s poetic judgment
when he made his collection was excel-
lent ; but his poetic faculty was much
impaired, and the highest kind of faculty
he never had. The results of a critical
survey are startling. The original Burns
pieces contain for the most part the dull,
stilted, artificial sentiments of his letters.
The freshness, the spirit, the truth and ten-
derness—nearly all that makes the songs
worth prizing, Burns usually borrowed as
he went along. The two best lines in
‘ Scots Wha Ha’e ’ are not Burns. ‘ Auld
Lang Syne ’ is not Burns. ‘ Cornin’
through the Rye ’is not Burns. ‘ We’re a’
Noddin ' is not Burns. The beautiful ‘My
love is like the red, red rose ’ is not Burns.
And so one goes on. Of 252 songs in the
collection attributed to Burns, 36 of the best
contain so little of him that they should be
forthwith struck out of his book. About
104 are more or less vamped—generally
more—and practically do not belong to
Burns either. The remaining 112 comprise
those which are indubitably by Burns ; or
which cannot be traced to other sources ; or
which, though based on older models, have
been rebuilt or all-but. And these 112 are,
with some notable exceptions, the weakest
lot in the whole collection.
» * * *
This notice, of course, does not profess to
deal thoroughly with the adequacies and in-
adequacies of Burns ; only to hint how far
the true conception of him is removed from
the conception which is legendary and Scot-
tish. In any case hapless Caledonia need not
mourn for laurels reft and banners torn.
The credit which is taken from Burns is
merely transferred to Scotland.,

Various Verses .
M.L. Agricultural Dept, expert Barker
says cancer in meat is not nearly so common as is
generally thought, and adds that cancer has six
victims among vegetarians for every half-dozen
among cannibals. or meat-eaters. Nor, he con-
tends, is cancer contagious between man and man
or beast and beast. But, then, so many people
make broad assertions, while nobody can tell you
anything really definite about the cause of cancer.
ROMANCE AND COMMERCE.
[FOR TUB BULLETIN.]
Heart-young and true ! In long-dead and rotten
springs
A youth I knew whose soul shone like an eye.
And his day-dreams gave him wings over mean
and sordid things—
Oh ! my memory’s ear can hear him as his clarion
song he sings—
Oh ! woe that such a youth should die !
Heart-high! head-vveak ! And so he sang his
theme ;
Pure of purpose, he, and chivalrous of thought;
In his eye Hope’s gallant gleam, and the world
seemed all a dream,
And life’s turgid sewer-channel was a lotus-preg-
nant stream,
And not anything was ever sold or bought!
Beauty was his world 1 And the bright Septem-
ber leaves
Never withered in the summer nor grew shaded
with the day.
Oh ! the tales young Fancy weaves—ere Time and
Place are thieves—
When the nearest signs of death are the glowing
golden sheaves,
And the grass is living even in the hay.
Nature told ! He heard the growing of the clover,
And the rustlings of their courtships from the
grasses lush and deep !
Unto him, like any lover, would the Bush herself
discover,
And the Earth gushed roses under, and chrome
the Sun threw over ;
And the Mysteries sang their depths to him in
sleep. •
Dead is the soul! To me now no star sings !
No Heaven-born chords are struck upon my
heart!
No more the high hope springs, but the world of
commerce flings
Many gifts in compensation for that Height it
never brings—
Now the Temple is the money-changer’s mart.
Randolph Bedford.
POSSESSION.
What in the world can take you from me now,
Wholly and solely yielded unto me ;
! So, with my lips, X seal it on your brow,
The bond whereof but death may make us free !
Tor you are mine though all the world said nay,
Nor lightly won, nor lightly lost shall be ;
Yet who could part us, taking you away,
Since taking you were taking half of me.
Love !—like a boat wherein the oarsman dreams,
Drifting at mercy of the changing tide,
Until a hailing voice from sleep redeems
The dreamer, and he makes his light craft glide
Swift on its way—l drifted long alone
Till through my slumbering soul your strange
voice thrilled
Like wind in airy harps. The magic tone
Passed, but my soul awoke, resolved, strong-
willed !
Trom that far hour with firm, unwavering eyes
Upon the object of all life —your love—•
I strove to gain it; yea, the starry skies
To win your heart I would have sought to move!
But now when all I ever hoped is mine,
Mine, only mine, ’tis all so dear and strange
I almost doubt, and fear to make a sign
Lest I should wake and all my dreaming change
To those dark, desolate days ere love was known,
When sad, with empty heart, I lived alone.
Ah, if it be that eyes and lips and ears,
Deceived by some wandering fantasy,
Should find, alas ! this dear face, wet with tears,
This tremulous mouth curved so beseechingly,
And low voice as of purling pebbly streams,
Dissolve like night-mists when the sun at morn,
High on the peaks, doth shame them with his
beams ;
Melt like a strain of music lightly borne
By fitful winds, that ere it can declare
Some strange new meaning, dies upon the air !
So, heart of mine, once more say o’er the words—
The little broken words and trembling sighs—
That fluttered on your lips like nestling birds ;
Love, let me hear them, gazing in your eyes,
And know that after weary noons that waned
In famished nights, our lips may feast their fill
Of kisses, and in circling arms constrained
That we may lie for ever if we will.
Yet one more kiss ! This is my day of days,
Unknown of death, though every other die;
Your love has crowned it with immortal bays,
Within my heart for temple set on high.
Mine, only mine, again and yet again
I say the words that seem so weak to hold
Such wondrous weight of meaning clear and
plain ;
Mine, only mine, till all our days are told.
Yea, though life’s fire be fallen to dull embers,
Bed flame will glow whenever love remembers !
ZADIG.
ACELDAMA.
Thirty pieces of silver, the price of the innocent
blood !
What can we do with the reeking coin, too vile
for the gutter-mud ?
We will take it and add to it, daily, the fee from
some woman’s shame,
The profit from childten’s labor, or the price of a
tainted name.
We will buy us “ the Field of the Potter we
will sub-divide—and then
We shall blossom and burgeon as landlords—as
Lords of the Land —and men.
But—the “Field of Blood ”no longer ; too much
would the words reveal!
Let us call it on poster and inset the “ Township
of Judasville.”
Let us gather the tithes and the rentals—the
wages of death and sin ;
With rack-rent and lease and mortgage—“ to bury
the strangers in ! ”
Maoriland.
EDWARD TREGEAR.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copy right by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or’ Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand, that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 11 and 13 Wool Exchange,
London, E.C. ; Gordon and Gotch, Ludgate Circus; Street
& Co.. Cornhill.

dard and Diggers News Co., Limited, Johannesburg.

In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Intervrovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
£l, with .2s. ’2d. added for postage. England £l, with
6s. 6d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

Owing to the imposition of lid. postage, the subscription
to The Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. fid. per
annum, payable in advance.

postage —lid. on a Bulletin of ordinary weight—now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition of The Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.
SATURDAY, OCTOBER 23, 1897.
Groing Back on Federation.
Very unobtrusively a combination of parties
lately shunted a measure through all its
important stages in N.S. W. Assembly, which
greatly reduces, if it does not quite destroy,
the present chances of Federation. The
measure was fathered by Levien and Neild,
both hardened parochialists of the old school.
It passed its second reading by a majority
of 19 —a very mixed majority made up from
all political classes. If any one of the three
parties had opposed it in dead earnest it
would certainly have been lost, but no one
was in dead earnest. Premier Reid voted
against the second reading as a matter of
form, but afterwards assisted in carrying the
measure through. Also, the Government
carefully left the matter open, and brought
no party influence to bear against it. The
bulk of the Freetrade party took the hiht
conveyed by this attitude, and went with the
anti-Federal cause. Lyne, See, Willis,
Crick and other Protectionists did likewise,
and the bulk of the Labor members present
followed suit. There are hardly more than
two or three politicians in the present New
South Wales Assembly who venture to say
openly that they are against Federation
in every form. But in a House of
73 members there were no less than
46 who grabbed enthusiastically at the
best chance they could get to strangle
it on a side issue. And unless the measure
gets lost in the Leg. Council—which, con-
sidering the narrow, parochial instincts of
that Chamber of Fossils, is wholly unlikely
—Federation, so far as New South Wales is
concerned, is probably dead—very dead in-
deed, and utterly deceased.
* * * •
The question was such an unobtrusive side
issue that it passed almost unnoticed, so far
as the community was concerned. Accord-
ing to the provisions of the Federal Enabling
Act, when the Federal Constitution is finally
drawn up it is to be submitted to a popular
vote in each province. The people are to
accept it or reject it—to give a straight
“Yes” or “No.” If New South Wales
decided to enter the Federation it was to
give a majority of affirmative votes, and the
affirmative votes must also number not less
than 50,000. If there were 49,999 votes for
Federation and only one for Provincialism,
still Provincialism was to carry the day,
which was giving the old order of things a
pretty long start over the new one. Poli-
tician Levien, of Quirindi, asked the Li aise
to make this bad state of things immeasur-
ably worse by requiring that more than half
the electors of New South Wales must vote
in the affirmative before anything could be
done, which, as it is only on rare occasions
that much more than half the electors take
the trouble to vote at all, meant making
Federation absolutely impossible. Jawbone
Neild proposed 100,000 as the minimum
Federal vote, and finally Premier Reid
offered to compromise on 80,000, which
figure was accepted by a huge majority.
* * * *
This was an utterly gratuitous breach of
faith with the other provinces and an act of
the meanest treachery on the part of the Reid
Government and everybody else concerned.
N.S. Wales entered into the Federal move-
ment on certain conditions laid down by its
own Parliament. The 50,000 minimum was
one of these. It is agreed in the Federal
Enabling Act that there shall be no Federa-
tion—so far as that Act is concerned—unless
three provinces, of which New South Wales
must be one, concur in the union ; the in-
clusion of the oldest and most populous pro-
vince is set down as an essential. The other
provinces Vent on with the movement be-
cause the adhesion of New South Wales,
under the provision for a minimum affirma-
tive vote of 50,000, seemed reasonably pro-
bable ; if it hadn’t seemed reasonably pro-
bable, they would doubtless have saved the
time and money and worry expended upon
the matter. Therefore, when New South
Wales goes back on the agreement under
which the whole business has been conducted,
and puts a huge additional obstacle in the way,
it is committing a gross and shameful breach
of faith. If any other province had done this
it would have mattered comparatively little ;
the proviso that New South Wales must be
in the Federation, if there is to be any
Federation at all, makes it matter a great
deal in the present case. The parochial
politicians who constituted the majority were
not only doing their little best to make
Federation impossible as regards their own
province, but as regards the other provinces
also.
♦ ♦ * *
In Victoria it is provided that 50,000
affirmative votes are required before the
Constitution is accepted, and, allowing for
the difference in population, this is equal to
a very little over 55,000 in New South
Wales. Tasmania fixed 6000—equal to 48,600
in New South Wales. Westralia also speci-
fied a minimum of 6000—equivalent to
55,000 in the mother province. South Aus-
tralia fixed no minimum at all; in that pro-
vince, if only three votes are given—two in
favor of Federation and one against—the
Federal Constitution will be accepted. But
in all the provinces which enacted a' mini-
mum an effort was distinctly made to keep
in line with the New South Wales minimum
of 50,000, and the mother province has
gratuitously departed from the common
agreement, and deliberately laid itself out to
give provincialism a longer start in New
South Wales than it has anywhere else.
* * • •
The one argument assigned in favor of the
change is that “for Federation to be a suc-
cess it must at least represent a reasonable
number of the people. ” It is somewhat late
in the day to decide that 50,000 is not a
“reasonable number.” Also, the whole idea
is one-sided and absurd. There will be two
issues placed before the people—Federation
or continued Provincialism. By the original
proposal, if 49,999 people voted for Federa-
tion and one for Provincialism that one would
have his way, and the 49,999 would count for
nothing. Also, if 49,999 voted for Federa-
tion and nobody voted for Provincialism, the
49,999 would lose and the nobody would win.
Yet hardly one politician was straight and
honest enough to say that the two ideas
should start fair, instead of the parochial one
getting 50,000 start—now increased to a
start of 80,000, and that it was quite as
essential to show that Provincialism “ repre-
sented a reasonable number of the people.”
If the present generation of Cornstalks had
ever deliberately accepted and enacted Pro-
vincialism by a majority, then there might be
some reason in assuming that they want Pro-
vincialism until they give definite proof to the
contrary. But they did nothing of the kind.
Provincialism came to them without any
acceptation on their part, and when the
Federal Constitution is put to the vote it
will be the first occasion on which they have
had a chance to say whether they want
Provincialism or not. Therefore the two
questions should start from scratch, and
there should be no minimum at all. If out
of 270,000 voters only ten vote for Federa-
tion and only nine against it, Federation
should be carried ; there is no more reason
to assume that the 269,981 electors who
don’t vote are in favor of one idea than of
the other, as they never formally accepted
either idea before. It would be quite as
logical to suggest that Federation shall be
carried unless 80,000 electors vote against it,
as to put the matter the other way about.
It is a noteworthy fact that not one of the
advocates of the 80,000 minimum, or any
other minimum, proposed applying the same
principle to local politics. Levien, of
Quirindi, who asked that there should be no
Federation unless more than half the total
number of electors voted “yea,” holds his
own seat by the votes of one-third of the
electors only. Neild, who demanded a mini-
mum of over one-third the total number of
electors, holds his by the votes of just about
one-sixth of his own constituents. To elect a
politician it is sufficient if two electors vote
for him and only one against; to pass a new
law, however momentous and however little
the people have been previously consulted
about it, it is only necessary, so far as the
Assembly is concerned, that 11 members vote
consistently for it with only nine against, so
that their joint numbers constitute a quorum
of 20. Consequently, the minimum which
the House applies to itself is only one-third
as large as the one which it declares to be
essential in the case of the Federal refer-
endum. And the members who declare that
a minimum of 80,000 affirmative votes is
essential to the acceptance of a Federal Con-
stitution reckon that there is no need for any
minimum at all when they are out in quest
of the loaves and fishes at a general election.
* * * •
The Bulletin believes that the votes in
favor of accepting the Federal Constitution
will enormously outnumber those cast for
Provincialism. At the same time, it does
not believe that there will be 80,000 affirma-
tive votes. And it is fully convinced that
Lyne and See and Crick voted for the
second reading of the Bill, and that
Reid and Brunker, while not openly
supporting it, didn’t throw the Govern-
ment influence into the scale against it,
because they believed just as The Bulletin
believes, and because they want to see Fede-
ration go down without taking the responsi-
bility of openly opposing it. At the election
of Federal delegates, the total number of
voters was about 144,000. On that occasion,
the Cardinal’s candidature roused up the
Holy Roman vote, and the Orange and mis-
cellaneous Protestant vote naturally rose in
opposition, and the sectarian issue dragged
large numbers to the poll who would other-
wise have stayed away. The Freetrade
party rolled up to show its strength by
putting the Government candidates as high on
the poll as possible. The Protectionist party
rallied round its group in order to show by its
vote that it was a great power in the land.
The Labor-party put up ten of its leaders,
and gathered up all its strength to demon-
strate, indirectly, that it was also a great
organisation, and one to be treated with
respect. The personal popularity of the
candidates brought them votes ; their per-
sonal unpopularity brought votes to other
candidates. Then the mere joy of the scrim-
mage counted for a great deal, and the
abstract question of Federation accounted
for the rest. But at the vote for or against
acceptance of the Constitution there will be
no sectarian issue, no rolling up to show
the strength of parties, no Cardinal, no
Orange racket, no Protestant demonstration,
no element of personal popularity or un-
popularity, no canvassing by individual can-
didates, no advertising, very little stumping
of the country, no scrimmage—only the
abstract question of Federation and nothing
more. And even though 99 electors out of
100 are in favor of Federation, the chances of
30 out of every 100 rolling up to vote for it
are very limited indeed. Even at the great
and glorious riot of the general election the
Reid party didn’t muster 80,000 voters all
told !
The latest Financial Joke.
Last week Premier Reid, of New South
Wales, placed his several th financial fiction
before the Assembly, and with a blush less
countenance told the House that he had
“another surplus.” Also, he added, that he
saw his way to have still another surplus at
30th June, 1898. Nobody else that The
Bulletin has heard of ever soared to such
heights of magnificent inaccuracy as Premier
and Treasurer Reid when he holds up his
great, obvious deficiency—which sticks out like
a bowsprit and refuses to be concealed—and
quotes the amount of the year’s revenue and
expenditure, the latter being conspicuously the
larger of the two, and then'tells his hearers
that the surplus is so distinctly visible that
they can’t help seeing it. Reid’s gift of in-
accuracy is beyond definition. It is too childish
and too wildly comic to be called lying, and not
quite childish enough to be called senile decay.
It isn’t finesse ; finesse is mostly inaccuracy,
but uninteresting inaccuracy that is bowled
out on the spot isn’t necessarily finesse. Also,
it would be rough to call a thing deception
which is too weak to really deceive anybody.
• » * *
The present cash aspect of Treasurer George
Reid is as follows : For the year ended 30th
June, 1897, he says that he received £9,291,986,
and that he spent £9,505,408. These amounts
don't wholly agree with the figures published
in the Government Gazette' but G. Reid
states that they are the final amended figures
with everything complete and brought up to
date. They show that for the year he spent
£213,422 more than he received, and Premier
Reid says that they clearly demonstrate a sur-
plus of £120,092. For the next year, ending
30th June, 1898, he estimates that he will re-
ceive £9,331,467, and that his expenditure will
be £9,392,082, which, by the ordinary rules of
subtraction, leaves another deficit of £60,615,
but Premier Reid describes it as a surplus of
£59,477. This isn’t ordinary Ananias ; it is a
great special gift of the kind which enables a
man with burst boots, and an antediluvian hat,
and no collar, and no buttons, and no socks, to
assert positively that he is Rothschild, and
look as if he believed it himself.
* * * •
In view of the fact that Premier Reid has
solemnly claimed to have a surplus at every
financial -statement he has made, the following
statement of his actual receipts and expenditure
is interesting. The figures for 1895 and ’96 are
taken from the Government Statistician’s re-
port (Statistical Register, 1895, pages 350
and 351) ; those for ’97 and the estimate for
1898 are Premier Reid’s figures supplied last
week :
Expenditure. Revenue. Deficit.
1895 (half-year) ... £4,844,299 £4,478,515 £365,784
1896 9,524,343 9,059,607 464,736
1897 9,505,408 9,291,986 213,422
1898 (estimate) ... 9,392,082 9,331,467 60,615
£33,266,132 £32,161,575 £1,104,557
These figures show the revenue and expenditure
for the years covered by the estimates of the
Reid Government, when set forth on the “ cash
basis ” which that same Government intro-
duced. That is to say, they show the actual
income and the actual outgoings for each year,
and the result is a total shortage of £1,104,557.
Further, the figures show that the Reid Govern-
ment has always spent more than it received,
that it always had a deficit while asserting
continually that it invariably had a surplu's.
*
There is another way, however, of showing
the figures—not the real “cash basis” way,
but the alleged “cash basis” way which the
Reid Government adopts. By this process the
net receipts are put on one side of the account
and what the Government calls the “expendi-
ture properly chargeable to the year ” on the
other. This arrangement shows the expendi-
ture fairly enough for the half-year ended
June, 1895, and for 1897 and 1898. For
the year June, 1895 to June, 1896—when the
“cash basis” dodge was introduced, and all
bills owing but unpaid at the beginning of the
year were charged back to the alleged “Dibbs’
deficit,” and all bills owing but unpaid at the
end of the year were carried forward to next
year, and the system of permanently carrying
forward about £600,000 of unpaid accounts to
next year was first introduced—it does not
show the expenditure fairly. In 1895-96 the
Reid Government, by the introduction of its
new “cash basis,” carried forward about
£600,000 of its debts, and a somewhat similar
sum has been permanently carried forward
ever since, which has relieved the visible
expenditure of that amount. Still, even
by its own elaborately “faked” devices,
the Reid finances pan out very badly. The
following are the net revenue and the net ex-
penditure chargeable to each year, as set forth
by Premier Reid’s own system of book-keeping.
The figures for 1895 and 1896 are taken from
Coghlan’s Statistical Register for 1895, page’
328. Those for 1897 and 1898 are Reid’s
figures of last week :
Expenditure. Revenue. Surplus. Deficit.
1895 (J year) £4,709,821 £4,558,569 £151,252
1896 ... 8,899,514 9,237,585 £338,071
1897 ... 9,505,408 9,291,986 213,422
1898 (estimate) 9,392,082 9,331,467 60,615
£32,506,825 £32,419,607 £338,071 £425,289
338,071
Net deficit £87,218
* • * *
These are the returns by the Reid Govern-
ment’s own system of keeping the accounts.
They show three deficits against only one sur-
plus, and they show a net deficit of £87,218.
Yet in the face of this the Freetrade party’s
blatant man of fat claims that he always has a
credit balance ; he alleged a while back that
his name “ would never be associated with a
deficit,” and his servile press follower, the
S.M. Herald, added that he had “faithfully
kept his word,” which is something beyond the
average in the way of unscrupulous support,
even from a very servile organ. Yet even the
very inferior results shown above were only
arrived at by desperate cooking of the accounts.
In 1896 the process of carrying forward all the
unpaid accounts was introduced, and the
reduction, for that year only, of the
expenditure set down as “ properly chargeable
to the year ” from an average of £9,500,000 to
£8,900,000 shows that a saving, on paper, of
about £600,000 was made in this fashion. In
other words, it appears that about £600,000 of
the year’s accounts are now regularlj’ left unpaid
and carried forward, and the year when this
dodge was first devised naturally shows a great
reduction in expenditure. By altering the end
of the financial year from December to June
the Reid Government collected years’
revenue while only paying two years’ contri-
butions towards the deficit (paper saving,
£75,000, as shown in Statistical Register,
page 329), and a little over two years’contribu-
tions to the various other sinking funds (saving
on paper, £33,000, as shown in Register,
page 351). Further, it collected two years’ in-
come-tax in one year ; and it has borrowed
largely and has spent more than half its borrow-
ings on entirely unproductive works, many of*
which were formerly charged to revenue. It
has cut down the road and bridge vote by fully
£IOO,OOO a year below Dibbs’s average (the
average at which it claimed that Dibbs was
starving the road and bridge service) and made
good the difference mostly with loan money.
And with all this it has had four big deficits on
the cash basis system, and three deficits (in
years) on its own system.
* * * *
In addition to the usual blank assertion that
his glaring deficit is a surplus, there is not
much in Premier Reid’s latest financial state-
ment. Among other things, he says that, com-
paring the Customs revenue for the year ended
30th June, 1895, with that for the year ended
30th June, 1897, he has “ relieved the taxpayer
of an annual charge of £828,000 ” a year. As
there is no return in the Government statistics
for any year ending June, 1895, but only for an
odd half-year, this statement is difficult to dis-
prove, but if the financial year ending Decern her,
1894, is compared with the one ending June,
1897 y Premier Reid hasn’t relieved the tax-
payer of that amount. He also says that his
expenditure is “£9,500,000 or £1,000,000 less
than in 1892,” when the Dibbs Government was
in power, but, as the net expenditure in 1892 is
set down by Coghlan (Statistical Register
for 1895, page 351) as £9,931,000, instead


of the alleged ,£10,500,000, thiß is evidently
another pleasing fiction. Otherwise the
N.S. W. Financial Statement for 1897 was a
very mild document. There was no “ piece of
cold iron for Protectionists to chew,” and the
alarming state of Victoria under Protection was
only mentioned once. Usually, it crops up at
least two dozen times.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Victorian Election.
The Victorian election gives the Turner Govt,
a majority of more than two to one—a bigger
following than that with which it started busi-
ness three years ago. It is very rarely indeed
that a Ministry holds office for the full term of
Parliament and then gets back by even a small
majority, and still more rarely that it gets back
by a bigger majority than its original one. As
a rule, after three years the public is fairly
tired of any Ministry, and is willing to try
almost anybody else for a change. In the pre-
sent case the Opposition only scored one success
worth mentioning. Of the fifteen new members
only one is a declared Tory—the unspeakable
Duncan Gillies, who got in for Toorak by an
immense majority. Probably Duncan will be
of more value to the Tory party than a dozen
rank-and-file men ; the Stomach crowd wants a
leader to replace the doleful Scotch MTntyre
and the lamentable Godfrey Carter. And,
though Gillies can’t construct a new Bill, or a
new idea, or a budget, though he is guiltless
of compound addition, and he never had
a policy in his life, except a policy of
drift, still, as an Opposition leader he is a
man of some value—or at least he used
to be. The Labor-party loses Prendergast
and Barrett out of its 13 members ; apparently
the former’s declaration of gory war against
the Turner Government led to his undoing.
Old Graham Berry lost his seat for East
Bourke by a bare 185 votes ; in the scramble of
six men after two seats the veteran was left by
a pair of younger and more active Liberals.
Francis Longmore, another democratic veteran
who deserved better luck, was ejected at Dan-
denong. Baronet O’Loghlen comes back to
Parliament after a long absence. He and
Gillies are the two fossils that returned, and
they were probably elected to make up for the
evanishment of Berry and Longmore, the two
old men that departed. It is by no means
a good exchange for the country ;■ still
O’Loghlen comes back as a professing Liberal,
and the fact that he knocked out the
unspeakable Thomas Bent is much to his
credit. In the interests of political decency,
any country would owe a debt of gratitude to
almost anybody who helped to keep out the
squat, ignorant and splodgy Bent. Max Hirsch
was among the lost, so the great Freetrade re-
vival is again unavoidably postponed-. Pro-
bably the men who could best be spared out of
the new Parliament are Gillies, the beauteous
and vacant Percy Chirnside, Jonas Levien
of smellful Mildura, Murray Smith, Staugh-
ton, the aged punster Zox, and Frank Madden.
The fatted Tories of the eastern suburbs gave
Madden 1812 votes, while the Liberal who was
highest on the list scored 601. And The Bulle-
tin’s valued poet, Randolph Bedford, got but
152 supporters, and manfully lost his deposit
in a good cause.

Freetrade and High Prices.
Melbourne Beacon, the little paper issued by
Max Hirsch in the Freetrade and importing
interest, publishes an article in which it is
strenuously maintained that the price of many
articles of food is higher in N.S. W. under Free-
trade than it was under Protection. The
author of the article is Lonsdale, M.L.A., of
N.S. W., and the Hirsch organ prints his state-
ment with pride as an “ exposure of a portion
of the concocted mendacity dished up ” by
Protectionists. A sample :
Mr. Carroll states that Freetrade has reduced the price
of N.S. W. produce immensely, a statement that is abso-
lutely false. Produce has been brinvina: a higher price in
this colony ever since the duties have been off. The fol-
lowing prices quoted are actual sales, and average
Onions. ' Potatoes. Butter. Bacon. Wheat.
1894 1894 1894 1894 1894
9Jd.
1895 1895 1895 1895 1895
4/9 cwt 2 17/6 12id. 43d. 2 9
1896 1896 1896 1896 1896
8/9 3/6,0 IOJd. 5/d.
1897 1897 1897 1897 1897
9/9 3/14/6 14jd. ski. 4/9
The quotations here given are sufficient to show the abso-
lute untruth of the statement made. They are taken
from a produce-merchant’s books, and are actual sales,
before and since the duties came off. (The duties were
removed on December 31st, 1895.)
This is a truly interesting result. In 1895
N.S.W. was a protected country. At the
beginning of 1896 the duties were almost en-
tirely removed, a lot of revenue was sacriliced,
the country was informed that it would gain
immensely by getting cheaper food, and that a
load had been removed off the back of the con-
sumer, and that the evil system which arti-
ficially increased the price of food and thereby
reduced the purchasing power of the workman’s
shilling had now vanished like a bad dream.
And here is the spectacle of the most infuriated
Freetrade paper in Australia rising to prove
that under Freetrade the workman lias to pay
more for his food than he did under Protection.
Consequently, the burden isn’t removed but in-
creased, and the purchasing power of the
shilling is diminished, and after sacrificing
much revenue in order to give the consumer the
blessing of cheap grub he hasn’t got it after all.
Also, the paper seems actually proud of all
this, and says distinctly that the statement
about produce being cheaper, and the consumer
being relieved from the crushing burden of
high prices, is a lie. Considering how the
Beacon man has labored for years to prove
that Protection is robbery beause of the dread-
ful way it raises prices, this is a very sad fall-
ing-off.
Fog.
The Victorian National Ass party went into
the election waving the usual hazy and inde-
finite banner, and came out badly licked, as the
party with a platform of misty platitudes
generally does. This well-known and widely-
unrespected organisation went down to attack
the Turner Government in its stronghold with
a platform of one item and five dense fogs, as
follows :
(1) Federation and interprovineial Freetrade.
(2) Opposition to dangerous Socialistic and class legis-
lation.
(3) Preservation of the rights of property and labor, so
as to ensure to every man the peaceable enjoyment
of his earnings and savings.
(4) Development of the natural resources of Victoria.
(5) A prudent and progressive public works policy.
(6) To secure the return to Parliament of capable and
honorable men.
The first item didn’t matter ; as the Liberal
party was quite as strongly—perhaps more
strongly—in favor of Federation and interpro-
vincial Freetrade, it furnished no reason why
anybody should give the Ass a single vote.
And the other five items were mere haze. The
old Gillies party is against “dangerous
socialistic and class legislation,” but it didn’t
explain what the term covered, so it asked
the electors to give it a majority and a
general commission to squash anything or
everything. It wanted authority to preserve
the rights of property and labor, but it didn’t
specify what it was going to preserve any more
than it did what it was going to squash. There
are some people who reckon that, so far as the
capitalist is concerned, the right which most
requires preservation is the right to be taxed to
the extent of 20s. in the £ of annual value :
while there are others who hold that the in-
alienable right of labor is to work twice as long
as it now does for half the money. While there
are such wide divergences of opinion the party
which asks any nation to take it on trust be-
cause it is in favor of the rights of capital and
labor, without specifying which rights, is ask-
ing altogether too much. The most crusted
Tory and the most combustible Anarchist are
equally in favor of the rights of capital and
labor, and the latter leaves his little bomb on
the former’s premises because he thinks the Fat
Man has an inalienable right to a bomb—with
a lighted fuse in it. Even the promise to let
every man peaceably enjoy his earnings and
savings didn’t charm the Victorian voter; the
peaceable enjoyment is there now, provided a
man has any savings to enjoy. And the same
eloquent gloom runs right through the pro-
gramme. The development of the province’s
natural resources is good enough, if the public
only understood a little more clearly what re-
sources were alluded to, and how they were to
be developed, and w'ho was to own them when
they were developed ; and Victoria feels inclined
for a “prudent and progressive public works
policy ” if they are the right works, and pro-
gress in the right direction, and the prudence
isn’t of the old Gillies variety. That gentle-
man’s public works progressed downhill in all
directions, and he said he was prudent, anyhow.
Probably the most beautiful item of all was the
Ass’s iron resolve to secure the return of
capable and honorable men, but in the absence
of any information as to whether it considered
Thomas Bent a capable man, the public had no
real information to go upon. With a little more
attention to detail the Ass might have raked
in a decent number of voters, but as it was
he brayed vaguely on unhearing ears and was
lost by a large majority.

The Vice-regal Contract.
The Melbourne jeamses offer a pretty lame
apology for Lord Brassey’s projected jaunt to
England, thus:
During a Governor’s term of office a holiday of six
months is provided for, and it has been the almost in-
variable practice to take this vacation in the middle of
the term. Lord Hopetoun, it is understood, was the first
of the Victorian Governors to take the six months at the
end of the term instead of in the middle.
The Argus, or the Age, or both of them, would
give the public to understand that a consecutive
six months’ leave of absence, whether a Gover-
nor takes frequent holidays or sticks to his post
steadfastly, is part of His Ex.’s contract
with the State that pays his salary—but it
isn’t. A Governor is supposed to work out his
six months in instalments, and Gov. Brassey,
by the way, is only just back in Mel-
bourne from a trip that extended over
several weeks of his holiday contract time.
The overpaid persons who foster snobbery and
“ loyalty ” at Government ’Ouse are, as a rule,
addicted to playing the fool with their official
obligations. The fact that no complaint is
raised by the public merely indicates an almost
unanimous contempt for His Excellency’s
duties. Nobody cares twopence as to whether
he fulfils his official obligations or otherwise,
seeing that such obligations are but the fiddle-
dedee formalities due from a figurehead. Lord
Carington, a weary yawner who accepted an
Australian Governorship in order that his
estates might be “ nursed ” during his absence,
served his full term in N.S.W. without taking
any holiday worth mentioning. He never
dreamed of going ’Ome for six months.
Neither did Sir Henry Loch, his cozitem-
porary in Victoria, attempt to slip out
of the country until he had been on
the vice-regal job for about five years, and when
he made his expedition to London he had press-
ing business in hand. Part of this business was
to procure land-boom knighthoods for Matthew
Davies and Bingy Benjamin. His successor,
Lord Hopetoun, was, in truth, “ the first of
the Governors to take the six months at the
end of the term ” —but it was not “ instead of
in the middle. ” Hopetoun exhausted his proper
holiday-allowance when he got away to Maori-
land at the period of the Melbourne banks’
crisis, but that was not until late in his guber-
natorial career. As for Hopetoun’s escape to
England on full-pay six months before his
term of office had expired, and his arrangement
to leave Chief Justice Madden to run the
show on half-pay, it was an astonishingly
“ cool ” bit of business, which would
probably have excited nasty public comments
had its true inwardness been understood by the
people previous to the getting away of the
smooth-faced potentate. Australia submits to
a good deal of imposition from its vice-regal
visitors, and although one Governor is all the
same as another, and it really doesn’t
matter whether Brassey stays on the contin-
ent or lives up in a balloon, or goes cruising
round the coast for evermore, The Bulletin
may as well protest against grovelling misstate-
ments of the “Brassey holiday” case in “ re-
spectable ” daily newspapers. Also, this is an
opportune moment for suggesting that the
people who pay Governors’ salaries might as
well be favored with full and particular details
of a Governor’s agreement with the “ firm ” he
is supposed to work for.
At a recent meeting of N.S.W. branch of the
China Inland Mission it was stated that seven
of the women present had “devoted” them-
selves as additions to the 720 representatives
which the Mission already possesses in China.
In view of the strengthening opinion that the
West, as a whole, will some day have to “ deal
with ” the East, as a whole—either in the way
of assimilation and absorption, or of deadly
antagonism—it is hard to say how far the path
to the more peaceful determination of the issue
may not have been gradually prepared by the
extraordinary craze of the white woman for
dealings of one kind or another with the yellow
man. Whatever the psychic or physiologic
basis of the infatuation, there is very little
doubt that the proclivities of Western
woman would be no very serious obstacle
to the settlement of the great hemis-
pheric difficulty by simple hybridisation.
Neither need the imagination be very vio-
lently stretched to the conception of a com-
posite Old - world - race, derived from the
practical elimination of Western man and the
appropriation of his women by the overwhelm-
ing swarms of Oriental masculinity. The
partial occidentalisation of the East may yet—
through the application of Western scientific
enlightenment to the military uses of the incal-
culable hordes of the Orient—bring about the
subjugation and degradation of the West itself.
China, India and Japan total already
800,000,000! and the degree to which this
enormous figure dwarfs, the European popula-
tion-tally is one which, at the respective pre-
sent rate of increase, must continue rapidly to
stretch. “Ex Orients lux!” is a dictum open
to much greater question than “Ex Oriente
finis ! ”
Writes “ Smiff’s Corpse Reviver ” :
In reply to “Smiff’s Corpse’’—The Jap certainly per-
vades the Torres Straits pearl-fishery, but who is to blame
for that ? The white man. True, that a Jap without a
shilling can be taken on as a diver, and get an advance of
perhaps £2O in cash, and tucker, &c., on tick. So can any
other diver, if the employer gets to believe he really
means to stay. A diver never signs on for less than six
months, and sometimes he does so for two years. Divers
are paid “on the lay.” As for advances of wages, every-
body shipped in the fishery gets these.
The “ Corpse ” speaks of white men who “ hare built
slips and provided the necessary gear for both repairing
and building boats, but the local white shippers have
starved them out, with the result that the beloved
brownies now own the slips and do the repairing.” Too
true that the Japs do all the ship-carpentry on Thursday
Island. Let their champion, storekeeper Doyle, explain,
as he did to the Commission : “ I went to a white, who
charged me £3O for doing up a boat. She went to sea
and came back leaky. Another white charged me £l2,
and she again returned leaky. I was then forced to em-
ploy the Japanese, and from that day to this I have not
employed a white boat-builder. Everyone could tell you
the same tale. I bought a slip here for two white
carpenters; I advanced the money to prevent it going
into the hands of the Japanese ; they could not work it,
and it is now in the hands of the Japanese. The white
men that we have had here leave their work because it is
uncongenial.”
Otherwise, “Smiff’s Corpse” is pretty correct. The
Jap is throttling Thursday Island. He has got to go.
But there is a big danger “on the top-side” to which
most people, in their wish to see whites employed in the
pearl-fisheries, are blind. I could quote you a score of
replies given to the Pearl Fishery Commission to show
that, when Japs are hunted off, white men won’t take
their places, but they will be succeeded by Manilla men,
Malays, Rotumah and other Polynesians. So that the
man who says “ Pack off the Japs ! ” is not therefore in
favor of employing white labor.
An extraordinary instance of how extremes meet is
found in the circumstance that the biggest monopoly in
Torres Straits is extra anti-Jap. Why ? And why isn’t
it extra-anti-all-round alien ? Because the Jap. becomes
an employer, and the other aliens don’t—to any greatex-
tent. So the monopoly is howling to shunt the Japs.,
and, as fast as it can, is itself putting on Malavs and
Manilla men ! The devil take the difference! Further
(and please let the Q. Pearl-shell Commissioners know
this, for nobody has yet told them), this monopoly is
actually agitating for the Govt, to buy out all the Jap.
boats and to sell them again to whites. It sounds liberal,
doesn’t it? It is liberal—for the monopoly. When
that little game was over, it would be found
that the monopoly owned almost every boat in
the Straits; because it could pay spot-cash', and higher
spot-cash, than anybody else, 'it is about a toss-up
whether such monopoly would be much less nauseous
than the present Jap blend.
I believe and hope that the Pearl-shell Commission
intends to recommend Govt, to license no more Japs as
boat-owners, and to make boat-renting to Japs illegal. Be
that same boat-renting : The Japs are getting very ’cute
in their dealings. They band in syndicates. They go
separately to storekeepers and enter into an agreement to
rent, with a purchasing clause, a boat apiece. The price
is (say) £6OO. They pay down (say) £lO on each boat.
They have got to pay for these boats outright with
shell. Do they want all these boats ? Not much. They
take them out to the pearling-grounds and test them.
That boat which is found to be the best sailer, the most
perfect, the best fitted, &c., is selected. All the best of
the shell that the other boats get is put on board of her.
She is mighty lucky in her sea-harvests, and is paid for in
a twinkling, so to speak. The other boats never seem to
get any decent shell at all (!!), their rentees, or prospec-
tive owners, get as far into debt as the boat-owner will
let them, and then forfeit. After which, they join the
good boat, of which they are part-owners. That is how
Japs acquire real good boats, and that is why they return
numbers of only middling boats back to the storekeepers.
Certainly, down with the Japs ! But keep your eyes on
the big white monopoly as well.
Bits from an election speech by Godfrey
Carter, M. L.A., the financial failure of the
Patterson Government: “It was individual
effort that had done everything for the progress
of the world. The State had done nothing.
Over-legislation was the curse of modern times.
Socialists were bandits. . . . The Mines
Bill, the Companies Bill, and the Credit Foncier
Bill were practically the measures of the late
(Patterson-Carter) Government. . . The
Government should abstain from wild-cat
legislation and promote the development of the
natural resources of the country.” So poor,
muddled, blatant Carter holds : (1) That it is
only private enterprise that is wanted, and
State interference is a curse ; (2) that the State
should interfere all the same to “ develop
the natural resources of the country ; ”
(3) that the Turner Government goes in
for objectionable wild-cat legislation ; (4) that
all the important measures of the Turner
Government were really the wild-cat ideas of
Carter and Patterson, which Turner stole.
Then Carter demanded that the Government
should engage experts to supervise and help
along the brandy industry—instead of leaving
it to private enterprise, and the plural property
vote of the city returned him by a large
majority. Almost his last audible words as the
brick and mortar suffrage rose to roar enthusi-
astic approval were that in his political capacity
he would “ support natural industries in-
stead of leaving them to be supported by pri-
vate enterprise—and that he would vote for a
Bill which provides for State interference with
and organisation of charities, and that he be-
lieved in a Government which would “ develops
the natural resources of the country.” The
Tory shriek for having the natural resources of
the country developed is about the haziest plati-
tude in Victorian politics.
There is prima facie a strong similarity
between the cases of De Clouet, recently found
guilty at Bathurst of attempted wife-murder,
and of Nixon, convicted a fortnight ago at
Adelaide of knifing his wife and her alleged
paramour. The difference on the side of mercy,
however, is, for once in a way, in favor of
Botany Bay ; for N.S. W. Chief-Justice Darley
—though compelled by the jury’s finding
formally to “record” the death - sen-
tence against De Clouet took it upon
himself not to “ pronounce ” it, and
greatly commiserated the prisoner. From
further particulars to hand, however, there
appears to have been a melodramatic flavor
about Nixon’s w r hole conduct which renders
less apparently harsh and uncalled-for the
Judge’s contemptuous reference to “acting.”
“ The making of eyes at her husband by Mrs.
Nixon, in court,” says an Adelaide paper,
“ was on a par with the blowing of kisses to
his wife by the prisoner from the dock.
Reflective people,” it is added, “can come
to no other conclusion than that Nixon
meant to murder his wife and her lover.”
According to a S.A. correspondent of The
Bulletin, however, the great bulk of the evi-
dence made powerfully for the truth of Nixon’s
assertion that it was only when Addison was
throttling him against the door and the wife
battering his head with an umbrella-handle that
he bethought him of the knife as a desperate
resort to save his life. And, as Judge Boucaut
must either have disbelieved this story or be-
lieved it, The Bulletin fails to see the com-
mon-sense or common justice of letting Nixon
off—upon the former assumption—with 18
months gaol, or of giving him—upon the latter
supposition—any punishment whatever.
Chief- Justice Darley’s way out of a difficulty
in the De Clouet capital case at Bathurst w-as
an unequivocal improvement on the method
adopted by Justice G. B. Simpson in the
juvenile capital case at Maitland. Judge
Simpson pronounced the death sentence, pre-
facing the ceremony with an assurance that it
would not be carried out, and supplementing it
with a communication to the Cabinet urging
a commutation. The Chief declined to pro-
nounce the death-sentence, satisfying legal con-
ditions by simply recording it. The latter
course has ever3'thing to recommend' it,
and, until the body of the law is al-
tered, should serve as an open door to
Australian Judges generally. The method
is well-known in English criminal procedure.
The writer was present in a Liverpool (England)
court many years ago, when the Judge presid-
ing at a case where a verdict of guilty was re-
turned against a wife for slaying her husband
in peculiarly aggravating circumstances, flatly
refused to pronounce sentence of death. “I
must record your finding,” he said to the jury,
“ and shall send your verdict to the Home Sec-
retary, but I’ll pronounce no sentence.” Justice
Simpson’s method, in this instance, is a curious
mixture of the grotesque and the satirical.
The English Engineers, still fighting for an
Eight-hour Day, have, of course, the hostility
of London Times. The engineers, in a mani-
festo, point out that the Times in ’7l argued
strongly in favor of a reduction of hours to
nine, although Sir W. (now Lord) Armstrong
then prophesied that the result would be to
smash up the iron-manufacturing industry in
Great Britain. And they remark that so far
has the limitation been from having this result,
that last year Armstrong’s firm’s profits reached
.£358,000. The cable messages suggest friction
between the striking engineers and what
are referred to as the Federated Trades.
This friction is four months old, and
originated in the old dispute—ballot or no
ballot. The engineers believed that the
employers intended to defeat them in sections,
so ordered an immediate knock-off; while the
boilermakers, steam-engine makers, and other
societies wanted a general ballot. The
engineers, who are not in the federation, had
already issued strike notices. Whereupon hard
words, charges against the boilermakers of
“ prostrating themselves before the Employers’
Federation,” and other things apparently not
s oon forgotten.
When the Levien-Neild bill for making Fed-
eration impossible in N.S.W. was before the
Assembly, Premier Reid’s various attitudes
were as follows : (l) He was in favor of Federa-
tion ; (2) he proposed raising the minimum
affirmative vote required before the Federal
Constitution could be accepted from 50,000 to
80,000; (3) he said that lie would vote against
the bill on its second reading, but if it passed
the second reading he would accept it; (4) he
did vote against it at the second reading, but
thereafter he voted for it ; (5) though he
voted against the second reading he didn’t
speak one single word against the bill, or
ask anyone else to vote against it, or
point out that it was a gross breach of faith
with the other provinces—in fact, he went
out of his way to assure the Assembly that his
second-reading vote was only a matter of form,
and that he was prepared to turn his coat
immediately afterwards. And he cheerfully
turned his coat accordingly. It was N.S.W.
which started both Federal movements, and in
that same province Parkes deliberately wrecked
the first one and Reid is setting out to deli-
berately wreck the second. Parkes and Reid
—the Iscariots of Federation !
Alfred Deakin’s position as guide, philo-
sopher and friend to the Vic. democracy is further
jeopardised by the malicious conduct of the
Argus which, just before the elections, took him
to its withered breast, and called him. blessed.
Whilst the Argus was spittinsr venom at Alfred,
the radicals could feel that there was hope for
him, but Argus blessings fall like bricks.

The revival of “ Romany Rye ” at Sydney
Royal brought a well-deserved increase of busi-
ness to the Rignold enterprise. There are many
food points about Sims’s veteran drama, and The
lulletin hereby leads that esteemed author
round the corner in the spirit, and shouts for him,
in recognition of the fact that his drama is wholly
unlike many others with which this pajier has the
misfortune to be acquainted. There are certain
improbaLilities in it, no doubt. Few hardened
bill-discounters, in a large way of swindle, would
wade in blood, and plots, and entrails, and mid-
night assassination, as Laurence Dunbar does,
merely to recover -£5OOO, seeing that they could
defraud someone else of twice that amount in the
time. Also, there is one objection, which applies
almost equally to every other melodrama: no
villain in real life could get so many shots at the
hero at short range, and miss him every time ;
and the incriminating document couldn't wade
through so many hair-breadth escapes, and be
burnt, and drowned, and thrown overboard, and
subjected to storm, and lightning, and dog-bite,
and yet come up fresh and legible in the last act.
Still, “ Romany Rye ” hangs together better than
most of the blood-and-splash dramas of later origin.
It is an interesting story which doesn't burst into
absolute impossibility anywhere. The dialogue is
above the average, and the ignorant miscreant
with the black-eye is really humorous in places.
There is no separate laborious funny man ; Frank
Harcourt, the miscreant above-mentioned, attends
to that business in the intervals of stoush, and
does it well, with some casual assistance from
Scragger. Probably the weakest point in the
whole business is the conspiracy manufactured by
the haughty ruffian, whose jewellery and pale
boiled shirt shimmer in the moonlight on the lawn
of his stolen mansion ; The Bulletin reckons it
could fix up a better scheme of murder any day
without gnashing its teeth near so much. Stili,
the general verdict upon “Romany Rye” is dis-
tinctly favourable.
* * *. •
Thirty-six people, besides loafers, hangers-
oi, flunkeys, supernumeraries, dead-beats and
d igs, help to illustrate Sims’s drama, which unfolds
itself in 17 scenes, some of' them very artistic,
and one shower of real, undeniable rain.
Diver makes a fine villain in his quieter
moods, though at a crisis he overstrains
the part so ewhat, and speaks it in
italics. Rignold plugs through a very heavy part
in good style, and among the other people Frank
Hawthorne, Hazlitt, Sterling Whyte, and par-
ticularly Harcourt, all do their duty with pleasing
results. Miss Lilian Wheeler, a pathetic heroine
with a large, brown dog, speaks her artless little
part rather convincingly, and keeps her simple
frock unspotted from the world ; likewise Fanny
Reynolds, as a ’ag with an eerie laugh in a cellar,
is highly praiseworthy. Probably this lady with
her dreadful shriek of mirth in the cellar is the
most interesting single figure in the show. She
seems to make a humble but honest crust by gib-
bering underground, and when she lets off her
ghostly cackle at Diver and then suggests that he
should give her £5O. his refusal is a matter of sur-
prise—her cry of woe seems fully worth the money.
The rest of the co., in its vastness and the impos-
sibility of sorting it out, suggests Israel coming
out of Egypt with a black eye and a short pipe in
its mouth, and hoarsely demanding gin in the
wilderness. In a drama of 30 characters it
generally happens that 32 of them are afflicted
with an incredible thirst, and lift their voices in a
bitter cry for gin.
#* * *
The Firm has compiled a rati. strong
company for its now dramatic venture at Her
Majesty’s. It includes the evergreen Titheradge,
daughter Lily (whose fitful appearances in the
latter days of the Brough co. made the public
desire to see more of her), Howard Vernon,
Sweeney, Miss Julia Merton, and a few dimmer
lights, and Caleb Porter, who so regally com-
busted the early Christian a few weeks back, will
stage-manage. Under these conditions the “Two
Little Vagabonds ” should do well, unless the
drama is wholly beyond repair.
** * *
That loud military crash, “At Duty’s
Call,” will end its confused Egyptian career at the
Lyceum on Friday night. Its successor is “ Fallen
Among Thieves,” a drama by Frank Harvey, and
the usual cinematographe will be thrown in, as
before.
** * *
On and after Saturday Harry Rickards
will have three theatres going in Sydney simul-
taneously, which is probably a record. The
“Bunch of Keys ” is doing its last nights at the
Palace, and on Saturday next (October 30) “ Binks
the Photographer ” will make his first appearance
in Australia. At the Tivoli the “loose-legged
comedian ’ has returned to shake his gutta-percha
extremity at the public for a short period along
with some newly-imported comedians, and the
Avolos are doing another season. In addition to
all this, Rickards opens the Criterion on Saturday
with an enlarged and improved edition of his old
Palace co. The programme includes the Bio-
graph, the rotund and silver voiced Phil. New-
bury, Miss Fanny Wentworth—the only really
clever lady humorist this paper has met with up
to date —and a lot of costly and valuable etcetera.
“A Cruel Wrong/’ which challenges com-
parison with the Royal show and is just about as
serio isly humorous, continues to wag its tale of
varied interest at the Alexandra, and the small
girl who didn’t find a watery grave in the prologue
gets kidnapped by immortal Augustus Glover
fifteen times every evening. Meanwhile, the sturdy
Alexandra management is preparing for the vast
production of “ A Fatal Tune.” The melody that
leaves funerals in its track will be heard for the
first time on Saturday next.
# * * *
“A Royal Divorce,” at Melbourne Prin-
cess’s, is leading a reckless, lucrative life upon
four tableaux and costumes to match. The crowd
rolls up, and weeps, in some cases, over the mental
sufferings of a Josephine who seems very like a
gratuitous insult to history. Also, the multitude
laments the loneliness of Julius Knight as he
stands, in the traditional attitude of an even
greater hero than himself, gazing at a painted
ocean with his back exposed to view. A change
in the cast of Napoleonic officers has rather
strengthened the acting portion of the business,
but it isn’t anybody’s acting that makes “ A
Royal Divorce” go. From a captious critic’s
point of view, the performance is altogether too
ro ugh and noisy.
* * * *
A “Dramatic Medley” ventured by the
Misses Sisley, at Melb. Bijou, last Saturday, was
a young and artless proceeding. Youth and inex-
perience are faults that nice girls grow out of,
alas ! and amateur stage-management yields to
the hand of time. Miss Barbara Sisley and Miss
B.’s sister Olive are certainly clever. They will,
moreover, be in a fair way to develop into
character actresses as long as they remember that
patience is a virtue, and will take the trouble to
bear several other valuable precepts in mind.
* * * *
The Bulletin would fain say something
new concerning the Rickards show at the Opera
House, but the only observation to hand at the
present moment is “ crowded as usual.” The bill
is a full-size,l holiday list of attractions, and many
patrons of the establishm mt need to loose their
hold up m the btek of the dress-circle if they wish
to join in the frequent bursts of applause.

At the Bijou, next Saturday, an E.lucated-
Horse Entertainment will appeal to the sentiment
of these racing cirnival times. Also, an Axe-
men’s Contest is beginning to fall due at the Ex-
hibition, and a revival of “Fun on the Bristol”
has already fallen due at St. George’s Hall.
** * *
“Clean or unclean,” writes Rose de
Boheme to The Bulletin from Frisco, “this
city will always attract strangers if only from the
cheapness of its living. You can dine here
cheaper, probably, than in any other city of the
world. You can also go to the play cheaper. For
weeks past we have had a very presentable opera
troupe, with a prima-donna of some European
repute, more than fairly supported ; orchestra,
admirable; Wagner, Verdi, Ponchelli, etc., in-
terpreted at least as well as by the opera-troupes
that have visited Sydney of late years. Yet, for
the best places in this entertainment you pay the
astonishing sum of two shillings, and if your
purse be light you can secure as good a seat as any
music-lover requires for one shilling. How it
pays is a mystery.

At Poverty Point.
Growler : “ An imported actor who saw the last night
of the ‘ French Maid 1 at Sydney was deli chted with the
superb chorus. In Australia there is little scope for
native singers : they must sro into the chorus or stay idle.
In England as soon as a girl can pipe her little lay in tune
she aims at playing parts. This country has been Hooded
with promoted English chorus girls as stars, ami managers
seem to prefer paying an inferior imported article rive
times the salary he could get one of his own song-birds
for. Onc.e in the chorus always in the chorus seems to lie
the motto here ; if Melba had started in that line she
would probably have been there yet..”
A gushing Australian chorus-girl, who knows not the
gulf which divides the common herd from principals at
’Ome, rushed up to a newly-arrived “ star,” and effusively
bade her welcome to Australia on behalf of the chorus.
The look of frozen surprise with which the outburst was
greeted by the haughty star was what one might expect
from Milord the Governor, were he welcomed to Australia
by one of the dishwashers on behalf of the cooks at Uuv-
nientouse.
Daring the final performance of the “ French Maid,” at
Sydney Majesty’s, Chas. B u keley, who was playing the
hotelkeeper, 4elt serious symptom-) of illness, and could
barely keep on his feet, but his pluck carried him
through the scene. Just as he left the stage, he fell un-
conscious. He received every care, but it will he some
time before he is fit for work.

James Garraway : “ Gloating over ‘A Royal Divorce,’
I yet cannot help reeling how immeasurably inferior it is
to my great national patriotic blank verse, ‘Drama of
(jueen Victoria.’ Imagine Miss Ferrar as the fresh, pal-
pitating young Victoria, compared with the rococo
Josephine, and Mr. Knight as the ardent, impulsive love-
making. not to he resisted I‘riuoe Albert, compared with
the jejune Napoleon. Why, the amphitheatre would
come down in a Niagara cataract of loyalty, drowning
the stage. Then my scene of the procession through the
streets of London, with Victoria on a real white horse
like Rignold’s. The coronation! The marriage ! A
genial sketch of Premier Melbourne for local color, as it
were. The Duchess of Kent, Wellington, and all the rest,
sticking out of the canvas, not forgetting the under-
miner, the sapper, sly Greville, the St. Simon of the era,
laying his Guy Fawkes barrels.”
