S.S. : “ According to the impressions given by ‘ A
Royal Divorce,’ Napoleons were intended mainly for
decorative work ; their mission was to cast a glamor over
the army and light up the field of battle. The extreme
difficulty of keeping their terriflo bats on and finding
their several limbs amongst the gorgeous draperies and
gilt fixings with which they were hung about, must have
rendered them comparatively helpless in the teeth of the
enemy. One or two of Nap’s generals at the Princess’s
are clattering, glittering visions, dressed within an ace of
their lives, and feathered with reckless profusion, and in
each case the poor actor hidden away under this barbaric
splendor is a sweltering, distracted martyr manifestly
sorry he was ever born, and hopelessly' incapable of acting
up to his external magnificence. Few actors could carry
off a uniform like that of Murat, whose mantle has fallen
upon Dick Stewart, and D.S. is slowly smothering
under it.”
“Two Little Vagabonds,” with Titheradge and daughtet

T.D. : “Seems to me that the world's singers regard
Australia as a recuperating-ground for damaged vocal
apparatus. Even the posters, by small-typing Barton
M Guokiu, setm to admit that he is at least a semi-bar-
bered Sampson. In its lower register, the tenor's
voice is frayed, but his reach-up for a top-note is an
object-lesson in vocalisation. All very well to say that
M Guckiu has only done 20 years’ work to Lloyd's 30,
but one was in nightly opera, and the other in oratorio,
pleasantly diversified by light ballad singing. Stephen
Adams kills nobody-save an occasional listener. Amy
Sherwin, also, is obviously here for a rest.”
Melb. :“ I suppose it is coming to this. The audience
will perform while the actors and actresses, like Christy
Minstrels, sit in a row on the stage and listen. On Thorn-
ton’s last night, at Melb. Bijou, the amphitheatre was
taken by some Mutual Admiration Club. They adver-
tised a programme of eight numbers, to which the ‘ Pri-
vate Secretary ’ was a side-show. Indeed, while the cur-

Church or stage. The sale of the five-shilling tickets
for the opening ceremony of Melb. St. Patrick’s Cathedral
(during race-time) has been so rapid that prices have
already run up to half-a-sov. for reserved seats. Arch-
bishop Carr hopes to get the huge place out of debt by a
colossal Cup-time collection.
Miss Elsie Lander, a young English actress, has taken
Brisbane Royal for three months, and proposes to stage a
series of dramatic shockers by Rhys Byrne. One piece
contains a broken-bri Igescene, something like that in the
“ Prairie King”—only without the horse on which King
Heille.v and his successor, Piimmer, used to fall into the
tempestuous tank. Miss Lander’s boss horror deals with
a Japanese invasion of Australia Jap man-of-war at
anchor, Australian sub-marine torpedo-boat approaches,
crash, torpedo-boat comes triumphantly to surface, man-
of-war sinks, and the sea full of drowned Japs, waiting to
be inquested. Then wild applause, and the singing of
local patriotic melody.

The return of Gillies and Murray Smith
accounts wholly for the defeat of Robert Harper,
at Vic. general election. Had the city crowd not
been so busy at Toorak and Hawthorn 30 more
votes at Preston would have saved them the seat.

tion with Mr. Gillies or anyone else ; l much pre-
fer to remain a private member.” Alfred Deakin
on the hustings. They are both in the House
again, but another Gillies-Deakin coalition is an
utter impossibility in these latter days.

Thomas A. Edison, the great inventor, has
just produced the “ HOME ” phonograph, which,
as the name implies, is intended to supply the de-
mand for an instrument-for private use having all
the vital points of the expensive exhibition phono-
graph combined witli a low price. This new
“ talking machine,” costing only £l2, will soon be
found in every house in the country. The manager
of the Edison Electric Parlor (102 Pitt-st., Sydney)
reports a strong demand.*
Vic. : “ Poor Graham Berry ! What a
lesson to a young man picking a career—Berry,
Parkes and the rest! This time last year Berry
was given to understand he. had no show for the
Speakership, but, just before this election, the
Govt, let him know he would be right, if he could
only get in. Well, he hasn’t. The whole province
elected him to the Federal Convention, and that
not the last of ten. A twopenny-halfpenny con-
stituency now knocks him out. Already there is
talk of what can be done for him. The Conserva-
tives would be prepared to give him a vote, but
the Trades Hall, in opposing it, would be a
nucleus to the Liberals. S.yme, in the Age,
though never Berry’s friend, cannot refrain from
pointing out his distinguished and unparalleled
services to Liberalism. He is again the ‘Run
Down Dingo’of a contemptuous Age reference,
thirty years ago.”

COULMN
The financial statement issued by Premier Reid
(ras evi n worse than the had state of affairs foreshadowed
in the a counts published three months ago by Sunday
Brunker. The receipts for the year ended 3(lt.h June,
1897, were £9,291,986, and the expenditure £9,51)5,408;
and all Premier Reid’s eloquent assurances that he has a
surplus can t conceal the fact that these figures leave a
shortage of £213,422. It takes a lot of brass to
claim a surplus under the circumstances, but Premier
Reid has the metal right enough—only the charm has
gone out of the old tale somehow. For next year he pro-
poses to raise abo it £85,000 new taxation bv increasing
the probate duties, and be hopes to collect, the great mass
of land-tax arrears. It is expected that the total revenue
will be £40,000 more than last year’s receipts, and it is
proposed to cut do vn expenditure by £113.000. When all
this is done Premier Reid expects that the revenue will be
£9.331,467and theexpenditure £9.392,082 -leavinganother
deficit of £60,615. Whether it will be possible to cut down
theexpenditure as propos d is doubtful, and if it is done it
can only be by charging still more of the ordinary outlay
to loans. Last year’s expenditure got. ahead of the esti-
mates by £lO6 00). and this year's estimates are cut a few
thousands lower than those of 1896-97. For the first
three months of the year, too, the expenditure, instead
of being less than that for last year, is t 900 D more, while
the revenue, so far, is £166,000 less. Of course there are
still nine month-; in which to straighten things, but up to
date the increased income and diminished outlay which
are promised have neither of them set in.
• * * #
The Maori land surplus for the year ended 31st
March. 1897. vaguely estimated at £125,000, proves to be
£138,728. Next year, ending 31st March, 1898, the Govt,,
anticipates £170,000 surplus. Maoriland's last deficit was
In'B7 —the closing one of a series which covered 'B5-6-7.
Whatever else may be said against its two last Ministries,
and despite one or two shady points about Treas. Ward's
finance, there can be no doubt that the Balianee-Seddon
Governments, and that of Atkinson which preceded them,
have put up the best continuous financial record in Aus-
tralasia. And Maoriland struggles under disadvantages
shared by no other province. It is the only one with
a heavy war-dent: and it is the onlv one where the
native race was strong enough to compel the purchase cf
the soil instead cf allowing the Government, to seize it
gratis—a fact which made an immense difference in the
land-revenue. Yet M.L. is the only province, barring
Westra’ia, with no accumulated deficit, and it is the only
one which builds its railways and other public works in
great part out of revenue.

Treasurer Fysh, of Tasmania, reckons hi«
revenue for the vear 1893 at £852,540, and expenditure at
£790,250; surplus, > 62,290. There is at least one thing
to be said for Fvsh ; he makes ends meet on paper, and
pegs awav continuously at reducing the accumulated
shortage, while bis .three predecessors Burgess, Bird and
John T’e >rv—simrlv sp'd downhill to perdition. As for
the wav Fvsh makes his surpluses, the best that can be
said is that it is a little better than the wav the other
fellows made their deficits, and the ways of both fellows
are worse than those of Premier Reid at. his worst. In
1803 Treasurer Henry spent £174.000 of borrowed money,
£158,000 of it being for works which yielded no dir ct
income whatever, and which should have beer, paid for
cut of revenue, and had £129.000 deficit in addition. In
1895 Treasurer Fysh got through £113,000 of borrowed
money, £llO,OOO of it being for non-productive works
properly chargeable to revenue, and had £13,000 surplus.
And in the interests of political honesty Treasurer Henry
should have been summarilv executed, while something
of a temporary character with boiling oil in it would pro-
bablv be sufficient to suit the case of Treasurer Fysh.

Svdnev Telegraph estimates that, if Premier
Turner carries out his scheme for the conversion of the
Vic. public debt into a uniform 3 per cent, stock of long
currency, he may, hv adding £3 690.000 or £4,000,000 to
the total debt, save about > 295,000 a year in interest. If
so, it is all the more reason why a part, of that saving
should be set aside for the redemption of the debt.. If the
Government was content to take the odd £95,000 a year
for the benefit of the revenue, and let the other £200.000
of the saving go in reduction of the principal of the debt,
the whole liability could he wiped off in about 75 years
wit out laving any extra burden whoever on the tax-
payer. Then Victoria could have a jubile ;of the biggest
kind “all on her own.”

• « «
The Royal Bank of Australia (Melbourne) con-
tinues its remarkable improvement. The following are
the profits shown for the hast seven half-years :
September, *94, ... £2875 September,’96, ... £2044
March,’9s, ... 2136 March, '97, ... 3308
September,’9s, ... 2038 September,’97, 3625
March, ’96, ... 2 >99
The earnings for the half-year just ended (£3625) are
therefore very near double those for the corresponding
half-year of 1896. A dividend of 3 per cent, per annum on
the written-down capital (£150,000) absorbs 12250, and the
remainder (£1375) added to previous balance leaves £2523
to the credit of profit and loss. The Royal looks healthy in
other ways. Note-circulation has expanded during the
half year from £3595 to £5594, a very large increase ; and
deposits (£247,849) are £25,000 more than they were six
months ago. There is a ver3' solid cash-reserve of £BO,OOO,
equal to 27 per cent, of all liabilities to the public, and
the institution appeals to shareholders to send along
more deposits, business having so greatly improved that
it has now profitable employment for more money than is
available.
The Royal, though it went safely through the
1893 panic, has written-off over £170,000 of capital and
reserves as lost, and if it proposes ever to make up this
leeway it has still a long road to travel. The mistake that
was made in the early days was that of paying too large
dividends (they were only 6 per cent., but still they were
larger than circumstances justified), and leaving too small
a margin for reserve. At present the board is doing
wisely in keeping the dividend down to 3 per cent., and
it would do still more wisely if it announced a fixed policy
of carrying a certain proportion of the profits regularly
to a new reserve-fund till the byegone losses are fully
made up. The fact that the sun is beginning to shine
again is the best possiole reason why this should be done
now. A bank with £150,000 capital and no reserve
needs to improve a great deal before it can take itself very
seriously.

Thpre is still no news from Brisbane about the
appearance of that famous report on the Queensland
National Bank, and no information about the Gentleman
Who Is Away. And unless the Labor-party keeps asking
for that report in season and out of season, and becomes

one that there will never be any report at all.
* » * •
A queer game of bluff was recently played by
Callan, M.L.A., and director of the Q.N.B. Callan spoke
in favor of Glassey’s motion for the production of the
Q.N.B. report thusly: “I do not agree in the slightest,
knowing ail the facts and figures, that there is any neces-
sity that the country or bank should be kept waiting for
the examination of anybody, whether in England or any-
where else.” After which profession, Callan voted against
the motion he had just spoken for, and all was peace
again. But the Govt, had a near shave—a very near
shave.

The fact of the officials of a Melbourne bank
treating its own genuine bank-notes as spurious, can be
consolingly placed alongside the recent supercilious
N.S. W. banker's assertion that Sydney shopkeepers are so
stupid that a forged bank-note would pass 70 per cent, of
them.

Dear Bulletin, — The prospectus of Emu Bay
Railway Co., published by you 2nd Oct., and your para-
graph therecn, contain a lot of misrepresentations and
distortions. The Bulletin is condemned out of its own
month, for it has said, with damnable iteration, that no
Govt, should spend borrowed money on any undertaking
which is not reasonably likely to pay interest. [Quite
true. The Bulletin says railways should be built
out of revenue.—Ed. B.] Now, it is not at all cer-
tain that there will be any paying mines at all in
the Rosebery - Dundas district which this line is to
serve. Only one certain big mine has been discovered on
the West Coast so far, the Mt. Lyell, and that has its own
line already. The assumption that the Waratah-Zeehan
line will cost onlj’£4001) a mile is very ridiculous, as the
other two lines on the Coast cost over £BOOO per mile,
and the 2ft. tramway which Govt, is building to Rosebery
will cost well over £2OOO per mile. And the syndicate
has not even asked Parliament for peim ssion to extend
the line from Zeehan to Lyell—and is not likely to get
that permission. The line will prohably pay 5 per cent,
i/all the traffic does not go to Strahan, and the district
is what it is cracked up to be. But there is not the
certainty about the project that would alone justify an
already semi-in sol vent community in borrowing cash
for it. (The Bulletin never proposed borrowing the cash.
—Ed. B.[ And Tas. Government is not so much asleep
as The Bulletin asserts. If the line proves a paving
investment, the people of the province can take it
over at the end of 21 years by paying the cost of con-
struction plus 20 per cent. Another point: if the pro-
posed improvement of Macquarie Harbor Bar comes off,
as it most certainly will, the bulk of the Rosebery and
Colebrook country ores will naturally seek the nearest
port, Strahan, via the Govt.’s Roseberv-Strahan line,
which, as it is now paying nearly 8 per cent, can stand
some reduction in freights. And Emu Bay is not much
of a port either. It is rot to ask th ■■ Tas. Govt, to launch
out into a wild and joyous expenditure of borrowed
money in assisting the Melb. boomers to make a big pile
in exploiting the West Coast. [Quite so. That’s
why The Bulletin didn’t propose expending any bor-
rowed money.—Ed. B.] Undemocratic and pigheaded
as the Braddon crowd are in many respects they
are doing as good work as any Government in Australia
in keeping down expenditure to the lowest possible point,
and resolutely refusing to dispense with the income-tax
because there is a small surplus for 1896.— Julias Swift,
Queenstown, Tas., 7,10 97.

The new warden at Hillgrove, N.S.W., Mr.
Murphy, says it is necessary to take a firm stand in deal-
ing with suspension of labor applications. No doubt, but
surely it is not necessary to put applicants to the expense
of preparing powers-of-attorney at a cost of a guinea
each to make application through an agent. Hitherto
the authority to act as agent issued by the Mines Dept,
has been sufficient. The warden recently ruled otherwise.

Pambnla : “ It is stated that sometime since last
week a bunch of specimen stone was broken off the wall

* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —Against your par. quoting
Prof. Crookes on Inverell diamonds (were they really In-
verell or Bingara ?) pit some facts stubborn things. If
you were here I could show you several parcels of Inverell
diamonds sent, by local residents, both to Amsterdam and
London, all of which have been cut without difficulty, and
the unanimous verdict about them is that though “ they
are somewhat harder than South African diamonds, yet
they are of unexceptional brilliancy, and the quality can-
not be surpassed.” The diamond industry of this mrt, of
N.S.W. promises to be a large and important one in the
near future, and Boggy Camp, where they are finding the
largest quantities, is a very busy centre. I should say
there are about 300 miners at work in the locality. I
have seen a nice parcel of 2430 carats in local 8.N.5.W.,
taken from 101 loads of wash (8770 diamonds), by Or y
and party, and the Inverell Diamond Co. is getting some
splendid yields from its several claims. Many of the
diamonds I saw would turn the scale at 2)4 to 3 carats,
and a good number would weigh a carat, hut the general
run averages about 3J4 stones to the carat. Prof. Crookes
is a long way from here, and where he got his information
I really don’t know ; but I do know that no such informa-
tion as that he speaks about has ever reached this locality,
or the parties immediately interested.—J.B. 11., Inverell.
• * * *
B.H. Junction North is about ready for more
active ore-export; 1500 tons or so of good stuff is broken,
most of it being temporarily stored underground.
* * * *
One effect of the pouring of immense quantities
of water on the burning timbers in the B.H. Prop, has
been to partly fill up Campbell shaft on Block 10, over a
third of a mile away. This is the deepest working on the
lode ; the water rose in it over 150 ft.—to the 850 ft. level.
No work is, however, done at that depth. But at 715 ft.,
in another shaft, a fairly good milling ore has been broken
into; its size, however, has not yet been ascertained.
Notwithstanding that the low price of silver continues, a
further sale of silver zinc “tailings” has been made at a
satisfactory price. The stuff goes to Germany for treat-
ment.
* * * *
There was the almost-annual little labor-trouble
at Broken Hill this year, after Eight-hours’ Day. The
men who had been expected to go into the fire-affected
section of Block 12 in the afternoon failed to turn up, and
were next day promptly “ not wanted ” in a body. Growl-
ing, of course, and some threats—and then the discharged
ones fell back unremeuibered among the crowd of un-
employed.
* * • *
Incendiarism being still suspected in connection
with the Broken Mill mine fire, a reward of ‘ 500 isoffered
(£lOO by Govt, and £4OO by the co.—later accounts
make it £lOOO and a free passage for the informer
to any part of the world); and a public-force detec-
tive from Sydney has been at work in the district
for some weeks. Well enough ; but the men are naturally
wanting to know why similar steps haven't now and again
been taken to get at the reason for the prevalence of
accidents in the same and neighboring mines. This, by
the way, is f he first time a detective has been sent into
the far west of the province, except during strikes.

The N S. W. toy metallurgical works at Clyde,
Sydney, have cost £6OOO, and for the first year’s run only
197 tons of ore, or less than four tons weekly, have been
treated. Neither receipts nor cost are given, hut it is
again explained that the works were not erected to treat
bulk lots, but only experimental parcels not above 10
tons. Extensive works for the treatment of ail classes of
ore having heen put up at Illawarra. the sooner this toy
Govt, establishment is shut up the better.
• • * •
Barrier miners, thrown out by the premature
stoppage of the smelters at the B.H. Prop, through the
fire, have had their thoughts turned to the Waukaringa
goldfield, just over the S.A. border. Marvellous specimen
stones have been shown, and the present inclination is to
think that the field hardly deserves its reputation for ex-
treme patchiness. The opening of the Govt, cyanide
plant at Petersburg, too, helps it a lot.
* * * *
Block 10 put up a record in shipments in the
last week in September, the total quantity sent away
being 3458 tons, made up of 1379 tons concentrates and
2079 tons zinc tailings. The old complaint of want of
trucks on the S.A. railways is, by the way, heard no
longer.
* • • •
Attempts were made at Broken Hill to organise
some recognition of the bravery of the men who made so
effective a fight against the fire upon its outbreak in the
B.H. Prop, and thereby saved the town from a financial
disaster. But the efforts failed dismally; and now the
co. is talking of doing something “on its own." A medal
will most likely be struck and awarded : the idea is that,
apart from the money bonus, this would he the form
most serviceable to working men who may hereafter be
on the hunt for billets.
* • * •
Opinions have always differed as to the nature
of the deposit in the old Burra copper-mine in S.A.—
the property which in 30 years yielded 51,622 tons fine
copper, sold for £4,750,000, of which £827,000 was profit;
this on an original capital of £12,230. While it was being
worked it was supposed to he merely a surface pocket ;
certainlv country was found almost everywhere under the
ore. But some old hands have always insisted that there
was a vein at one point, in the bottom at 70 fathoms, and
that the surface deposit was a “spew” from this. The
theory is now about to be tested by a drill. The mine has
been idle 20 years.
• • • ■
In Committee of Q. Assembly, an amendment
has been carried on the Land Bill, providing that, royal
and other metals shall be reserved to the Crown in any
alienations of the public estate. What’s the use of half,
doing a thing? A Mining on Private Property Act straight
out is the reform needed. Besides, there is a difference
of opinion among lawyers as to whether metals and
minerals are not already reserved to the Crown under ex-
isting Q. laws, together with the British laws that are
held to be in force in Australia.

The Westralian gold-yield for September, accord-
ing to statistics issued bv the Coolgardie Chamber of
Mines, was 78,281 ounces from 53,200 tons of stone —an
average of nearly 20 weights to the lon. The list, repre-
sents returns from more than 300 mines altogether, the
crush in 200 cases, or thereabouts, being less than 100 tons,
and running as low as half a ton. 123 crushes exceeded
100 tons ap’ece, 70 of these going more than an ounce to
the ton, whilst the other 53 went less. Kalgoorlie district
took the bun easily with 14,917 tons for 33,846 ounces.

Westralian gold figures show a progressive im-
provement. calculated to madden early British investors
who rushed hither and thither after the wildest of “cats.”
The output for January to March, ’96, was 64,128 ounces,
and, for the first four months of this vear, it rose to
152,8660 z. May to Sept., ’96. produced 152,866 ounces, and
the same period in ’97 has been good for 298,1750 z. Sep-
tember went 6650 ounces better than August, which was
6000 ounces richer than any previous month. Wherefore
elated Westralian barrackers invite Tim Bulletin to take
back a number of alleged statements which it didn't- state,
ann plead jrnilty to many awful errors that never were
made in these columns. As for the awful truths commit-
ted by this heastl v journal, the aforesaid barrackers for
t»e “Golden West” will magnanimously forgive them on
the understanding that they are to be forgotten.

The W.A. Smelting Co. has been formed in
London, with a capital of < 300,900. Works are to he
erected at Fremantle. George Aarons, formerly manager
of the Londonderry, is the chief founder of the scheme;
W ittenoom, Mines Minister, will be a local director: and
one Dickinson, it. is announced, is about to leave London
to superintend the erection of the machinery. With coal
found in the country, and the great pvrltic mines of Kal-
goorlie at hand, there seems a good chance of these works
succeeding, though much will depend on the working of
mines other than gold—more particularly copper or lead.

Moran, M.P. (W.A.), on the expansive pro-
mises made by Forrest in London to persons, chiefly
boomst.ers “ What has brought about the slump and
cursed W.A. mining was the action of London promoters.
The nominal capital represented 150 millions, and per-
haps five milli ns cash had heen sent out. The men who
had thrown dust in the eyes of the Premier were men who
regarded mines as a gambling-table, scrip as dice, and
had wrung huge sums of monev from widows and
orphans at the expense of W.A.’s'reputation. Thev all
knew of instances of mines bought for £2OOO, boomed up
to £2oo,ooo—and yet cables had to go every fortnight for
£2OO to pay wages !” Very true, hut did Moran, or any
other member, raise his voice against this thing when it
was at its height? Did a single newspaper utter one
word of protest? No. It was a boom-time, and, like all
others, it was horn in lying, chiefly in and by the aid of
newspapers ; it was kept up bv lying; it, has been killed by
truth, and the things now declared by members of Par-
liament, newspapers, and the whole Westralian gang con-
cerning the wickedness of the boom have heen said week
in and week out by The Billetin' for three vears. “ But
what about the Great Boulder? ” is the yapping cry the
writer has to endure, as if the greatest mining “spiels”
perpetrated in Australia were condoned by the accidental
finding of some rich mines.

The Mining Standard gives good advice to all
sending gold to market—not to send it in a retorted state
or in “ dust.” Retort cakes and alluvial are both liable to
accidental loss and pilferage—and such loss can occur
without the owner suspecting. It is safer to despatoh gold
onty in the form of bullion or melted pieces.

What is Gov. Brassey’s body-guard about
that it permits the doddering old gentleman to be
interviewed l • pressmen ? Last week he gave
away the following artless confession to a Melb.
’Ebald reporter, who wanted to know why he
didn’t bring the Sunbeam yacht back to Melb. in-
stead of leaving her in Sydney Harbor whilst he
came along by train. “As a matter of fact,” said
H. Ex., “I had to be here to-day, and, in fact, I’ve
been quite long enough away.” (As though he
could be long enough away!). “The Chief Jus-
tice has been sworn in as Acting-Governor in my
absence, and, you see, I have to pay the Chief
Justice ten sovereigns a day while he is acting.”
Ten sovereigns per day is about half the salary
paid to Brassey, and why, in the name of Reason,
should an imported failure be allowed to pocket a

Gov. Buxton lately electrified Adelaide by

* * * *
Adelaide had a fine sensation the other
day in the Nixon-Addison trial, wherein Nixon, a
railway clerk, was charged with the attempted
murder of his wife and H. M. Addison, chairman
of S.A. State Bank and a big man in local politics.
Nixon found his wife in Addison’s office at
mid-day, and stabbed the two with a boot-
maker’s knife, but they both recovered. Mrs.
Nixon is a charming woman, young and petite,
and Addison, in the box, admitted having finan-
cially assisted her, but stoutly denied adultery.
Local feeling was strongly in favor of Nixon, who
was cheered as he left the dock, and the jury was
expected to acquit, but found for unlawfully
wounding.
* * * *
One of the produced letters from Mrs.
Nixon to her husband, during the Adelaide
stabbing-trial, contains 16 “loves,” 14 “dears”
and “dearests,” a “thousand thousand bless-
ings,” and a miscellaneous assortment of “ blisses ”
and “ raptures.” She “ longs for the time ” when
she shall have given herself to her dearest Albert,
and shall have on her lap a curly-headed boy with
his dear papa’s bright blue eyes, and climaxes
with an assertion of her intention to treat said
papa “like a splendid flower,” and fill his life
“ with perfume and love ! ” None of which looks
very much as if—as she says in same letter, she
was assured by a doctor —her blood was turning
to water 1

A smart Sydney-
ite, it was found the
other day, had been
living rent - free for
many months by a neat
little dodge. The
house is in a terrace
foreclosed on by a build-
ing society. On Mon-
day when the collector
comes round the blinds
are taken down and a
“To Let” notice put
in the window, so the
tired perfunctory official, believing the house
empty, passes on.
* • » *
Strange story from Paris chronicled in
London Daily Telegbaph :
To-day an odd story is being related of the alleged
sequestration of a young Frenchwoman, who was engaged
about eight months ago as a companion by an Australian
family, and taken to Sydney. According to this tale, the
girl, after spending a few days with her new friends in
their antipodean home, was removed to another house
and kept a prisoner, on the ground that she was not ful-
filling her share of the contract. A fellow-countryman
somehow got into conversation with the captive, and re-
ported her adventure to the French residents at Sydney,
who started a collection, with the proceeds of which the
young woman was able to propitiate her employers, and,
at the same time, to defray the expenses of her journey
back to Paris. Such is the strange story which has just
been unfolded, it being added that the unlucky demoiselle
will not be able to obtain any redress, as there are legal
obstacles.
• * * *
Q. Chief Justice Griffith is resigning, by
outgoing mail to Ireland, his position of Provincial
Grand Mudturtle of the Irish Constitution, select-
ing G. S. Hutton as successor to his gorgeous
frills, and fripperies. Sam Griffith’s reason for
retiring is that his judicial duties press too heavily
on him to enable him to fill adequately the com-
missions of Masonry, whereas the ordinary man’s
way of putting it would be that his Masonic flap-
doodleries were so pressing as to interfere with the
exercise of his judicial duties. It is arguable
whether the mere fact that a man is a master-
tradesman, or a Grand High Gobblewager of a
Splittoed Order of a Mighty Ancient Anything,
or any other secret society, should not dis-
qualify him from acting as Judge. A Mason
is, at anyrate, bound by vows to love a brother
Mason better than his wife (that is, the Mason’s
wife), and the degree.of intimacy between Masons
is illustrated by the inspired local par. that tells
of Griffith’s resignation, and describes how “the
retiring Provincial Grand Motley shook hands
with each of the Bandicoots of the Whisky Tie
present, and thus formally took his leave of them.”
Is all right in the East? “Boaz.”
* * * *
In one Australian city, it is averred, a
member of our imported aristocracy lately
took to himself a new groom. When the
groom’s first dinner in that capacity was due,
a maid dumped down a plateful of meat
and cabbage on the stable-door; whereupon the
groom made for the kitchen, only to be told that
by the mistress’s orders he would have to feed in the
stable. Next midday, half-an-hour before feeding-
time, the man’s dinner was dumped outside on the
kitchen door-step; whereupon, finding the door
locked against him, he promptly fired the banquet
through the dining-room window. And now 7
another man is being broken-in to feed with the
horses.

In Sydney recently, within a day or two
of each other, an old man of 85 drowned himself
in the harbor, and a youth of 18 fatally cut his
throat. One thinking the journey would never
end—and the other afraid to face it!
AN “ARMY” IDYLL.
[FOR TUB BULLETIN.]
“ Salvo ” bonnet quaint and taking, eyes of soulful giaj.
Gown of modest Quaker making, steal my heart away,
While the big drum deals out thunder,
Sweetest! is it any wonder
That my soul has knuckled under
To your tuneful lav ?
You demure and captivating, working mischief sore ;
“ Captain’s ” testimony grating—what a beastly bore !
Let me quiz the eyes uplifting,
Kiss the dimples ever shifting
While my fancy goes a-drifting
To the Golden Shore.
Hateful is the vulgar surging of the sinful crew,
And the drunken sot emerging ’twixt the light and you •
Hallelujahs fervent growing,
Torchlight fitful shadows throwing,
Pair of lovely gray eyes glowing—
What’s a man to do ?
W.D

_ Treas. Fysh, of Tasmania, in his florid
financial statement, jubilates over a boom in the
marriage-market of 1896 of “13 per cent, above
the average of the three preceding years, and a
return to the numbers of ’9l and ’92, when we
were all happy and careless, and thought not of
the morrow.” This is Fysh’s way of saying that
only careless people get married.
* » * *
The s.s. Mataura, which arrived in the
Derwent the other day, was placed under an apolo-
getio semi'-quarantine surveillance by the Hobart
health authorities. Though the vessel was allowed
to take up her wharfage and land cargo, a barrier
was erected to block all shore communication ex-
cept that of the very necessary lumpers and
specially privileged persons. Subject to fumiga-
tion at the local hospital, some steerage passengers
bound for Melbourne were allowed to land. A
couple of Mataura firemen strayed through the
barrier and had “a good time” until next day,
when they were arrested and forcibly purified. Q.
Premier Nelson and party (eight) also went ashore,
but were not subjected to a sulphur bath. In
view of the laudable determination of the authori-
ties to do their duty by the steerage passengers
and the firemen, how Nelson escaped their vigi-
lance is an official mystery. It is also a pity.
Tasmania had the chance to do Queensland a
truly Federal service by disinfecting its Premier
and his titles, but, of course, managed to let the
opportunity slip.

Pathetic or business-like ? The other day?
an advt. calling for a married couple to reside
upon and caretake a Sydney city-office elicited
many applications. There was no mention of an
“encumbrance” clause, but one wedded pair, in
admitting a recent union, suspected a disqualifica-
tion on the possibility of such contingency arising.
They begged the advertiser to dismiss any fears
on the subject, as the event would be strictly
guarded against.
* * * *
A lady medical student was walking home
from Sydney University the other evening, and
when crossing an unfrequented space of Hyde
Park a male shadow rose suddenly out of the dark-
ness, opened its arms seductively, and murmured
“ Ducky ! ” in beer-laden tones. The medico
would have run, but she had her professional
Gladstone-bag of tricks with her, and couldn’t.
The man came closer and breathed horrible things
down her back, and told her that she was a “fair
tart ”; and still nobody came along. Matters
grew desperate, when the lady had a phenomenal
inspiration. “I don’t like live men,” she said.
“ Wot! ” said her would-be embracer, alarmed.
“No, Hike them like this’’—and she dived into
the. bag and brought out a formidable skull, which
grinned awfully in the lamplight. In 2secs. all
that was left of the man was a streak of beery
odors floating like an echo among the trees, and the
maiden medico popped her skull into her bag and
scuttled hard in the opposite direction.

The Australian press cable-combine for the
daily despatch of news from London seems to
have struck a little snag. By arrangement with
Reuter’s co. the cableman in London is allowed to
use Reuter’s wires received from its correspon-
dents in various parts of the globe. In return the
company’s Melb. representative has the perusal of
all cables on arrival from London. Reuter no v
sells he news in Victoria, N.S. Wales, Tasmania
andS.A. to papers outside the big dailies who
hitherto have monopolised the . cable-press
messages. Low prices for the despatch of these
cable items attract many influential provincial
papers to the list. One of the Wagga papers pub-
lishes all the cables simultaneously with and some-
times ahead of S.M. Herald
* * * *
From “ M.C.” The Bulletin receives the
following alleged particulars of the case of one
Brown, convicted at Wellington of illegal opera-
tion (without injury to the patient) at the time
Sheridan was on trial in Sydney for an offence re-
sulting in the death of his patient
Brown got 18 years, and, being put to brick-carrying,
broke down—as a man totally unaccustomed to manual
labor—in about a week; fell with a bundle of bricks
against an iron railing, and injured his spine so that for
the last 21 months he has been paralysed and unable to
stand. Petitioning the new Governor, Lord R., for re-
lease, he was promptly answered, “Not sufficient grounds.”
It would be interesting to know what the M.L. authorities
would consider sufficient grounds. The miserable man
was induced to perform the operation, not for lucre, but
by pity for a girl who declared her intention, otherwise,
of suiciding; he received a tremendous sentence, and was
put to the brutally oppressive labor which has made a
mere human log of him—according to medical certificate
—for the rest of his days.

Premier Seddon’s announced intention to
abolish a “ shameful and objectionable statute ”
(the C.D. Act) has brought down upon him a
deluge of congratulations from the uneasy old
hens in England who, if the C.D. Act were re-
pealed to-morrow, and the age-of-consent raised
to three-score and ten, would either have to scratchi
out some equally savoury morsel to cackle over—
or die.

Melb. : “ The Christian Endeavourists.
have been on the flute hereabouts. But they haves
a pillowy and bolstery look. No grimy foundry
lads. No scrubological lasses. All the Endeavour-
ists impress one as having' that without which,
says Joe Thompson Solomon, you ought never to
begin bookmaking— viz. , a bit of string, spondu-
licks, quids, brass, money. It is the Young
Gentlemen’s and Ladies’ Association, and the first
endeavour on the programme is to attain chronic
N iceness. ’ A sudden reappearance of the Twelve
Apostles would shock them out of their ‘ good
work.’ It has never struck them that Christi-
anity was a vulgar religion when Endeavouring
began.

Brazil—where the nuts come from—has
lately been disturbed by a religious fanatic who,
says the cable, “claimed to work miracles, and
was regarded by his followers as the Messiah.”
There is food for reflection in the cabled news that
the Messiah was killed. The Govt, didn’t believe
in his miracles, and the chances are against his
founding a Eaith of the Future. He is possibly
a Mahomet, but the time for starting a new
religion by peaceful or warlike means is not
nowadays. Try to imagine a successful Messiah
preaching the Word at a meeting where news-
paper-reporters took it down in shorthand, or
talking the Word into a phonograph ! Absurd
idea. If an irreproachable prophet were to try
his luck in Sydney to-morrow—well, he would be
. obstructing the thoroughfare.” His followers—
if he caught on ’’ —would swarm across the tram-
lines and cause him to get fined 40s. or a month—
,n!?Ment finite unsuitable for publication in
Holy Writ. Any further manufacture of Holy
Wnt is out of the question.

A N.l. paper has been interviewing a lot
of Mormon polygamic wives. Whether it picked
the ayes does not appear, hut it has no noes. The
consensus is in support of the enthusiastic view
with which one of Brigham Young’s widows
startled the readers of the North American Re-
view a few years ago. Physically, the poly-dames
find marriage a delight instead of a woe. Then
they recognise that Man has many moods. Some-
times he likes a nice, pious woman. Other times
a twirler in the mazes of the giddy waltz. Also,
as Cow Fat philosophically observes : ‘You have
one wife, she fightee you ; you have two wife, she
hghtee her.
From time to time, Prahran (Melb.) City
Council formally acknowledges receipt of a protest
from Mr. Chas. Salter against the intolerable
behaviour of the local Harmy. This pertinacious
complainant appears to be acting “on his own ”
rather than as agent for rival religious societies.
Xhe Xlarmy is baiter’s particular grievance and
he has just struck a fresh objection to its musical
rites. The discordant drums, tambourines
brass instruments, and other vehicles of distrac-
tion, says Salter in his latest petition, “ are calcu-
lated to endanger life and depreciate property bv
frightening horses and bicycles.” When Con-
verted Jane bangs her cymbals together all the
faery, untamed bicycles in the neighborhood are
apt to overpower their riders and bolt, and, if the
nearest tram-car should also take fright at the
blast of the trombone, an awful collision might

Petrus: “ Archbishop Carr will have St.
Patrick’s, Melb., off his mind directly. His next
card is the new Divinity College, Melb. Uni-
versity. Next year he interviews the Pope, and
the Propaganda, on this scheme. It is hoped
there will be a start with the College long ere the
century dies. On the precedent of St. Pat’s this
new place is to wipe out the Anglican Trinity, the
Presbyterian Ormond, and the Wesleyan Queen’s,
in one act. The old school holds that all priests
must be imported, but Archbishop Carr is im-
pressed with the necessity of having an Australian
National Priesthood, on the same lines as America
with its Divinity Colleges.”

“F. Hepburn,” from Brisbane
J[?„\£ e 0 l nt •u? Ll '5 TIK contribution “ Maroogaline ”
recalls the horrible days in Jamaica when Eyre was
Governor of the island. In justice to a much-maligned
man, let me give a personal per contra version of the
Jamaica trouble. I was in Kingston at the time. The
Ja , malca formed a very small minority
and, outside of Kingston, were completely at the mercv
of the negroes, so that our horror can be imagined at the
news that the Morant Bay court-house had been burnt
by the blacks, and that the Custos, Baron Kettle-
holdt, and other magistrates and whites had been burnt
with it. A mixture of rum and gunpowder was then
°“ be Bku,,s of the victims ; diabolical orgies
followed, and the negroes dispersed to continue the w ork
of slaughter. It must be remembered that there had
been previous negro risings in Jamaica, and some old
people even recollected the savage slaughter in Havti of
every white man, woman, and child in the island. In this
case the public buildings in Kingston were to be fired
and m the confusion the white men were to be massacred!
though the women were to be “spared.” In the excite-
ment caused by the discovery of the plot Gov. Eyre was
urgently pressed to proclaim martial law in Kingston
and it is to his everlasting credit that he refused thus to
give the whites the chance of promiscuous vengeance.
Martial law was only declared in the actual scenes of out-
rages, and the chief instigator, an educated mulatto
named Gordon, was court-martialed, found guilty, and
executed. The effect of this promptness was instan-
taneous, and it doubtless prevented the destruction of the
whole white population. Jamaica was not then a Crown
C ?vL ny r and f he Governor was bound to act by the advice
of the Leg. Council (composed of white and colored per-
a°ns); ™ hlcb fully endorsed his actions. The rancor of
the Exeter Hall party” even went the length of
demanding Eyre’s life, but the Home Parliament fully
exonerated him, and unbiassed history will do him justice.
Since the Halifax (Eng.) Chamber of Com-
merce inspired Chamberlain to bring the little
matter of injurious wool-marking under the notice
of sundry Australian premiers (including the
unbucolic George Turner, who never saw a bit
of live mutton branded in his life) there has been
an increased demand for Tattoo Oil, a preparation
that is easily wiped out in the process of wool-
cleaning. Its freedom from pitch, tar or lead
gives -tattoo Oil a special claim to the patronage
o wool-growers, and hence a recent appearance
of worthless imitations” on the Australian
market. Messrs Wilkinson, Hey wood & Co., of
Melbourne, wish to impress upon Bui.lkitn
readers the necessity of seeing that they get
Tattoo Oil when they order it. Each package of
the real, old, genuine, original “Tattoo” bears
the maker s name--David Storer & Sons, London,
and any dealer who endeavours to substitute an
inferior article for the sake of a larger profit is— >
or ought to be—in danger of hell fire.*
THE DYSPEPTIC NIGHTMARE.
I cannot sleep except I dream—
Ah, dreams of fear, and dreams that harry
My very soul. And yet I seem
To scorn the curse my sin may carry.
For God is merciful ; and such
His pity is, that in the reaping
He damns not those who drink too much,
But simply gives them hell while sleeping.
HOPE.
In the garden I am lying,
Kindly Nature looks on me;
Birds are singing, branches sighing,
All is blest tranquility.
Comes a-winging and a-crying—
Notes are ringing, birds are flying,
Hope they’re bringing (Hope was dying),
Hope is clinging, Hope is trying—
Hope beginning, Hope begun ;
Hope is winning—Hope has won I
Sydney.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, October 19, 1897.
Mr Dear Myke—
A guide to fashion, issued by a big drapery,
illustrates its creations and names them after cele-
brated warblers. On the tea-gown pages ideal
beings with regulation fashion-plate 'sinile simper
in flowing robes labelled “The Melba,’ “lb©
Patti,” “ The Albani,” “ The Crossley,” and
“ The Schmidt.” Since Florence is thus sartorially
placed in the front rank of divas, she ought to be
composing a hew and mellifluous name for public
use. Crossley keeps her own surname, dropping
her front one — en regie for a concert
the first grade. But a soprano must be “La
the fine-sounding title “ La Svengali ” no doubt
helped “ Trilby ” along tremendously. An Ade-
laide girl blessed with a soprano hall-marked by
’Ome professional approval is now anxiously
waiting to be theatrically christened, and invites
suggestions per The Bulletin. _ Among stage-
names of Australian girls “ Syria Lamonte is
about the prettiest yet invented, and “ Leonoro
O’Relli ” about the clumsiest—it looks so much
like O’Reilly in a thin disguise (though it isn't)—
and “Regina Nagel” the most brusque. Of a
short, catchy, euphonious name for a concert
vocalist the best example is “Millie Wynn ” : had
she searched a dictionary the cheerful little
soprano couldn’t have hit on a better appellation
than her own.
Many Sydney children (probably the offspring
of Chow missioners) may be seen now toddling
along in Chinese slippers. The shoes are highly
ornamented, generally with elaborate dragons
and butterflies embroidered on silk ; but
the thick, soft soles are short and heelless,
and the little Wearer, pleased that he is so gaily
shod, trots proudly along at his nurse’s apron-
strings with the characteristic Chow jog-trot. If
the kiddies of to-day take to working their heels
and toes in the true Celestial shuffle, the much-
abused Australian gait will have still more con-
tumely heaped upon it.
On Thursday afternoon the Victoria Club Salon
lured visitors to the Artists’ show in Vickery s
Chambers by dispensing music and muffins. It
was a dressy affair. A Salon gathering is always
a battle of styles, and the new picture-galleries
give a perfect back-ground for fine clothes. Tom
Roberts was having a tea party the same after-
noon in his new studio, on the same floor, which is
now pretty nearly monopolised by devotees of art.
Many of the guests injured their digestions at
both shivoos. There were some notables, besides
brushworkers ; inside of 20 minutes I counted two
poets, a vocalist, two art-critics, musicians in
plenty, and one man who “ talks a little.”
The annual demonstration by the St. John
Ambulance Society, on Saturday afternoon, filled
the galleries of the Town Hall, while the ground-
floor was given over to the branches and their ex-
hibition of surgery and bandaging. Tim giving-
out of cards and medallions by Mrs. Ferguson
was long and wearisome, and as the recipients
filed up the loudest burst of applause was for the
police who tramped up in procession to get their
awards. The ladies who demonstrated wore black
and white. Girls who came for their cards
tripped neatly up to the presenter, looked their
thanks and walked calmly off ; but the men
strolled shamefacedly up and hustled the act
through as quickly as possible. Some murmured,
others emitted a loud “ thankee ” audible all over
the hall, others merely grabbed the grey cards and
shot past blushing furiously. As the really brave
loathe personal adulation in public, I often wonder
how it is that any man ever attends to receive his
medal. The demonstration of first-aid was in-
teresting. Every class rushed at its subject and
bandaged him or tied him up in splints in half a
jiffy. And in about five minutes the hall looked
like a battle-field without the bleed. Among the
most applauded items were the smart work of the
Meat Works Corps, who showed’ how to improvise
splints and bandages out of stringy-bark ; and the
stretcher on wheels improvised from a bicycle,
some bagging, saplings, and a clothes-line, on
which a wounded hero was wheeled around by his
mate. < ■
Overworked lodge-doctors are the firm friends of
the above society. A knowledge of first-aid
among the men in a huge concern like the meat-
works or sugar co’s., where cuts from huge knives,
slashes from “boners,” or scalds from boiling
syrup are happening all the time, often saves the
doctor a long journey ; while the Civil Ambulance
Brigade, which supplies transport to the nearest
medical assistance, is looked upon coldly by the
hospital authorities. They declare that injured
folk run in on the slightest pretext on the brigade’s
stretchers often turn out to be very slightly hurt
or merely drunk.
Money from the country isn’t flowing freely into
che coffers of the hon. treasurer of the consump-
tive homes ; town folks respond, but up-country
returns are disappointing. One reason of this
hard-heartedness may be that country people
can’t, at long range, get the smile direct from
Government House. Cine, views exhibited in
backblocks towns, showing the Hon. Dorothy
Brand receiving a cheque, or Mr. H. Levy with
his tallest sheep-hurdle collar raising his hat to a
subscriber, might work wonders in scooping in
P.O. orders and notes.
The Gay Parisienne hopped on the stage on
Saturday night again in her well-known frocks,
which have both of them wilted and crumpled in
retirement; fresh chiffons will have to be found
for the new-comer, Ada Reeve, if Melb. audiences
are to get a glimpse of J. Bonbon’s style. Her
<ong, “ Only a Penny,” brought out the browns
from the pockets of a crowded gallery more effec-
tively than a charity conference with a Premier at
its head. A new feature was a new and acceptable
song by E. Shand, haritoned by J. Peachy.
Among the d.c. were many members of the nuirter-
otis dramatio co.’s here pro tern. The Paultons safe
side by side, the lady, about her husband’s height,
motherly, grey-haired, in unobtrusive black. A
large leading lady and a friend loomed conspicuous
in the front row, clad above the waist in their
own loveliness and a strip of gimp trimming. Cer-
tainly the gimp was wide, but still it was only
trimming.
Prof. Scott’s lecture on Women’s Work and
Wages before the Suffrage Society in the School
of Arts had many good points, his most applauded
hit being made at the charity-ball nuisance, which
he slated vigorously, with the evident agreement
of the large audience. The professor declared that
the dancer at a charity hop merely goes for the
dance and the supper, and lays the flattering unc-
tion to his soul that he is squaring the heavenly
ledger by making large charitable entries. A lady
speaker replied to this, noting the incongruity of
ideas in many generously-supported annual dances
for Good Causes. “For instance,” declared the
speaker, “ 1 couldn’t dance without a shudder at
the Tempe Refuge ball.” But promoters of that
particular hop may quote Scriptural precedent, for
if there is joy in Heaven over one sinner, what s
the matter with gladness on earth over a whole
homeful of them? The lecturer denied that
women for special work weren t paid at men s
rates, taking the rather exceptional cases of Bern-
hardt and Flora Shaw, whose special gifts earn
their out-of-the-way incomes.
The case (dismissed) against the Forest Lodge
schoolmistress points out strongly that harmful
unnecessary caning is no part of school discipline
for tiny children, but only makes insubordination
and general ructions. The teacher was alleged to
have severely caned a little girl of eight, so that
her arms and legs bore marks of the bruising.
Medical evidence for the defence showed that the
child's flesh would bruise easily; also, it was
stated, that although charges of unkindness to
pupils had been brought against the teacher before
they had never been sustained. A little girl of
eight can’t be such a monster of iniquity as to need
keeping in order by beating her legs and arms
black and blue. No parents, who are not callous
brutes, could be expected to allow such punish-
ment to pass without remonstrance. Then there s a
scandal and a police-court case, the teacher s pres-
tige suffers, and she has to cane harder than ever
to beat back respect for her into her flock. Elimi-
nate the cane altogether for young children, and
all such complaints vanish.
The juvenile dress-up was Saturday evening’s
attraction at the Artists’Show, which, ever since
its opening has kept the ball rolling in the way of
amusements. About as many grown-ups as chil-
dren were present. The prize-giving was a great
success for the infants whose costumes met the
judges’ eyes and a howling failure for the mites
out in the cold. One dainty, green-froeked dam-
sel —her portrait is in the show —lifted up her
voice and wept because her bosom friend received
a highly-colored toy-book while she had nothing ;
her friend, a chubby little nun, promptly shared
the prize, and they gloated over the treasure to-
gether. A smart coon was held up to admiration,
also, a court lady and a huntsman. The presi-
dent’s daughter carried about a palette and took
third prize as emblematical of the Society ; artist
Souter's children were original in nightgowns and
candlesticks ; a bright red imp was picturesque.
Miss Cowan led the procession and, aided by
others, worked up the ball successfully; a nice
party, but the boys looked a trifle bored. lhe
small boy’s idea of a shivoowas tersely summed
up once by a youngster I know, in reply to his
sister’s query of how he had liked a party she was
taking him home from. “Had a splendid time,”
he recounted joyously, “ one dance, three fights
and five suppers.”
Victoria Salonites, following in the footsteps of
their Melb. sisters of the Austral Salon, are, for
the second year just dawning with reasonable
rosiness on the institution, to be admitted to after-
noon crushes solely per official ticket. “ Please
present this card at the door is an epidemic
phrase now. Members are to have another half-
guinea sprung on them, the entrance-fee (now due,
as the circular grimly remarks) being raised
50 per cent, on last year’s. The Club and
Salon combined is on the look-out for a new home,
as the trams outside in Phillip-street drown not
only the music, but the chatter.
The programme of Madame Henry’s last monthly
meeting was made up of an amusing comedy (in
French) and several songs and recitations. A
promising pupil of Madame s repeated, by re-
quest,” the scene from “Ruy Bias” which con-
tained the address to the Virgin. This item, an
enthusiastic scribe reported in a glorious anti-
climax : “ Her invocation to the Virgin was given
with much feeling, and the costume worn was
wonderfully rich and beautiful.
There are two Richmonds in the field just now
to aid the poor governess who is becoming a much-
boomed person. One is the new Teachers’ Registry,
to be opened soon by the Teachers’ Association. It
is not a charitable institution, but will be run on
business lines as a glorified registry-office, and any
little surplus left over after working exes, will be
set aside as a nest-egg for a superannuated
teachers’ home. The Australian Governesses
Association is also started, and at a meeting held
recently, chaired by Mr. Larke, agent for the
Canadian Govt., the aims and objects of the
society were set forth in a paper by its president,
Miss Nisbet. So far as a bewildered outsider may
judge, the two movements are travelling like two
cable-trams on the same set of rails. But in prac-
tice they ignore each other. Competition may
make business lively, but co-operation would work
better in the long run.
The melancholy duties of the Buxton suite in-
clude carrying about an air-cushion for Lady Vic.
B. It must lessen an aide’s dignity in the eyes of
the surrounding audiences when he trots meekly
in the vice-regal rear into concerts and such with
an inflated rubber bag under his arm. He can’t
be haughty under such circumstances, and nobody
has any use for a suite unless lie’s “ hawty.”
Writes my Melbourne correspondent
“ The handsomest of the Melb. Wesleyan nuns
is just married. The old hard-shell Methodys rub
their hands in glee, with ‘Told you so ! ’ for they
hate the Wesleyan nunhood like poison, with
many gro-a-ns, as a step towards the Scarlet Lady.
Pastor Edgar puts the best face he can on the
matter, and gives the cream of his nuns a graceful
send-off. ' She is tall, handsome, rather aquiline,
and a first-class business-woman, a combination
which bursts the chrysalis of the Wesleyan Con-
vent. Of course, none of the others would do it
on any account.

ladies, by the way—must have a poor time, as

her expensive orders came from Sydney women.,

“At the Bijou, last Saturday night, those
enterprising Sisley sisters showed what they could
do turn and turn about _ with a long-winded,
painstaking orchestra. Miss Barbara, as Juliet,
was winsome and pretty, but her Hubert was a
small, unconvincing, namby-pamby villain, with
his comic facial make-up, and slim girlish feet and
ankles in black stockings, and ladylike evening
shoes, showing below his brief brown ulster.
Sister Olive, as Juliet’s nurse, had also simu-
lated a funny, sharp, little nose, by some mis-
guided make-up device, and was so daubed under
the chin to suggest wrinkles that an outsider
would have thought the cats had been at her.
Burglar Bill, as represented by one of the girls,
had put too much black under his chm. too, to
give him an unshaven look, probably. He was a
butter-fingers, this ruffian, and spoiled lots of
matches in foiled attempts to strike them on his
pants, but his chief fault was his resemblance to
‘Ran, Ran the dirty old man, who washed his
face in a frying-pan.’ Nevertheless, the Sisley
girls are promising elocutionists with youth, looks,
and courage in their favor.
“Boor Shepherd Punvis is quite out of conceit
witli his charges (the Frazer Island blacks) since
their drunk - and - disorderly reception of the
shocked Brasseys, and the flight of the latter back
to the Sunbeam. Had things gone smoothly, and
the distinguished guests, on coming ashore, been
welcomed by smiling darkies, neat as new pins
and good as gold, the guide, philosopher and
friend of the dusky saints would probably by now
be the proud possessor of a presentation copy of
4 Our Voyage in the Sunbeam,’ with watergraph
inscription by the authoress’s husband Gov.
Brassey’s unfailing reward of virtue.
“Miss Ethel Castilla, who read a paper on
4 Browning’s Message ’ the night H.E. visited the
Salon, was subsequently honored in this way.
And if you or I had the luck to-morrow to rescue
Baby Brassey from drowning, or to avert a col-
lision between the Sunbeam and some huge mail-
boat, by waving a scarlet underskirt in token of
danger ahead, we, too, might confidently count on
similar valuable additions to our libraries.
“ Small wonder that Lady Brassey and her nieces
took to the boats at the first glimpse of the groggy
darkies aforesaid. In some Bananaland towns,
hot so very long ago, the sleeper generally started
from his beauty-nap on Saturday nights to hear
through the tfiin wooden walls the street carry-
ings-on of the local tribe staggering home to camp.
As often as not a crashing blow, soundiwj heavy
enough to fell an ox, would testify that Spider was
quelling his gin with a waddy. Oil Monday, how-
ever, when Judy came to scrub the verandah, she
would look much as usual, except for a huge gash
on her head only half-covered by her red turban
handkerchief.
“ A Southern Governor and his womankind are
either incredibly don’t-careish and indifferent to
matters of domestic detail, or else they are
double-dyed, rampant economists. At a recent
4 little dinner,’at Govt. House, the meat course
included only a skinny pair of fowls at one end
and a heaped dish of mutton-chops at the other.
Most of the non-resident guests had breakfasted
that morning on chops in their own suburban
villas, and the majority, in view of the present
costliness of joints, had doubtless lunched on cut-
lets also. Nevertheless, they finished up the day
by dining on mutton, arguing inwardly that the
host, with his numerous and distinguished house-
party (most of them titled), had the best right to
the poultry. ‘Noblesse oblige’ cuts both ways,
you see.
44 If the newspaper account of manslaughtered
and flogged W.A. blacks is reliable, then Legree,
the slave-torturer in ‘Uncle Tom’s Cabin,’ com-
pared to some of our own compatriots, was a soft-
hearted sentimentalist. Old-school Westralians,
however, have the stupendous assurance to al-
lege that they always hit it well in past
days with the local natives, who were in-
offensive creatures and not so very lazy.
Nigger-whacking, ironing and shooting (say the
six families) entered the province along with
t’otherside gold-seekers. Old \V. A. residents look
back 45 years to times when as many as 300
friendly natives would camp at the foot of a
settler’s garden. After tea the ladies and children
would stroll down to the encampment to plaster a
half-roasted piccaninny or dose a coughing gin
with honey and vinegar, and from all the un-
trained savagas gathered at the fires the visitors
never received so much as a saucy look. In those
days would-be colonists had not yet realised that
Westralia was not pastoral or agricultural but
mineral. Families ariving from the old country
would travel 80 or 100 slow miles from Perth in
bullock-drays to settle down on freehold land
which only fed one sheep to several acres and
ended by breaking the purchaser’s heart.
Girls just released from British boarding schools
soon get accustomed to the sight of natives of both
sexes hanging about the kitchen ‘mid nodings on
but a coat of grease, a dingo’s tail round the head
and not always an opossum waist-fringe. A
newly arrived settler, a cheerful Irish gentleman
with a string of half-grown daughters, fresh from
the pruderies of spinster governesses and maiden
aunts, tried to bowdlerise matters for the inno-
cents by remarking in a casual, accidental sort of
way in their hearing that “of course, black men
and white were birds of totally different feather 1
His hearers, so far as he knew, swallowed his state-
ment with childlike credulity, but nowadays they
tell their descendants that, seeing for themselves
how strong was the family-likeness between
daughters of Mother Eve, whatever their color,
they began to ponder, for the first time, whether
what was true of the goose might not apply
equally well to the gander.

Indian-expert Col. Home’s report on the
prospects of irrigation in N.S.W. is a gloomy

Personal Items.
In making such a unanimous pother about
Tom Finney, M.P., and his dog-bite, it was not
the politician whom the Brisbane (Q.) press para-
graphed, but the hunky advertiser.
Laurence (known as “ Larry ”) Benjamin,
ex-clothier, is perhaps the wealthiest individual
Melbourne city property-owner, now that mil-
lionaire Payne has handed in his last cheque.
The recent death, in England, of George
Ashwin Cheeke, associate of N.S.W. Justice
Cheeke, of the early days, removes the third son-
in-law of old “ Bill ” Long. Martin and Dallev
died in Sydney.
HAPPY HARRY.
“I am saved and happy. My heart has been filler
with JOY. Give up your evil ways and be
happy with me. Hallelujah, hallelujah.”
Excellency Lambing seems to have caught,
on behalf of Brisbane (Q.), the Sydney scare about
the destruction of wild flowers. He says that
ferny, flowery, sylvan bowers should be preserved.
Whereat people chuckle consumedly.
Chief-Justice Way, rich and a bachelor,
paid all the expenses of his recent visit to England
to be sworn in as a Privy-Councillor, and he also
forfeited half of his official salary, which was paid
to the barrister temporarily appointed to the
Bench.
It is claimed that an ancestor of Father de
Boijmenu, now on his way to the New Guinea
mission-field, was present with Joan of Arc at
the coronation of Charles VII., 600 years ago.
This looks, from a Potts Point view, a sheer waste
of ancestry.
The claims of Barristers Harris and Wade
for the. N.S. Wales District Court judgeship
to fall in on the retirement of veteran Judge
Forbes are pressed by friends with such an equ d
cogency that it is now considered that some silent,
standing-by dog will get the bone.

Thomas MTI wraith began his Australian
career between 40 and 50 years ago with an em-
broilment in one of the biggest law actions of that
day, and some of his Q. friends prophesy that he
will close his career in equally signal fashion. On
the first occasion he won all along the line.
Judge Boucaut (S.A.) will be 66 Oct. 29 ;
and N.S. W. Sergeant-at-Arms Larry Harnett
has a birthday on Oct. 31. Opposition-leader
Lewis (Tas.) and Federal-delegate Venn (W.A.)
both celebrate their birthdays Oct. 27. The
latter is 53 and the former somewhat younger.
“Paddy” Hannan, discoverer of gold at
the Boulder, Westralia, is to be awarded two town
lots, to be selected by the Warden. No money—
the Govt, apparently hasn’t had enough to pay its
bills on call lately ; but it has hundreds of thous-
ands of miles of country, for which there has not
been quite the same demand recently.
John Chipp Hamp, who defended Nixon
at Adelaide the other day, and has during the last
few years been in most of the big criminal cases in
N.A., is little over 30 ;heis a S.A. native, from
Port Lincoln way, and has political tendencies.
Nixon’s work-mates at the Islington loco, sheds
found most of the money for his defence.
Self-advertisers profusely circulate an ap-
peal for subscriptions to the tomb of Baron von
Mueller, in the obscure little St. Kilda cemetery,
visited by about one person in the week. “To
keep alive his memory. ” Pooh ! Nothing can
kill Baron von Mueller —not even the superincum-
bency of 20 tons of granite, but the finest monu-
ment in Melb. cemetery fails to keep Bart. Clarke
alive.
N.S.W. Justice Minister Gould’s com-
placent assertion that no good could have resulted
from a scientific examination of murderer Butler’s
brain has now to be compared with the anxiety
of Professor Lombroso to make the aforesaid
examination. But, then, Lombroso is only the
greatest living criminologist, and Gould is—
Ghould !
Jersey was the only N.S.W. Governor who
trusted himself to speak extempore. Hampden
appears now and then to do so, but it is said to be
because he forgets what he committed to memory.
Carrington used to carry his MS. in his pocket
and, when stuck, unblushingly pulled it out even
though he had begun, “ I did not intend to deliver
an address on this occasion.”
King George 11., of Tonga, whose 6ft.-
high wedding cake ex-Premier Dibbs joked about
when shown at the islands, is said, in latest
reports, to kick against marriage altogether. His
advisers dangle a pair of beauties before him, but
his own tastes wend otherwheres, and between all
he sits down and sulks. When a student at
Newington College, Enmore, he was notably
bashful.
Bennett, one of the Q. strike prisoners,
who has been putting in a year or two at the
White Cliffs opal-mines, leaves in a month for
Lane’s Paraguayan colony, Cosme. With him
goes Mooney, a prominent figure in the last
shearers’ strikes. Bennett, who has late letters
from Lane, discredits the statement that the latter
is acting as general immigration agent for the
Paraguayan Govt.
Charles Harpur, the pecuniary distresses
of whose aged sister turn the mind back to the
early days of Australian verse-building, was urged
by Robert Lowe about 50 years ago to pack up
his literary accomplishments and share his (Lowe’s)
luck in England. Lowe’s liking for Harpur’s
verse was not a passing weakness; all through
his life he insisted on placing the Hawkesbury
native among the immortals.
“ Sas ” Williams, the rising Melb. artist,
was a leading spirit in a recent controversy among
the Vic. National Gallery students as to whether
wood-carving should have a place in the annual
exhibition. “Sas” favored the innovation, but
George Pontin, the seascapist, was dead against
the carvers, and wound up with “ What are these
carvers? Mere hewers of wood!” “ And what
are you,” asked “Sas,” “ but a drawer of water! ’’
Graham Berry fought strenuously on the
popular side at the first Vic. Parliamentary elec-
tion under manhood suffrage, 13th Oct., 1859. He
was then a. young man of 37, and was, because of
his oratorical powers, elected to the Assembly in
the following year. Thirty-eight years after, prac-
tically on the anniversary of the success of man-
hood suffrage, he is rejected at the age of 75. The
’ld man probably has not a shilling to bless him-
self with.
Henry Lawson writes that he is leaving
his little Maori hamlet for Wellington in a few
weeks. Both he and Mrs. Lawson find Manga-
maunu too unutterably lonely : there is neither
butcher nor baker, and white faces are seen about
cnee a month. So it’s knock out your pipes and
follow him to the capital, and maybe—-who
knows?—to London by-and-by. But the school
has made copy, at all events —probably a bookfui
of copy, so quaint and queer are Maori children.

When hangman Hutton went to Rock-
hampton in ’6B to “ tui'n otf ” Griffin, police-magis-
trate and murderer, he lodged in a pub. where
many practical jokes were played on him by
the landlord, or at his instigation. On leav-
ing, Hutton’s last words to his host were : “ Look
here, this is the first time I have been in Rock-
hampton, and next time I come here it will be to
hang you and he kept his word. The landlord
was butchered by Hutton in Rockhampton a few
years afterwards.
Duncan Gillies comes back into Yic. Par
liament looking young enough to be a persistent
office-hunter for another 20 years. Yet he has
already been an office-holder and office-hunter for
30 years. He was a Minister first in ’6B, so he
started before Berry or Longmore, and almost at
the same time as Higinbotham. Duncan was a
placid barnacle in seven Ministries, and for 13
weary years the country kept him supplied with
Ministerial screw —to say nothing of three years
more screw as Agent-General. And Duncan has
returned as rotund, and as oily, and as hungry
after Ministerial screw as ever.
A brilliant, but alcoholic, reporter got into
the Press gallery at Brisbane (Q.) Parliament
House, the other night, when the Sessional Sot
was on his legs strewing the Chamber with hic-
cough. Hie answered hie electrically • and as the
aberrant orator stumbled with his tongue, the
rep. would interject—" Good, ole man ! Hie,
givitum, hie, ’gen ! Shlopiton ! Hie! Gooronyou,
olebuck ! ” The authorities sent a keeper and
carried off “ the stranger ” into a room apart,
where he was let sober up. There is real hope for
Brisbane newspapers now that they entertain
neuromaniacs even temporarily on their staff.
In the early days, the just-deceased Vic.
millionaire, T. B. Payne, was stuck-up and robbed
of over £2O. He is said to have thereafter made
it a rule never to carry more than £1 about with
him. The schedule filed for probate, whilst show-
ing a grand total of assets, £1,068,650, gives the
amount of "money in house” as 17s. 9d. It is
estimated that had T.B.P. died nine years ago,
his estate would have had to pay probate on
£2,000,000 “ boom ” values. Eight hospitals and
other charities will cut up £60,000 among them
under the will. The writer happens to know that
of late years, Mr. Payne rarely spent less than
£5OOO a year in subscription-charity, and yet his
name hardly ever appeared in lists.
Mr. Frank L. Gardiner (well-remembered
in Australia as the husband of the gallery idol,
Miss Carrie Swain) is reported as about to visit
N.S.W. in connection with the trouble at the
Wentworth mine, in which he is one of the biggest
shareholders. The pallid Gardiner, with the
raven hair and the stony black eyes of Sitting
Bull, has had astounding luck in London, where
he is one of the best known and most important
magnates of the mining market. His chief suc-
cesses have been in Westralian scrip—he it was
who took Zeb. Lane from Brookman and handed
him over to the Barnatos, to the great gilding of
the latter. P.L.G. runs a magnificent flat in
Paris, and drives the best team of horses seen in
the Champs Elysees.
It has, so far, not been printed that Butler,
just before he was hanged, confessed that he
kicked up that hideous racket in and on the way
to court, in order that he might gain time to learn
by heart the “ statement ” which had been pre-
pared for him to deliver to the jury. A prisoner
is not allowed to read his statement, but is sup-
posed to deliver it extempore; and, as the yarn
made up for Butler reached him too late to be
learned by rote in the time at his disposal, he
shammed an epileptic fit in order to get the neces-
sary leisure to commit it to memory. After froth-
ing, yelling, kicking, and biting all and sundry,
he was locked up in his cell with his “statement,”
which he calmly settled down to study, and was
letter-perfect in it when the court resumed sitting.
J. S. Reid, who has just successfully pro-
moted the £350,000 co. to take over the Emu
Bay-Mt. Bischoff line and run it down to
Mt. Lyell, is one of the pluckiest and
brainiest of the “ Broken Hill
crowd.” It took a good deal of
push to get into that crowd, in
the first place. When Silverton
“broke out” Reid, who had
come down from N. Queens-
land (where he and his
brother ran the Hodg kin-
son Minek), was making
bread and an occasional bit
of cheese out of a little
newspaper at Wilcannia.
After a lot of trouble, he
got tlie plant across to Sil-
verton, later on “ bar-
racked ” consistently for
Broken Hill, and bought
share by share with his
savings and makings, until
he was able to sell out of
the little paper and “ ope-
rate.” He dropped a tidy
slice of his wealth in the
attempt to make the Euri-
owie tin-field pay, but
he has made a fair
recovery by several
shrewd schemes. Just
after Reid settled at
Silverton another re-
putedly canny Scots-
man, Alexander Riddel,
settled at Broken Hill and
set up a printing press there.
Other things being equal, the
second canny Scotsman ought
to have had the best of it. Some-
how, though, things slipped through his fingers;
and of late, after much buffeting, he has been
editing Charters Towers Eagle, and saying nasty
things about the men into whose circle he tried
hard to get.
Dr. Nicholson, three times Speaker of
N.S.W. Leg. Council before Responsible Govern-
ment, will be 89 years old next month, and is thus
the oldest living representative of early Australian
politics. He was returned member for Port
Phillip at the first Parliamentary election held in
Australia, in July, 1843 ; was made a baronet in
1859 ; and is several sorts of University magnate.
A STAGE-VETERAN; “JIMMY HASKER”
Colonel Home, who left Sydney the other day,
is an ideal ci vilser vant of the old school. He handed
his report on irrigation to the Minister for Agri-
culture, satisfied himself that the last instalment
of his salary had been deposited to his credit, and
shaking hands with those immediately about him,
prepared his traps for England. M’s.P. again
and again tried to tap him in the course of his
official survey, even Ministers “ bit his ear ” for a
trifle of information in advance, but in vain. “I
engaged to have my report—[for which N.S.W.
gave him =£2ooo] —ready in 12 months. It will be
ready then,” was his invariable reply. And it
was ready to the day and hour. When the
Colonel arrived, Queensland and other provinces
had it in contemplation to engage him when his
term in N.S.W. was up, but his exclusive reti-
cence discouraged overtures.
Chief Justice Griffith (Q.) knows a lot about
Law and Federation, and Constitutional Govern-
ment, and the Beautiful Art of Feathering One’s
Own Nest. He is also an authority on Mixed
Metaphors—or else he doesn’t know the difference
between Noah’s Ark and the Ark of the Covenant.
Which is strange for Samuel. In his State-paper
on the Draft Federal Constitution, lately presented
to Q. Parliament, he solemnly refers to “a fear
that the sacred ark of Responsible Government
may not find a secure anchorage in the unknown
sea of a Federal Constitution, and a desire to
establish an artificial shelter for it.” Noah’s fake-
ment, whatever else it was, wasn’t sacred ; and the
other thing wasn’t a boat and didn’t anchor in
any sea. Samuel should go to Sunday-school.
As he stands, he is a disgrace to his reverend pa.
A well-known Australian, who lately sui-
cided, had a wonderful nerve. On one occasion,
after a drinking-bout with a friend, Billy (that
name will do) suddenly suggested suicide, and his
companion agreed. The proposer took a pack of
cards and shufSed them. “ The first man to draw
the ace of hearts shoots the other,” said he ; and
the task of shooting fell to the other. “ Come to
my office,” said Billy ; “ I’ve a gun there. You’ll
first shoot me and then yourself.” With mingled
feelings, the other followed. Billy loaded a Mar-
tini, handed it to his companion, and stood
against the wall, but the other vanished with all
speed, Billy caught him, gave him a hiding, and
condemned him for a coward. “By God ! ”
said Billy, “if that ace had fallen to me I’d have
shot you dead!” And no one who knew him
doubted it.
The supreme failure at the Vic. Elections
was one Christie, who stood for Dandenong, and
polled 69 votes out of a total of 2110, divided
among four candidates. Christie is a haw!
nephew to the chief partner in the firm of Christie
and Manson (London), the autocrats of “ high-
art ” auctioning, The family, in dear old Eng-
land, will have a pocket-borough ready shortly for
the dear boy, so he said, but in the meantime he
desired to get some political practice in a
“colonial” parliament. Christie never doubted
that he would finish on top at Dandenong.
He secured the services of a large pressman who
manages the dog-fight department for a local
sporting sheet, and instructed that potentate to
secure his return. Also, the charming Mrs. Christie
undertook to canvas the electorate on Primrose
League principles, and the combined efforts of
Christie, the lady, the massive sporting editor,
and the long beers, brought 69 grinning voters
up to the scratch.
