Art and Other Matters.
Sydney Society of Artists prints its “ prologue ”
in the middle of its exhibition catalogue, but the
English is just as bad and bombastic as if the
“prologue” were at the beginning. And this
opinion admits of no artistic disputation ; for, ac-
cording to local studios, only a literary person is a
judge of letters.
* * * •
The black-and-white section is the best in the
exhibition. There, one feek the artists are on
something like a level with their work ; elsewhere,
they are below it. The artistic possibilities of
color are greater than those of black-and-white;
but much is required when much is given. Aus-
tralian Premiers, who would have dazzled an
eighteenpenny restaurant, looked mean, they say,
at the Hotel Cecil.
For the decorative illustration of books, no
better drawings have been made in Australia than
those which G. W. Lambert contributes to the
adornment of J. M. Whitfield’s fairy-tales. Con-
sidering their apt originality of conception
and delicate dexterity of treatment, for “ no
better drawings ” one might substitute “ no draw-
ings so good.” /
Of especially noticeable merit, also, are the
heads by J. S. Watkins and Joe Wolinski; and
tihe “ Portrait of a Lady ” and “ Darling Harbor ”
by Geo. W. Hirst (the latter are not mentioned in
the imperfe»A index-list of exhibitors and pictures).
But the moi’t tone chosen to show Darling Har-
bor should always be purple ; the play of evening
purples round the Pyrmont bridge is one of the
most constant color-effects you can mention.
• * * *
Criticism of the oils may begin with the obvious
objection that Mahony’s “To the Bitter End ”
isn’t “To the Bitter End.” He shows two cen-
taurs grappling on the edge of a cliff which leaps
to a sheer abyss. Up the perspective three
females, cleverly grouped, watch the contest.
(Presumably a female centaur is also more or less
built on the traditional male model; and as the
ladies, so far as visible, are not so built, they may
be alien nymphs. But that is a detail.) The
coloring is attractive ; and the whole conception
strikes one as worthy of inspired development.
But the whole conception hinges on the fight—
the terror and ferocity of that fight should glare
insupportably out of the canvas and give you a
pain in your head just as a glaring light gives you
a pain in your eyes ; and there isn’t any fight
worth mentioning—consequently no horror, con-
sequently no effect, consequently no value for the
lovely .£l5O which the artist asks for his picture—
and which he has no doubt earned by his labor
and sweat, though he hasn’t earned it by the result
of his labor and sweat. It is only by study and
intellectual accumulation of evidence (such as the
title, and the edge of the precipice, and the
THE DROUGHT BROKEN.
SAMUEL SOAKER (who has been “ off the grog” for a few months, discussing the
pastoral outlook): “Say, mate, no mistake but drought has broken
up properly now !”
anxiety of the nymphs) that you can convince
yourself that the centaurs are meant to fight. At
first glance they remind one of Lawson’s verse
about Jack Ellis —
He took my hand in a distant way
(I thought how we parted last),
And we seemed like men who have nought to say
And who meet—“ Good-day,” and who part—“ Good-day,”
Who never have shared the past.
So it is hardly worth while to complain that the
face of the beaten centaur is hilariously grotesque
rather than agoniscdly tragic, or to insist on minor
faults of technique (easily forgotten in major
merits). Mahony has not merely missed the
bull’s-eye: he has missed his target altogether.
And when you do that in art there are no three
tosses : it’s sudden death—and fatal.
But it only looks inconsequent to vow and pro-
test that Mahony’s failure on the grand scale is
worth to Australian art precisely 8237 of the cheap
successes of complacent mediocrity with which
Julian Ashton year after year deluges our exhibi-
tions. Julian Ashton is a practising Sydney
artist of considerable experience and capacity and
influence ; and his name is an artistic stumbling-
block and scandal because he sits in judgment
on the work of other practising artists as a
member of the Board of Trustees of N.S.W. Art
Gallery—to which he has sold pictures. And,
with the talent to produce something good if he
tried, Julian Ashton seems at present to produce
little more than a monotonous succession of com-
monplace female figures—some well-painted, some
indifferently-painted, but all abundantly lacking
in artistic virtue or artistic stimulus. Of course, a
man does as he pleases, and an artist likewise; but
the man or the artist who makes public appeal in-
vites public judgment. Which is why anyone
with some sense of the artistic ideal can take
.pleasure in opposing Mahony’s picture to the
ruck of Ashton’s —high failure and low success.
* * * *
All thif time Sidney Long has been patiently
waiting. Now, there have been pictures by Sid-
ney Long which no amount of patent pepsin could
enable this critic to assimilate. They might have
been art; but they looked like arguments to prove
that a cockroach has an immortal soul with a bone
knob on the end like a Cape York woomera. Yet
apparently Sidney Long is developing and clarify-
ing : a melody comes out of his mist. “ The
Spirit of the Plains ” —a dim nymph leading
flock of quaint birds through a dreamy, sensuous
color-scheme —has the sweet seduction of Keats.
“ A Sea Idyll ” is another slow draught of poppy-
syrup, not made quite so strong; and there
is a pretty glimpse called “On the Road”
—only a glimpse, but a glimpse one loves
to linger over. Yet one comes back to “The
Spirit of the Plains ” —the land of the mild-eyed
melancholy lotos-eaters is full of just such visions.
Were art-critics as rich as they deserve to be,
Mr. Long would receive his cheque for 50gs. by
to-morrow morning’s post.
Alice Muskett’s pastels—there are a whole tribe
of them—seem particularly good this show. For
choice, take the wallflowers, where the dusky-
brilliant tints of the subject seem, to suit the
medium ; and “Spring,” where color slides gradu-
ally into beauty.
“ A Symphony in Grey and Green,” by J. S.
Watkins (most versatile of artists), looks as if it
ought to impress more than it does. The same
painter’s “ Naiad ” has subtler suggestions, yet
one somehow feels that the keynote in the har-
mony of color has not been struck. (Perhaps a
contrasting frame would help; this itch to have
everything uniform is perilously near to
plate-cleaning. Dull grey skies over dull grey
seas have a certain attraction, but it is a dead
kind of attraction,.wanting a flash of violet
lightning to vivify it.)
And one pauses a minute before Ethel Ste-
phens’ nicely-touched “ Day Dreams ; ” before
the brilliant water on the low land-edge of
Fred Leist’s “Solitude” (the sky is un-
satisfactory); before Marion Drewe’s “Un-
est,” to take away a “rather nice” impres-
sion; before J ulian
Ashton’s “Por-
trait ” (72), to won-
der what the lady
dyes her skin
with ; and before
the preposterous
Potteresque wrig-
gle of Florence
Schmidt’s arms in
Lambert’s por-
trait.
* •

water-colors —-to
‘The Ceaseless
‘Battle ’tween Sea
and Rock” —what
an ugly title !
apparently the

water - and •

R. Sidney Cocks.
“The Hail Storm”
is mostly wild and

another good exer-

real water in
“’Tween Sea and

it foams, you can
see the spray leap-
ing. The sky is
flat: the artist
doesn’t seem to
put so much
pains or know-
ledge in his skies.
Mr. Cooks’s “ Sunset on Leura Cliffs ” is a striking
picture ; and one hopes the blue is right,
it j s unfortunate that Julian Ashton’s pretty
“ Five O’ClockTea ’’chiefly suggests that he might
do better. Frank M'Comas’s “ North Head ”
does one good without giving any obvious reason

* * * *
In the sculpture section, Allen Hutchinson
models some interesting Hawaiian types. A very
remarkable ability is shown in Geo. Hirst’s
models, “ Citizens of the Commonwealth ” such
skill deserves better subjects. And Theo. Cowan
very cleverly suggests Saint Cecilia evoking the
spirits of melody from the traditional keys. Per-
haps the farther arm does not seem to sprout from
quite the right place; but it is a very difficult
sprout indeed to rear in clay-relief. Noticeable
vigor is shown in the execution of the group of
“The Taking of Samson,” by Geo. Tinworth ; but
one’s general attitude is “ Blow Samson ! ” as an
artistic subject, unless a strong conception univer-
salises him.
* « * »
One stops for a moment with Sid. Long’s fire-
screen panels in the applied art section ; and for
another with George Bray’s clever leather-work.
Souter’s posters are the only ones which show a
touch of poster-genius ; and they show more than
a touch. The whole exhibition is uncommonly
suggestive and interesting; it has some elements
of originality—gets farther away from the rut
than the Art Society’s exhibition did ; and though
that exhibition contained more artistic achieve-
ment it contained less promise of artistic achieve-
ments. While ruts are an abomination.
Rev. Dr. David Bruce (D.D. of St. An-
drews) is signalising his moderatorship of the
Presbyterian church of N.S.W. by a “ progress ”
amongst the churches of his connection. Rev.
David had a similar billet in Maoriland 20 years
ago, and so magnified his office that lie was ill-
naturedly nick-named “ the bishop ” (next to call-
ing him “The Scarlet Women,” the very hardest
thing that a real black Presbyterian can say of
“ the meenister ”is to call him a bishop). Then
he laid away his geneva gown in lavender and
drifted into journalism. He became a shareholder
in a weird print called the Auckland Observer,
then largely composed of such inspiring mental
pabulum as “ What were T.D. and X.Y. doing in
the Choral Hall vestibule last Tuesday?” and “The
popular A.H. is leaving Rathay’s drapery-store.
How the girls will miss him ! ” An unregenerate
person named John Dickson Wickham ran an
opposition rag, and would roast the journalistic
parson by quoting a column or two of these
trivialities, following each line with a small-cap.
acknowledgment— “"Rev. David Bruce, in the
Observer.” Rev. David, however, got a good
hoist when his millionaire relative, Berry, of
Coolangatta, N.S.W., died. He developed into a
clerical dude of colossal and smileless sanctity,
and he is “moderating”—whatever that may
mean—the N.S.W. Presbyterians for all he is
worth.
Solomon Blay, the late Tas. hangman, was
enjoying his glass in the Square and Compass
Tavern, when a former flagellator came in. Times
had changed for him, and he was selling hot pies.
A man who had been talking to Blay recognised
the ex-flogger. “ S’elp mo !” he exclaimed, “ this
is wot they call providential. Why I’ve been
waitin’ for this here moment ever since that blank,
blank guv me five dozen, nigh on thirty years ago,”
and with that he wiped the floor with the pieman.
When it was over, said the latter, “ W’y didn’t
yer help a pal, Sol?” “Wot!” cried Solomon,
“ You a pal! Why you wos the common flogger ! ”
“That’s all right,” answered the other, wiping his
gory nose, “ but aint you the common ’angman?”
“Well,” said Blay, disgustedly, “the bloomin’
ignorance of yer ! Can’t yer see the difference ?
When you’d worked ’em off, they warnt much the
worse o’ the operation. They turns up, I believe,
in after years, and gives yer wot for ! But my
bloomin’ patients, they never gives no more
trouble arter I’ve done with .’em.” Blay took a
humorous view of his vocation. “The Chief Jus-
tice,” said he, “ is a sort o’ semi-colon in a sentence.
It’s me as puts a full stop to it.” He hanged
“Rocky Whelan,” the bushranger. “It give me
quite a appitite,” said he, “he was such a game
un ! ”
One of the landmarks of Melbourne was
Sergt. Flannery, the burly “Tip.” who suicided
the other day at Flinders-street railway station,
which had been 20 years under his special care.
Flannery’s eloquent “ Putt out thot poipe ! ” was
as well-known to Melbourne suburbanites as the
sta'tion itself, and as a testifier in the witness-box
he was the sole survivor of that band of which the
only Dalton was head. Despite his 58 years,
Flannery was a man of immense physical power,
as many a “kicker” found to his pain. One
evening he was helping a drunken French man-o’-
warsman into the Port Melbourne train, when
the bluejacket turned and, with the Gallic
“savate,” he landed his toe in Flannery’s
eye. The sarrjint abandoned moral suasion;
he collared the Frenchman and threw him on
to the platform—and then, merciful man that
he was, he gathered up the prostrate frog-eater
and toted him up to the hospital to be re-
suscitated by galvanism. The hospital was per-
haps as well-known to Flannery as the station,
for, as part of his duty, he had the grisly task of
conveying all victims of railway smashes to either
that institution or the morgue. Possibly his long
association with the smashed-up contributed to the
unhinging of his mind, but the proximate cause,
no d‘>ubt, was the land-citm-bank smash. In court
Flannery followed the Daltonian school in matter
as well as manner, and would frequently drop a
hint to the bench : “ He’s a poor miserable wretch
wid a wake brain, and not much av thot, and wid
not the pluck to shtale. Six months’ harrd wud
be a mercy to him.” Or, under other circs., “ He’s
one av the lowest blaggards in the city, your wor-
ships—two years would do him a power av good.”
With Flannery disappears the last of the broad-
broguedMelb. “ cops ” of the seventies and earlier
years, and to-day there is left a force which boasts
Constable Bartolo Mafferzoni as one of its brightest
lights.
SPECIALLY TAKEN.
My chum took a notion
He’d send o’er the ocean
A photo, depicting his seat on a horse
And as I had a Uxture
For taking a picture —
He demanded my service, of course.
We yarded the Rat,
He’d been “ spelling,” was fat —
Conditions which tend to enliven.
He was saddled with care,
I’d a space to prepare —
Was to take when the signal ivas given.
The signal was “Go /”
I thought he said so.
But, all things considered, it might
have been “ Whoa l ”
’Tivas something I heard,
As he flew like a bird —
And the picture depicted him so !
LOUIS M.

N.S.W. Police Inspector-General Fosbery
trots out a story about a “ blind-froin-birth ” men-
dicant excusing himself, when caught reading a
paper, upon the ground that “he couldn’t be
always on duty.” The yarn is so good that it
doesn’t matter whether it was t’other fellow or
Fosbery himself who prigged it from the well-
known picture, “ An Interval in Business.”
“Col. Olcott, on whom the Theosophic
blanket of Mme. Blavatsky has fallen, got bowled
out in Maoriland recently,” writes a Bulletin cor-
respondent. “He was lecturing, in Sleepy Hol-
low, on the cure of disease by his special occult
process. Having demonstrated the passes of the
hands he would use for curing such ills as lumbago,
colic, paralysis, kidney-trouble, he went on to
state that by such means he had cured thousands
in India —all the great manifestations of Theo-
sophic power occur in Thibet, or Kafiristan, or
some other remote Oriental place, where there is
about 'OO7 of an enlightened white man to the
square mile, and the chief objects of interest are
lamas and praying - wheels. Then the bulky
Colonel called for orders, and an ‘ identity ’ got on
his feet and said: ‘ Mister, I’ve a poor blind
friend that badly wants to be cured. You’re free
to operate on him as soon as you like, and if you
cure him I’ll give the local hospital £so.’
It seemed a fair-enough offer, especially as the
expansive Colonel had said that the power only
worked when he exercised it ‘in a pure spirit of
benevolence.’ But, no, he could not accept it,
because—because he was leaving within 24 hours.
Whilst, by remaining 25, he might have cured a
man of his blindness ! The Mahatmas, Arhats,
and Adepts of his cult employ a miraculous
velocity in their migrations, but, for the present,
the lobengulous Colonel is content to deport his
‘ too too solid flesh ’ by steamer. His ‘ Atm a, ’
must strive for more complete union with his
‘Buddhi I ,’ for the further development of his
‘ Manas ’ and the subjugating of his ‘ Sthula
Sharira,’ and all that sort of hocus-pocus, and then,
after a ‘ devachanic interlude,’ he may become less
enormous, and flit about the earth like a disem-
bodied spirit. But, in the meanwhile, that blind
man is waiting to be cured.”
Re the recent Footscray explosion and the
Factories Act: the British Act compels the re-
porting of even the smallest accident; and it has
one provision which gives inspectors a very strong
hold on employers. In cases of infringement,
penalties up to .£lOO can be recovered, and the
Secretary of State may, if so advised by the in-
spector, order the whole or part of this to be
paid to the injured person, or to his family in
case of death.
Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had post free’ or 1/3. Senior,
chemist, George-street, Sydney.*
Adelaide papers are marvellously polite.
The day after the wife-shooting trial was over the
Register had a leader, in which the prisoner, just
sentenced, was carefully referred to as “Jfr.
Nixon.” Same day the ’Tiser headed a contri-
buted article —“ By Mr. P. McM. Glynn.”
Speak Out.
Thb Searchlight of Publicity is pleasing to Aus-
tralians.
Publicity is what the people want.
Let the people speak on the subject.
There has been too much claim—too little proof.
Claims made by strangers are not proof.
There is only one kind of proof for Australians
The experience of people they know.
Endorsement by friends and neighbors.
Such statements cannot be questioned.
Here is one of many for the value of Warner’s
Safe Cure for liver and kidney disease. No man
is better or more favorably known in Riverton,
N.Z., than Mr. T. Whilkes, who writes : “ War-
ner’s Safe Cure Co., Gentlemen : Judging by the
number of testimonials you receive from persons
who, like myself, have used Warner’s Safe Cure to
great benefit, I thought you would not take it
amiss to receive a few lines from myself, stating
my case and what Warner’s Safe Cure did for me.
I have not heard of any other case like it, for
medical men could not tell me the nature of the
complaint or give me any relief. It was on a visit
to Victoria some time ago that I was one day
seized with severe pains in the head and pit of the
stomach. The stomach began to swell to an
abnormal size, and the pain was *io severe I was
unable to walk about. I was advised to return at
once to New r Zealand, and returned by the first
boat, arriving home in a very weak and miserable
condition. Not knowing what to do to get relief,
some friends advised trying Warner’s Safe Cure.
I did so, with immediate relief. I continued
using the medicine till I had used six bottles. By
this time the swelling had gone down, and all pain
disappeared, and I am now in splendid health.”
Dropsy is not a disease, but a symptom of disease i
resulting from weakness of the kidneys, liver or
heart.

Holder M. : “ The female petitioner in a
Sydney divorce-case, supporting her allegations of
marital cruelty, testified to .the respondent’s ugly
habit of bursting into her boudoir unawares. A
battalion of beauty-bardlets to the contrary not-
withstanding, the average woman en deshabille
presents an unkindly spectacle, and the husband
who persists in surprising her thuswise invites a
decree nisi, together with costs, oustody of the
children, alimony and general opprobrium.”

... NINETEENTH REPORT, ...
PRESENTED TO THE SHAREHOLDERS AT THE ORDINARY GENERAL MEETING,
ON 27th OCTOBER, 1897.
The Directors have pleasure in placing before Shareholders the Balance-Sheet and Statement ol
Accounts to 30th September, duly audited and certified. The n“t profits (including'balance broughl
forward from last half-year) amount to Os. 5d., out of which they recommend payment of a
dividend at the rate of 3 per cent, per annum, which will absorb .£2250, leaving a balance of
Os. sd. to be carried forward.
The Directors are pleased to state that the Bank’s funds are now fully employed, and they would,
accordingly, be glad to receive deposits from shareholders at current rates for the purpose of extending
the business. Although the volume of business has increased, profits are kept low owing to the keen
competition in rates, which makes the Directors the more anxious to enlarge their operations, and they
trust that shareholders will take every opportunity of influencing desirable accounts to the Bank.
By order of the Board,
H. T. WILSON, Manager.
Statement of Assets and Liabilities.

Medical.
X
Rheumatism, Lumbago,
Sciatica, &c.,
oan be COMPLETELY CURED by
MR. C. F. PARKER,
145 Victokia Parade, Eitzroy, Melbourne
Extract from sworn affidavits
declared before a Justice of Peace: —
Mrs. Marks, Devonshire Arms Hotel, Fitzroy
street, near Cyclorama, aged 73 years, after being
a cripple, having all the joints stiffened, and
suffering agony over 11 months, pronounced in-
curable, failing to obtain any relief from others,
was completely cured by me 15 months ago, and
has kept in better health since.
Harry Sanderson, 493 Fitzroy-street, Fitzroy,
was cured by me over 12 months ago, when all his
joints were swollen and in acute pain, in a few
weeks.
Mrs. Nicholle, near corner of Nicholson and
Victoria streets, East Brunswick, aged 72 years,
after suffering 10 years with rheumatism in every
joint, failing to obtain relief from other remedies,
and had been pronounced incurable by five doc-
tors, was completely cured by me in two months.
Acknowledgments.
“Dear Sir, —I wish to testify to the cure you
have effected in my case, being a sufferer from
severe attacks of Lumbago and double Sciatica,
which incapacitated me from all work at intervals
during the past three years. I tried doctors,
electric baths, patent medicines, etc., without
deriving any benefit therefrom. In August, 1896,
I was very bad, suffering excruciating agony. I
consulted you, and obtained relief from the first
supply of your antidote, and am thankful to say
after five supplies I was cured. I have kept well,
it being now over six months since I saw you last.
—Heleg Exstrom, McCracken’s City Brewery,
South Melbourne, 26th July, 1897.”
Dear Sir, —I wish to thank you most sincerely
for having cured me of Sciatica, having suffered
most acute pain for a long time, but, under your
Remedy, I am glad to say that I have completely
recovered. It is now 17 months since I saw you,
and I have not had the slightest return of the
pain—one week’s treatment having quite cured
me. If reference to me will assist any sufferer to
try your antidote, I shall be glad at any time to
tell them what it has done for me.—Yours grate-
fully, Mrs. T. Hughes, 29 Starling Street, Kew,
19th August, 1897.”
“ Dear Sir, —In grateful testimony to your skill
for having cured me of a very painful attack of
Rheumatism. I suffered great pain, having the
joints swollen. After a short while under your
treatment, I completely recovered. It is now over
three years ago since I was cured, and I have kept
well, being 64 years of age. I consider your anti-
dote the marvel of the age for suffering humanity.
—Yours very truly, Professor W. T. Lamont, Ven-
triloquist and Entretenir, 4 Condell-street, Fitz-
roy, opposite Town Hall, 23rd August, 1897.”
Mrs. Laura Sunderland, 80 Bell-street, Fitz-
roy :—Rheumatic Fever, six months in bed, 12
months on crutches, joints stiffened and useless.
After six weeks’ use of antidote joints completely
cured. Can do all household duties, and has not
used either crutch or stick now for over 12
months.
Miss Mary Davidson, Crimea, Burnell-st.,

POLITICAL
POINTS
The coming idea : “ One vote for me and
one for my bike.”
The new Vic. Parliament includes 18
agriculturists, 16 lawyers, five auctioneers, three
sci-ibes, and eight nondescripts alias gentlemen.
Rumored that delegate Walker will retire
from the Federal Convention before its re-
assembling in January, and that John See, next
highest on the poll, will take his place.
Victorian Ministerial salaries : Premier,
.£1400; other Ministers, £lOOO. N.S.W.: Pre
mier, £1370; Attorney-General and Colonial
Secretary, £lB2O ; other Ministers, £1370. Which
is the saner scale ?
“ The tariff is a question of markets—not
of maxims.” “The distrust of the present will
not be removed by a distrust of the future.” “ A
patriot makes a better citizen than a pessimist.”—
A frier, political sayings.
The bike plays into the hands of the plural-
voter.. The present writer, when out to put his
paper in the slot, met one person of property who
had visited five Melb. suburban polling-booths on
his wheel and voted at each.
The Reid Government announces that it
will reform the N.S.W. Council this session, or,
if it fails, go to the country on the question. Isn’t
that the same question the same Government went
to the same country on in 1895 ?
The Reid Cabinet seriously deliberates
about a further addition to N.S.W. Leg. Council.
1 f Reid sends up another thirteen like his previous
appointees, that alone will be a sufficient reason
for the abolition of the institution.
The Maoriland Govt, proposes setting
aside £120,000 out of the current year’s surplus
as the beginning of an Old-age Pension Fund.
By all appearance, M.L. will lead the way in this
m atter—as it does in most others.
S. A. Opposition-leader Downer is so openly
bidding for Labor support that he has alienated
several of his stalwart Tory followers, and is
helping the Kingston Govt, to get a consolidated
majority independent of the Labor-party.
A N.S.W. advocate
of late Parliamentary sit-
tings points triumphantly
to the Statute Book and
claims that nine-tenths
of the Democratic mea-
sures found there were
passed in the early morn-
ing. Fat obstruction, he
contends, will run any
risk for sleep. He names
several members who, on
o -casions of reform-de-
b ites, declared they’d die
on the floor of the House
in opposition to the Bill,
but when 12 struck
dodged off home.
The hopelessness of
the tale dealt out by the
Australian free-raid press
about the loyal recipro-
cal yearnings of Canada
towards England, looms
large and unmistakeable
when the reciprocal-
yearn tariff of that cold
country is examined. Its
Freetrade yearn puts a
duty on British woollen
manufactures of 27 per
cent., whilst Victoria, a
Protectionist country
that doesn’t yearn,
charges only an average
of about 18 per cent.
Printed cotton fabrics:
Canada, 26£ per cent.;
Vic., free. White and
grey cotton goods: Can-
ada, 19 per cent. ; Vic.,
free. Pig - iron : 2dols.
80 cents, ton ; Vic., free.
Bar-iron and steel, 7dols.
ton ; Vic., free. Manu-
factured metals : Cana-
dian yearn tariff, 25 per
cent. ; Vic., free; and
then on through a long
list of similar rates.
The Bavarian House of Representatives
objects to Kaiser William claiming a personal
ownership of the German army, navy, and Parlia-
ment. Vic. Assembly has even better grounds
for asking Gov. Brassey to cancel the pronoun in
his recent reference to George Turner as “my
Premier.” Speaking of the last Federal Conven-
tion, his awful Nibs said—“ It was strange to hear
-my Premier expressing his views in Sydney.”
Vic. candidate Warr : “ Instead of borrow-
ing, people were driven out of the country, and
those left were starving, or as near that as damn
it.” Poor Warr was badly left on polling-day.
He wasn’t at the top of the poll; in fact he was
even nearer than dash it to being at the bottom.
W. A. Watt, who took the responsibility
of ousting Prendergast from Vic. Assembly, is
quite a splendid speaker in his way, has a taking
manner, and a good political education. Of course
he is a leading A.N.A. official. He is 25 years old.
King Syme soon forgets, when it suits his
book —he “whooped ’em up” for Fish Creek
Richardson and Baronet O’Loghlen this election,
just as if the big guns of the Age had never
belched red-hot shot at these discredited poli-
ticans.
BRISBANE TO NELSON.
The sounds take shape and grow,
Still deeper, deeper, deeper :
“ Hugh ! where’s thy br’er Tom Joe ? ”
Says Hugh : ‘ ‘ How should I know ?
Am I my brother’s keeper ? ”
The name of one woman (Dr. Janet
Greig) lately resident at Melb. Hospital, and now
located at Brighton, somehow got on to the
electoral-roll for last week’s Vic. general election.
The lady did not attempt to avail herself of the
privilege of voting, and missed a fine opportunity
of making her name historical.
Ballarat (Vic.) Presbytery declares that
Premier Turner’s conduct in offering to leave the
Bible-in-schools question to a national referen-
dum is “most unfair.” The guileless Vic. parson
constantly declares that the Jonah-whale crowd
are in the majority; what he is mad about is being
offered a fair and square chance of proving it.
One of the most sad-colored features of the
fiscal question in N.S.W. politics is the tale of
Cold Tea Knapp, in the Sydney daily papers,
about how some “ keen-witted merchant ” told
him something of a most disparaging character
which finally demolishes Protection. No cause is
helped much by a bore of no account stating that
some anonymous'person said something. Any-
body can say that any anonymous person said
anything.
Canadian Premier Laurier (it is cabled)
brags that he “ will never rest until the whole of
the exported products of Canada, and 90 per cent,
of those of the U.S., are carried per St. Lawrence
River route.”
Brag on, brag on, Canadian Laurier,
Your Frenchy side is not your best,
And when you’re dead, you’ll feel much sorrier ;
For, since you swear you’ll never rest
Until achieved these things you'crave,
You’ll never rest within your grave !
Premier Turner’s loyal masquerading at
the Jubilee seems to have aroused the suspicion of
Vic. Labor-members. Prendergast “ fetched ” the
horny-handed electors of N. Melbourne with a
snorting allusion to “ the portraits of a distin-
IT’S SIMPLY THE WAY YOU PUT IT.

the court for begging. How is it you lady beggars escape? ”
LADY BEGGARS: “Indeed! we are not begging—we are collecting
funds to purchase a bicycle for our curate!”
guished person in a sword and cocked-hat, and
powder and grease, and stockings, and all that
kind of thing.” But didn’t the local ’Fraud com-
pliment poor old George T. on his “ handsome ”
appearance in Windsor uniform? And is Madden
to have a monopoly of Victorian native splen-
dour? And though his constituents cheered Pren-
dergast for his attack on Turner they threw him
out all the same.
DISTINCTION WITHOUT DIFFERENCE.
M.L.C. (to ragged brother): “Ah! Bill, you and me have gone two different
roads. I have got into the ’Ouse, and you—you have gone to the devil.”
BROTHER: “ Yet, hang me if I see where you get the pull of it!”
Premier Reid announces that the idea of
establishing Local Government in N.S.W. is
definitely abandoned—for some years, anyhow.
Almost the same day the North Shore bridge-
scheme was biffed out of the Assembly by a three-
to-one majority. The North Shore bridge and
the Local Government system are the two carrots
which the N.S.W. public ass has industriously
trotted after for 30 years. And it overtook the
two fleeting vegetables at the same instant, only
to find them both bogus.
On Thursday night last week Premier
Reid announced proudly that, owing to the advan-
tages of Freetrade, the number of applications for
work at the Labor Bureau had greatly diminished.
The same day the annual statistical volume which
includes this subject was issued. It contained the
usual information—except that, for the first time,
the number of applicants at the bureau was left
out. Query : Was it the fact that the public
couldn’t check his statements which made Premier
R. go ahead so violently on this theme?
The shindykit which wants to build a tun-
nel from Sydney to North Shore will apparently
get its little job through this session. Considering
that the harbor is deep, and the exit on the north
side is blocked by a high hill, and that there is no
short way out except by hauling the passengers
up out of the bowels of the earth with the
tunnel sloped gently upwards till it reached the
open air in the usual way it would come out some-
where about Middle Harbor—this looks about the
worst place on earth for tunnelling.
Bookseller Sligo captured the Dunedin
seat (left vacant by the death of Henry Smith Fish)
for the Opposition interest by 979 votes. But
the Tory party gains nothing by the victory, for
Fish, though a Democrat in many wavs, generally
voted Tory for the sake of being against the Gov-
ernment. Fish was against almost every Govern-
ment on general, bang-smash principles. And
Sligo is a peaceful man who can’t knock things
about, and leave a trail of broken crockery and
wreckage behind him as the tremendous Fish used
to do.
The chief business of N.S.W. Labor
Bureau seems to be sending men from Sydney to
relief-works as far West as possible. Then, when
they are sacked as unsuitable, it takes them a
good while to walk back, and a fortnight’s work
may keep a whole crowd of unemployed out of
Sydney for two months, thereby greatly reducing
the amount of poverty in the city. - Orange just
now has a large number'of the out-of-work class,
and when the local railway deviation contract was
manned by Sydney men the air was filled with
blasphemy. ,
Yic. Defence Dept, proposes to arm all the
local forces with the Lee-Metford rifle, the Mar-
tini-Henry being considered out of date. Yet the
authorities of this department are the very same
who reported, as the result of recent tests, the
immense superiority of the Australian Ashton
rifle over the Lee-Metford. This leaves only two
options: either that the Defence Dept, is calmly
going to fritter away tens of thousands by substi-
tuting one obsolete weapon for another, or it has
no faith in the honesty or competency of its own
o fficials ! One thing or the other.

Maoriland' politics are full of persistent
rumours about another loan. For fully eight
years the province contrived to suppress its desire
to borrow for public works purposes. Then it
raised 2J irfillions to keep the B.N.Z. afloat, and
millions to ’start the State Land Bank, and it
began to feel the desire to go on a great burst
ptirring in its blood once more. Next it gave way
so far as to authorise a loan of One million. And
now it-is like a reformed drunk hesitating outside
the door of. a public-house and wondering if he
niight not just as well have'one more big, soul-
filling razzle after all.
F. : lie Higgins’ telling illustration of the
operation of the Tory dual-vote scheme—that it
would give Christ one vote and Judas Iscariot two
votes because Judas bought a bit of land: The
Bulletin says Judas didn’t buy any land. Fact
is that the Bible says in one part that the 30
pieces of silver were paid for the potter’s field by
the priests (Matthew xxvii., 7); but, in Acts i., 18,
Peter tells the disciple that Judas bought a field
with the reward of iniquity ! Of course, if the
Bible is in doubt about the point, it is not surpris-
ing that The Bulletin should accept one verse,
and overlook its contradiction.”
If there be any truth in the Melbourne
story as to a conference at the Age office between
David Syme, Premier Turner and Isaac Isaacs—re
the Eastern Suburbs election and the bestowal of
Age patronage upon a certain Liberal candidate-
well, then it is indeed time that Victoria should
doubt the character of the Age’s political man-
dates, and the alleged purity of boss Syme’s
motives. It is evident that the country did begin
to examine the question whether an Age despotism
is a benevolent form of Liberal government. The
result was a pretty bad pain in David Syme’s
stomach when the numbers went up.
The success of Gillies for Toorak, at Vic.
elections, was in no way due to any spasm of
popularity for that Sphinx-visaged veteran. The
fact is that the seat is now shown to be an out-
and-out Tory fortress, and Lewis was never in the
running. But the Argus and the small-numbered
but fat-pursed National Association, by raising
the scare of “ seat in danger,” managed to
establish a funk among the weak-kneed
Bryantites, and about 200 Bryant-pledged rats
prevented Duncan hearing his knell. Bryant
is likely to slay the next dear personal friend
who tells him he was very, very sorry, but had to
vote for Gillies to save the seat.
The Brisbane lesson for the day is written
in the 25th chapter of the Gospel according to St.
Horace, commencing at the 24th verse : “ Then he
which had received the one talent came and said :
‘ Bpss, I knew thee that thou art a Nevermind
man, reaping never where any one sows and
gathering never where any one straws, and I was
ryebuck, and went and let her go, Gallagher,
generally; 10, there thou hast that is thine.’ His
boss answered and said unto him : ‘ Never mind !
Have a whisky ? The Queen’s all right, Tummy’s
all right, the English girls are all right, you’re all
right, I’m all right, we’re all right, so Never-
mind !’ ” Here endeth the present Lesson.

—succeeded in retaining their seats ; also Shiels,

Harper ; James Moloney, a brother of Dr.
Pat Moloney, Melb. University's first medical
graduate; and barrister-Baronet O’Loghlen, re-
turned for Port Fairy.

DISEASES OF THE EYE.
A most valuable remedy for inflammation!,
blight, pearl or film, &e., is now being placed
before the public; it is known as “ Professor
Thomson’s Eye Water,” the sole right of which
has been purchased by Mr. Albert Sharp, of 257
George-street, Sydney, the proprietor of the
“ Cannalin ” pile remedy. Mr. Sharp has some
wonderful testimony of cures it has effected, and
being inexpensive (Is. 9d., 35., or s*. Bd., post free
to any part of Australia), it will come within the
reach of every sufferer.*
Westralia had 12 deaths from typhoid in
*9l. In ’94 the number had reached 73; since
then—’9s, 325 ; ’96, 400; first half of ’97, 299.
“Greater Melbourne,” with all its stinks, had but
97. deaths from the disease during the first half of
this year, though its population is nearly three
times that of \V.A.
The resignation of Attorney-General Sep-
timus Burt leaves Premier Forrest the last lone
survivor of the Westralian Ministry which took
office in 1890.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Apart from the fact that it published an Am-
berite “tip" in a little verse, Tint Bimum* has no
occasion to offer an abject apology for lost week s Caul-
fidS Cup remarks. Nine horses only were mentioned as
being worth consideration on paper. Ambente and Par*
thenopaeus, the subsequent winn-r and runner-up. were
included, and four others of the nine finished among the
first nine in the race. There were 26 starters, and about
20 of them carried some good money. Amberite was a
steady 20 to 1 quotation, inasmuch as the ‘ books never
lay long prices against a high-class 3-.vear-old in a handi-
cap race. Yet, as the public in general carefully avoided
Amberite on the day, he would doubtless have paid a 40
to 1 dividend on the tote. . 4
An apology for Amberite’s running in the
Caulfield Stakes was given at the time, but although it
sounded reasonable enough, punters, as arute, showed
their horse-sense in letting the Sydney Derby-w nner run
loose for the Caulfield Cup. The average excuse proves
to be a delusion—if not a fraud. Still, there are excep-
tions to the rule. Amberite s poor show in the Slake*
was attributed to inexperience of a left.-handed pou r ?,
and the statement that the Carbine colt didn t take kindly
to the Vic. way of racing was possibly a solemn fact.
Anyway there were lots of precedents for
startling reversals of form in Sydney horses at Caulfield—
the place where calculations are always getting upset,
and the sporting atmosphere is apt to stink of evil re
port. Cremorne last year won the Cup after having run
nowhere behind Hopscotch in the Toorak Handicap.
Paris won two Cups, starting at longish odds each tune
on the strength of an inglorious performance in the
Stakes race a week previous. Amberite was merely fol-
lowing the famous Paris fashion. By the way *t ..
remarkable how Sydney horses win the Caulfield Cup
five times out of the last six—and fail to shake
the big Melbourne event. Eleven years have disappeared
since Bill Gannon’s Arsenal won the Melb. Cup slake for
NSW. This year it should be taken across the Murray
once again, the Sydney invasion has never looked so
strong the recollection of the present wn ter.
Positano, Parthenopseus, Coil, Fucile, Amberite, True
Hue, Toreador, Delaware, The Grafter. With local
Aurumoutof the way, the foreigners would appear »
have Melbourne Cup matters pretty well all to themselves.
But, then, prophets must allow for the unexpected in
Turf results. The Unexpected is a horse that never tires
of happening on behalf of our beloved brother, Isaac
Moses.
The Parthenopteus crowd talked ot a protest
against Amberite, on the ground that his jooltey had
indulged in foul-riding during the early P 0 [ t, ?''° | ,he
race when they were all on the jostle. A fine idea,
truly » Would the loner-suffering public continue to sup-
port racing if the horse that finished first were subject to
disqualification through the evidence of a p® bl « con-
spiracy of wizened pigmies concerning deeds which only
themselves could swear to? Not much A® race-nding
is conducted in Australia, the early part of a Caulfield
Cup must always be a go-as-you-please within the limits
of toleration, otherwise we should have Ikey M°-bribing
half-a-dozen boys to test lfy “ foul-riding in the case of
any red-hot favorite that might have been crowded
among them away at the back of the course. -
When the cry of “ protest ” was raised after the
Caulfield Cup, the public kindly assumed that gome of the
ruling powers had backed Parthenopseus, and were going
for another Tim Swiveller-Sainfoin decision.
Staffa, who had finished last in the Toorak
Handicap, won the Windsor Handicap last Saturday.
There was a whisper that the speedy South Australian
couldn’t do himself justice on Guineas Day, owing to sore-
ness and general effects of his recent journey from Ade-
laide. But punters at large don’t hear these whispers.
They have to take “form” as they find it, and they
found it so unpromising for Staffa that the white-faced
chestnut sent them home, at the end of a bad meeting,
looking almost as pale as the orse. They all went for a
recovery on Hordern’s Mirella.
Symmetry, by Trenton —Butwrcup, ran away
with the Nursery at Caulfield, to the great profit ( of the
St. Alban’s stable. Even before the “ good thing came
off rumor had it that Symmetry would follow it up by
winning the Maribymong Plate at Flenungton.
Re Amberite’s chance of beating
V R C Derby, it must be remembered that « aterfall put
up a Caulfield Cup performance that was almost equal to
Amberite’s—the weight being 4lb. less and the time a
quarter of a second faster. Yet Aurora • 818l .® r ’,f
beat Waterfall in the Derby and Cup. Also, Wallace and
another big colt with a lumpy knee got m front of Oxen-
hani’s champion for the blue ribbon. The writer s Derby
tip is Aurum—Amberite—Fleet Admiral.

No horse had a worse run in Caulfield Cup than
Gaulus. He. had inside place, but, as soon as the race
started, he was bumped terribly. He must have lost
60yds. in the first furlong. Considering his running at
Rosehill on the previous Saturday, he should have had a
big show, as there, with Sst.,he ran the U furlongs in
2.23V6 ; so he only needed to do the extra furlong in even
y m e—14sec.—to make the Cup journey in 2.37V6, with
91b. less weight. All going to show that- no horse can be
expected to run up to form in a crowded fleld^

per working day, changed hands through horseracmg.

• • * •
Merman’s capture of the Cesarewitch Stakes
the biggest betting handicap in England bar the Cam-
bridgeshire—was foreshadowed b.v W. It. Wilson s report
of the chestnut’s excellent condition when he (W.R.) had
seen him and backed him shortly’ before sailing for Aus-
tralia in September. Merman, at 7st. 51b., won the race
by a neck from the five-year-old The Rush (Bst. 31b.), ana
the result proves that an Australian top-weight is at
least too good to be “chucked into’ a Cesarewitch. If
Maluma’s prospects are not discounted by the soreness
which troubled the mare when Mr. VVilson found her
“ looking splendid ” she will probably’ win the Cambridge-
shire, and then good-bye to Newhaven’s chance of
getting likewise chucked into handicaps at ’Ome. Mer-
man only did his duty by Australia in winning the alleged
£40,000 for Mrs. Langtry. He left this country with a
final record of two consecutive victories—in the \v illiams-
town Cup and the Yan Yean Stakes, at Flemington
Spring Meeting (’96)-his weight being over 9st. on each
occasion. Previously he had carried 7st. 121 b. in
the Caulfield Cup race, where he certainly would
have put his number up hut for getting a bad
start and being subsequently bustled up to assist
his stable companion, Resolute, at the very moment
when he should have been steadied for a rush on his own
account. Nobody knew the full strength of Merman’s
“ form ”in Australia. He was absurdly under-estimated
by the W. R. Wilson stable at the outset, and, as soon as
he began to do big things, Mrs. Langtry's commissioner
came along and hought him. Mrs. L.’s horses appear to
he pretty trickily managed; nevertheless,-the handi-
capping of Merman and Maluma for the autumn events
was unreasonably favorable to their fascinating owner.
About 10 weeks ago, outsider Merman unexpectedly ran
away with a race, the stable-money being planked upon
the "first favorite. Re this little surprise, the Referee
man wrote : “ Saturday’s running I leave to the usual re-
port. except to say that there were two stories about Mer-
man, the winner of the Lewes Handicap, who, as a colonial
with a character, wight have been given more weight .”
* * * *
Bill Martin was scalp-hunting in Adelaide, and
much of his spare time was devoted to answering charges of
insulting langwidge and assault. The gentle William was
fined £4 by the league for abusive remarks hurled at its
officials, from chairman Crnickshank downwards.
William, however, brings the gate, and the league hadn’t
the pluck to disqualify him.
An Adelaide bench has since sent Martin to
gaol for 14 days for a gross and unprovoked assault on
F. N. Ridley, "a cvclist-member of the S.A. Council which
disqualified Martin for two months over his M’Donnell
dispute. In a word, William lias been punching the
Supreme Court. The gentle Bill is reported to have said
he will race no more in Australia—which is very likely
indeed under the circs.
• * * *
“ Fitzsimmons having definitely retired from
the ring, Corbett, the ex-champion, claims the champion-
ship.” Peter Jackson has gone to America to look for a
fight. It is not quite off the cards that the old-time rivals
may come together again, but, for Jackson's sake, the
writer hopes it may not be, for even in his salad days the
darkey ever required the best of condition to make a good
showing.

to 23 l-6sec., and the half-mile to 47 l-ssec.

Dear B.—Your last issue says : “ Platt-Betts’
record-breaking attempts in Australia will not draw the
crowd. The crowd does not want to see a human machine
plugging on its own behind a quad. ... He lacks
brains. Compared with Martin, he is the cyclist who
does not know. . . . Platt-Betts could burst 16 pacers
up; but he couldn’t burst up one opponent. . . . Platt-
Betts will confine his cycling in Australia t<s record
attempts and time trials. Who really cares a rap to see
Platt-Betts, or any other man, race time ? ” In short, you
voice the general opinion that P.B. is a woolly-headed
youth of no sporting interest whatever, but in another
part of the same issue appears a long photo of P.B. with a
biographical notice attached.”— Critic.. [Well? —Ed. B.]
A striking feature of the Melbourne Friendly
Societies’ cvele-track is the “two-foot” mark, a white
line two feet from the inner edge of the track. This space
belongs to the leading man, and whoever would pass him
must cut round outside the white line or be disqualified.
The idea is a Yankee one, and ensures safety for compe-
titors, in protecting them from the trickiness of certain
racing nien.

* * * *
Since Melb. City Council introduced an objec-
tionable by-law making cyclists ring bells at every move-
ment, the Melb. wheelmen have formed a conspiracy to
carry cycle-bells in their pockets, and when crossing a
street on foot, to spring their bells behind every City
Councillor they meet. After several members have
jumped 20ft. aud upwards a repeal of the by-law may be
expected. * * *
A few years ago there was running on the Vic.
lines an engine—No. 102—with a gruesome record of 21
human lives to its debit at the time it was ordered by a
scared department to be broken up. From the London
cycling press conies a similar story of a fatal bike, which
dealt death and destruction to all who rode it. “ The
machine, now lying in Scotland Yard police station,’ says
the account, “ made its d6but some years ago when it was
found lying by the side of a young artist on Wimbledon
Common. ' The cyclist was quite dead, having evidently
side-slipped and struck his head on a large stone by the
roadside. After the inquest the bicycle was taken over
by a cousin, but on the first journey taken it slipped on a
tram-line, throwing the rider so heavily that he died of
the injuries he received. A week after his death an
auctioneer’s clerk, who was wheeling the machine to his
master’s showrooms, was knocked down by a hansom cab
and killed - three deaths within a month. The history of
the wheel got abroad, and when it was put up for auction
no bids were made for it. It lay unnoticed amongst some
lumber for a time, when it was bought by a dealer for ten
shillings, and sold to a burglar. The same night he
cracked a crib, and was shot dead as he was riding awa\
with the swag. During the hubbub round the dead
cracksman an audacious thief made off with his wheel,
but a week afterwards made his appearance at the North
London Police Court charged with having run over and
killed an old lady.” A good lie !
* * * •
P. : “ Fishing at Lake Macquarie recently, I
caught several small ‘squire’ (red bream) with the mem-
brane of fins and tail almost wholly decayed. At the base
of the fins there was a slimy mucus, and the general color
of the fish was dull. As these coastal lakes are the nur-
series for our schnapper-grounds, the matter should be in*
vestigated that the disease may be diagnosed.”
Paragraphed in Adelaide papers that Admiral
Bridge and Lieut. Duff had “ left Adelaide on Wednesday
for a few days’ shooting on the Murray.” Whereupon an
irreverent person wrote asking if these big gentry would
be duly prosecuted for shooting in the close season ?
* * * *
Premier Kingston, waited on by a large deputa-
tion of Adelaide pietists recently, intimated his intention
of legislating for the suppression of street-betting, but
plainly refused to listen to the demand for repeal of the
Totalisator Act. “ I have had an opportunity of seeing
how things have worked before and after the totalisator
came into force, and I believe there was bigger betting in
the old days than there is now,” he said.
» * * *
“ Watch’em ” suggests to The Bulletin a cine-
matographic want—a dog-fight.
“ Chows make dexterous billiardists,” writes a
Bpllktin contributor. “ I have met several replicas of
Bret Harte’s no-savee heathen, who have allowed them,
selves to be inveigled into playing against cocky whites,
the latter being duly taken down. One feels a degree of
sympathy with the inscrutable yellow-man, whose absten-
tion from brag is a point so distinctly in his favor.”
• • • •
Recent Bulletin par. tells of an Ohio child,
blind at seven months old, who had a bad fall, thereby
losing both hearing and speech but regaining sight.
Writer had a friend in Rockhampton who was afflicted
with St-. Vitus’ dance. One day this individual, while
turning a corner, ran his hike into a dray, aud was thrown
off some yards with considerable violence. Got up some-
what shaken and stunned, and found—the St. Vitui
trouble had disappeared. It lias never appeared since.
P.S.—lie rode a hike with tyres.

The wife of a leading N.S.W. jockey, in apply-

got £5OO a year, in addition to substantial sums from
owners of winning horses.

Extract of Chinese letter to proprietor of
Kwong Yik W*n Bo : “ Be sure to spend the money I
send on ticket for Melbourne Cup only. There are many
sweeps on Melb. Cup, and if I get a horse the owner can-
not so easily blackmail me as he can in a race where there
is only one sweep."

In reply to a correspondent: The original
“Money” Miller didn’t object to his sons’ connection
with the turf. Although he never figured as a racing
man, he was understood to be more or less the master of
the Miller stable in its young days.

Although interesting in its results, it is not
gratifying to local builders that Mr. “Sam
should invariably give his orders on the other side of the
herring pond. When Mr. “Sol” Hyam decided to test
the qualities of the narrow and broad-tucked boats, he .
pave an order to the crack builders of the respective
styles, Reynolds and Joe Donnelly. Mr. Hordern might
follow suit, and give a Sydney order for a crack to meet
the Englishman.

A Question of Price.— The best price to pay for a
bicycle is the price of the Best Bicycle. That Red
Bird is the best. They are ridden by the better class
of cyclists.
Why do our competitors belittle Red Bird cycles?
Because they are afraid of honest competition with a
honest bicycle. Better pay for the Red Bird quality
than pay for repairs. “ That Red Bird Cycle is the
best.”
“ Under the best apple-tree are found the most
sticks and stones.” Some firms adopt (?) methods in
setting their goods against Red Birds, but Red Bird
salesmen never belittle other cycles. “They don’t
have to.” See !
Beware of the friend (?) who talks against Red Bird
cycles. He receives a commission to sell other wheels.
Red Bird cycles are sold direct by the manufacturers,
who have a reputation to lose. We have manufactured
better bicycles than anyone else for the past 20 years.
“You get the benefit of that experience.”
The Australian roads are strewn with the bleaching
wrecks of abandoned cycles, but no RED BIRDS are
seen among these sad monuments of cycle-construc-
tion. Red Bird quality is a sure guarantee against
mishap ; their first cost is last cost, as they never re-
quire repairs.
The perfect bicycle at last. The new little oil-bath
bearings of the Red Bird are as far ahead of othei
bearings as the pneumatic tyre is ahead of the solid.
Martin’s celebrated straightaway mile in lnnn. 1.
3-ssec. is proof of their easy running qualities.
The greatest road-race ever held in Australia was
the great Sydney-Goulburn race, a distance of 138
miles. A. E. Gra'em made his reputation in this great
event by breaking all records and winning first time-
prize He attributes much, of his success to the
superior qualities of his RED BIRD, which was the
only Red Bird in the race. “ Red Birds never grow
weary.” There is no road too rough, no journey too
*°Thk great Austral Wheel Race was won by 0. B.
Kellow on his Red Bird. He attributes his success to
its sterling quality.
The heart of the cycle is the bottom bracket. The
new adjusting chain-alignment bottom bracket of the
Red Bird is an improvement of the greatest import-
ance, and with it you will always have a perfect-
running bicycle. The bearings are absolutely dust-
proof and oil-retaining, the balls running in a continu-
ous oil-bath. These improvements make the Red
Bird the fastest wheel on earth, and they never require
repairs.

(From the Melbourne Advocate.)
Dave Kirk, the well-known and highly-popular
hero of the cycle track, was, on the Bth October of
last year, training in the Scotch College Grounds,
Melbourne, when, all of a sudden, and without
any warning, the tire of the leader broke, and
becoming at once entangled in the wheel, brought
the rider to the ground with terrific force. So
great was the impact that not a single limb or
member of the unfortunate cyclist’s frame escaped
serious injury. In all haste the victim was re-
moved to the Melbourne hospital, more dead than
alive, and quite unconscious from shock and
hemorrhage. A hasty examination of the
patient by the medical staff of that institution
showed them that the case was a most serious one,
and the grave looks of the surgeons in attendance
plainly indicated that they entertained but the
slightest hopes of the unfortunate young man’s
recovery. AIJ that the resources of that fully-
plenished hospital could command were brought
into play for the relief of the patient, but the
gravest fears were felt as to his ultimate safety.
The external injuries were not. after all, the most
serious—the symptoms of serious internal injury
to the nervous system were all too patent to the
practised and expert eye of the medical faculty.
So serious, indeed, was the case of Dave Kirk
considered, that an eminent surgeon who called to
see the patient declared it as his opinion that the
patient would, in all probability, never rise from
his sick bed. There poor Dave lay, betwixt life
and death, for about seven weary and exceedingly
trying months of pain and suffering, unable to
stir hand or foot, unable to move from side to side
—a victim of hemorrhage, spinal disease, pleurisy,
dysentery, complete paralysis of the body, and
last, but by no means least, locomotor ataxy, a
fearful complaint, which utterly prostrates the
human frame and destroys the power of the will;
so muCh so that the common functions of nature
are performed by a power absolutely extraneous to
the will. An idea of the dreadful injuries sus-
tained by the patient may be gathered from the
following details gleaned from Kirk by our repre-
sentative. His left heel had to be removed, and
32 stitches were inserted in that part of his
foot, 18 being placed in his right leg. The knee-
cap was smashed, and had to be lifted. Alto-
gether seven surgeons had to do with the sufferer,
who was reduced to a very weak and almost coma-
tose state after nine operations had been performed
on him. Mortification set in in parts of the lower
limbs, and artificial means had to be employed to
draw off the urine. The system was washed in-
ternally four times every day with water and car-
bolic acid, the stomach being cleaned out by means
of a tube. Injections were also largely employed.
This was a most singular case, and one which
must have afforded ample play for the physician’s
skill and experiment, yet it remained for Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills to effect a radical and com-
plete cure.
THE BRIGHT SIDE OF THE PICTURE.
AN ASTOUNDING CHANGE.
After seven months of bed-ridden existence in the
Melbourne General Hospital, Dave Kirk left that
institution with faint hopes of ever being able to
get about without the aid of crutches, with all
hopes vanished as to his ability to again enter the
cycle arena and carry off once more those brilliant
laurels which were at various big wheel gatherings
wont to be his, for he was Victoria’s largest scratch
crack.
In May last Kirk’s friends, being impressed by
the numerous authentic and spontaneous testi-
monials from persons of both sexes, of every age
and condition of life, and in all parts of the world,
recommended a course of the now world-famed Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People as a last re-
source. Marvellous, but true, as may be learnt
from the patient’s own lips, no sooner had he begun
to take these wonderfully curative pills than
Kirk experienced palpable relief. To let him de-
scribe himself the returning powers to his limbs
and nervous system generally:—“ Gradually the
sense of numbness began to disappear, and the
extremities acquired by degrees their wonted
healthy glow and warmth. While under treat-
ment in the hospital the sense of feeling entirely
disappeared, so much so that when the surgeons
would put a needle into any part of my'body I
was absolutely insensible to the fact. To be sure
of the absence of the sense of touch, the doctors
would conceal my vision, and then question me as
to the part they were piercing ; but my answers
clearly proved to them that I was absolutely bereft
of all sensibility !
“ After a month’s trial of Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills I was able to throw aside my crutches. But
one of the most powerful effects of the pills was
the restoration of functional health. Bowel
troubles had been a terrible trouble to me ever
since the day I met with the accident. Regu-
larity in this direction was perfectly set up, and T
am now, after four months taking of these pills, in
the enjoyment of perfect health.” It wanted no
assurance of this, as those who had seen the sub-
ject of this narrative are not slow to express their
surprise at the picture of health and almost com-
pletely resuscitated power of body and mind pre-
sented in the person of David Kirk.
But pi rliaps the most demonstrative proof of
the absolute numbness and absence of all feeling
in the patient while under treatment in the hos-
pital was the fact that, although he received a
shock from a powerful galvanic battery—a shock
so strong that the operator declared it to be half a
volt more than he had given to anyone in the in-
stitution for 40 years—Dave Kirk was utterly un-
able to feel its effect!
A PRACTICAL ILLUSTRATION OF
RESTORED VITALITY.
A few days ago Dave Kirk, who, by the way, is
to be found at his hairdresser’s and tobacconist’s
establishment, 401 Elizabeth-street north, caught
sight of a certain individual to whom, it Seems,
he had let out one of his bikes on hire and
who is said to have been possessed of so bad a
memory that he forgot to return the machine to its
owner ! As showing the restored strength and
vigor of the erstwhile bed ridden man, Kirk made
a clash, and a hand-to-hand struggle ensued. His
opponent was a powerfully-built fellow, and a much
heavier weight than Dave. Blood flowed freely
on both sides, but Dave proved one too many for
his man, and witli a dexterous effort managed, un-
assisted to get him into a cab, and took him uncere-
moniously off to lay a charge against him. That
was, perhaps, the most practical evidenceof restored
vitality that could well be given on the part of
the cycle champion. Naturally enough, the thou-
sand and one chums of the once-thought dead, but
now very much alive, cyclist are loud in their
praises of the world-famed Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills, and whenever Dave goes out he is beseiged
with congratulations on his regained health. In
common gratitude, the athlete never fails to give
the whole credit of his recovery to that wonderful
remedy discovered by Dr. Williams. Moreover,
Kirk is able to trench a large garden at home, and
walk two miles daily to his business place.
The following articles appeared in the Mel-
bourne Argus and Hkrald last year shortly after
Dave Kirk met with his mishap, proving that his
condition was considered hopeless :
EXTRACT FROM THE MELBOURNE
“ARGUS.”
“Everyone has heard of Kirk, the crack racer.
About a month ago he met with an accident
whilst training, and has been in the Melbourne
Hospital ever since. The medical authorities say
that he will never ride again, as his spine has
been so badly hurt that he will likely be paralysed
for life.”
EXTRACT PROM THE “HERALD.”
“Da y e Kirk, residing at the Coffee Palace,
v ictoria-street, North Melbourne, sustained
severe injuries through falling from his bicycle
while training on the track at the Scotch College
ground. The tyre of his bicycle burst, and Kirk
was thrown violently to the ground. He was re-
moved in a cab to the Melbourne Hospital.”
The latter extract appeared in the Herald on
the afternoon of the accident, October 9, 1896.
The former appeared in the Argus about a month
later, after the physicians and authorities had
E laced him under treatment, and discovered that
is injuries had resulted in paralysis, spinal disease,
and locomotor ataxia, and that he was entirely
beyond their aid. His case was accordingly, by
the physicians who examined him, pronounced in-
curable, and they told poor Kirk that he would
never rise from his bed again.

WA.LKA.LLA GOLDFIELD.
Tlie Famous Long Tunnel (Cohen’s) Line of Quartz Reef.
ABRIDGED PROSPECTUS OF THE
Southern Long Tunnel Gold Mine Company (No Liability).
(TO BE REGISTERED.) i «/ tf
CAPITAL : £20,000 in 40,000 Shares of 10s. each.
14,000 Shares paid up retained by the Vendor.
26,000 Shares are offered to the public on the following
terms :—ls. on application, and Is. 6d. on allotment.
All the money subscribed, less £l5OO, as cash payment
for the property, expenses of flotation and brokerage, to
be placed to the credit of the Company. The Company
may be registered when 15,000 Shares have been sub-
scribed. The money paid to the Vendor will only com-
pensate for the prospecting work and tunnelling on the
mine to date. The public thus receives a property pros-
pected and ready for immediate development, as the
reports attached will testify.
PROVISIONAL DIRECTORS—W. IT. Holyoak, Merchant,
Melbourne ; W. P. Wynne, Machinery Merchant, Balla-
rat ; John Haworth, Wool Merchant, Geelong; T.
Tupper (late Mines Manager Long Tunnel), Walhalla.
BANKERS— Bank of Australasia.
SOLICITORS— Fink, Best & Hall, Rialto, Collins-street,
Melbourne.
BROKERS —Melbourne : J. 11. Butler, 66 Elizabeth-st.;
Ballarat: H. S. Wyatt, Mining Exchange; Geelong:
Herbert W. Day, R.vrie-street; Walhalla: Charles
Refardt ; Sydney : W. H. Vivian, Stock Exchange.
LEGAL MANAGER and OFFICE (pro tern.)— Herbert
Wm. Day, 415 and 417 Collins-street, Melbourne.
This Company is being formed for the purpose of
acquiring and working the Leases Nos. 2295 and 2341,
having a total area of 53 acres 0 roods 30 perches, situated
at Rifle Butts Gully, Walhalla, 224 chains to the South of
the Long Tunnel Company’s ground.
The famous Long Tunnel (or Conen’s) Line of Reef has
been proved by tunnels set in at various points to extend
right through the Leases, and has been inspected and re-
ported on as the prospecting work was progressing, by
some of the most eminent and practical Mining Managers
in the Walhalla district, including :
Mr. R. NOBLE, Manager of the Long Tunnel Extended
G.M. Co.
Mr. WM. LAWSON, late Manager of the North Long
Tunnel and the World’s Fair G.M. Companies.
The late Mr. J. BARRAS, Manager Great South Long
Tunnel G.M. Co.
CALEB THOMAS, Esq., Government Mining Surveyor
for the District of Walhalla ; and
Mr. J. COLQUHOUN-THOMSON, Manager of the
Walhalla Tailings and Sluicing Co.
The practical knowledge and long experience of the
Long Tunnel Line of Reef possessed by these gentlemen
Is sufficient to recommend their reports and opinions.
A considerable amount of prospecting work has been
ione in proving the existence of the Long Tunnel Reef
on the property, including 696 feet of tunnelling, sinking
a shaft and winze on the lode.
No. 1 TUNNEL is driven 145 ft., and cuts a solid body of
stone 18in. wide, carrying free gold.
No. 2 TUNNEL at 74ft. intersects the first solid body of
stone 24in. wide ; at 89ft. the second body of stone is cut,
18in. thick, upon which a winze has been'sunk 10ft. ; and
at 148 ft. there is a third body of solid quartz 3ft. wide.
No. 3 TUNNEL is in 400 ft., and it is estimated that an-
other 24ft. will .-lit, the No. 1 Lode of No. 2 Tunnel.
The stone intersected in the tunnels carries payable
gold and is highly ~,.neralised. Front No. 3 Tunnel there
are about 400 ft. of backs, but as the reef will increase in
richness below water-level it has been recommended to
sink a permanent shaft at the No. 3 Tunnel, and drive
levels on to the lode.
There is abundance of timber on the ground, and every
facility for the economical working of the property. The
Mining Managers who have inspected the mine are of one
opinion ns to its value and possible developments.
It is intended to start by immediately erecting a 20-head
Battery to crush the stone from the 400 ft. of backs already
in sight. The Battery is to be increased as the lower
levels are reached.
EXTRACTS FROM REPORTS
Mr. WM. LAWSON reports : —“The property is situated
on the famous Cohen’s Line of Reef, and in in y opinion
these two Leases comprise a very valuable property.
“ The Upper or Main Tunnel (No. 2 Tunnel), has been
driven a total distance of 16()ft. At about 100 ft. from the
mouth of the tunnel a well-defined body of stone is inter-
sected and has been opened upon for some distance
South on the course of the reef. There are two clean-de-
fined walls, the reef has a slight underlay to the East.
This stone gives a fair prospect. At 16ft. further East
another well-defined body of stone is showing, on which a
winze has been sunk for about 20ft. The reef is about
18in. thick and gives fair prospects.
“ Further East a large belt of diorite dyke is intersected,
over 40ft. thick, and on the Eastern side of this dyke a
strong well-defined lode is met with between two belts of
dyke. The lode is fully 2ft. thick and carries fair gold.
“ These three lodes all have a slight underlay to the
East, but in my opinion this is merely local, and when a
little greater depth is attained they will be found to take
their proper course or underlay, and I am of opinion that
at no great depth it will be found that the different reefs
intersected in the tunnel will converge into one, as soon
as the two most easterly ones reach the dyke formation.
“ The strike or trend of the reefs are almost identical
with the Cohen’s —N. 19° W.
“ At a short distance lower down the hill another tunnel
(No. 1 Tunnel) has been put in a distance of 140 ft. At
about 90ft. in, a body of stone has been passed through,
the same being fully 2ft. wide and highly mineralised.
In my opinion this tunnel has not been extended far
enough to the east to cut the other reef intersected in the
upper tunnel.
“ A shaft has been sunk on the reef from the crown of
the adjoining hill, and here the reef is 2ft. thick, hard,
and well-defined, and carries gold and a good deal of
mineral.
“ In conclusion, I think this is a valuable property."
CALEB THOMAS, Esq., reports—
“ The formation is upper Silurian. There are two
tunnels driven and a shaft sunk on the line of reef. In
the upper tunnel three reef formations have been inter-
sected, having an average underlay of 73° E. ; the first,
about 70ft. in, is 2ft. wide, fairly good laminated stone ;
the second, at 90ft., has a winze sunk on it about 16ft.,
showing fair stone to the bottom ; the third, at 150 ft., is
in a rubbly quartz formation, and a very fair prospect of
free gold was washed from this in my presence.
“ The three formations may possibly converge in depth.
“ I consider that the claim is well worth developing, as
there are certainly good prospects to be obtained, and the
ground is easily worked."
Mr. R. NOBLE reports :—“ The No. 1 Tunnel is driven
143 ft. ; the reef is 15in. thick, and you can get gold by
washing.
“ The No. 2 Tunnel is driven 163 ft. ; the western reef is
cut at 100 ft., and the eastern reef at 125 ft.
“The western formation is about 2ft., and you can get
a little gold by prospecting.
“ The eastern reef is mixed with slate and quartz. About
4ft. between the walls you can get a fair prospect of gold
in this reef.
“ The large body of diorite dyke carries leaders and
spurs of quartz, from which you can get a prospect of gold
by crushing. The property being ■ situated close to the
South Great Long Tunnel, and on the Cohen’s line of reef,
I should recommend to drive south on the course of your
different lines of reef, and sink the No. 1 Winze.”
And further reports on 2nd October“ Since I last
visited your mine I see that you have driven a tunnel
400 ft. north-east, and passed through several formations
of loded country. ] think it would be advisable to sink a
shaft 300 or 400 ft., about 120 ft. south-east of the lower
tunnel. The different lines of lode will come together by
going down a depth. It would not be expensive to sink,
as it is a good, clean slate country.”
Mr. J. COLQUHOUN-THOMSON reports“ The
Leases are situated on the Long Tunnel or Cohen’s Line
of Reef, about 22J chains to the South of the Long
Tunnel Company’s ground. I found some very good
prospecting work has been done.
“ The westerly or No. 1 Tunnel is driven in 143 ft., ana
cuts a body of nice-looking laminated stone, 15in. thick,
from which I dollied a sample and got free gold.
“ The No. 2 or Upper Tunnel thoroughly proves the
value of the ground. I took measurements and found
that it intersects three well-defined bodies of solid stone
at the following distances : —At 74ft. the first body of
stone, 24in. wide, is cut, which has been opened upon
sufficiently to show that it is between clean well-defined
walls. I got free gold from samples I took. At 89ft. the
second body of stone, fully 18in. wide, is intersected. A
winze has been sunk 16ft. on this. I took a sample from
all over, and on crushing same got a payable prospect of
gold. The walls of this No. 2 body of stone are also well-
defined. From this point a body of diorite containing
quartz leaders occurs, and at 148 ft. the third body of
stone, fully 36in. wide, isi ntersected. Samples taken
gave good payable prospects.
“ No. 3 Tunnel is set in from the creek level, about
150 ft. lower than No. 2. Is driven in 400 ft. At 20ft. from
the mouth a body of stone is intersected, 18in. wide,
highly mineralized and carrying good gold. From point
50rt. to 7lft. in this tunnel, the country is very heavily
mineralised. At the face, the -slate carries bands of
mineral.'
“ 1 found pretty heavy water making at the face, and
from the underlay I estimate that'within 24ft., No. lLode
in No. 2 Tunnel should be cut.
“ The No. 2 Tunnel proves the existence of the line of
reef, and I am of opinion that the three bodies of stone
will converge into one at a depth.
“ The three stones are alike in character,- nicely lamin-
ated and carrying heavy mineral. The total width of
stone visible is'Gft. 6in., carrying payable free gold.
“ l consular that the property htis been sufficiently pro-
spected, and that it should now be worked to.win the gold.
That although you would have nearly 400 ft, of backs from
your No. 3 Tunnel, I am of the opinion that you should go
below water-level. The rich stone got at a depth from this-
line of reef recommends this.
“ I would, therefore, recommend to sink a shaft to the
south-east of No. 3 Tunnel, and crosscut east and west.
From the nature of the country, this can be done at a
comparatively small cost; sinking a few hundred feet
would give faces for a large output of stone.
“ In conclusion, I consider your property a very valuable
one. From the nature of the country and the surface
facilities, it can be opened up and worked very econo mi
cally."

At long, long last ! Once more has Lon-
don E. News reproduced—as “ Our Short Story ”
—a Bulletin yarn ; but, this time, mirabile diet'd,,
with acknowledgment of source. Conscience-
smite or show-up ?
Premier Braddon claims Tasmania as “the
most loyal of all the self-governing colonies.”
True; and Van Dobson is the loyalest Tassy of
the lwt. The Jubilee illuminations of ordinary
citizens were limited by the prospective length Of
the bill, and their gorgeous designs in twisted
gaspipe sputtered for two brief nights of glory,
only to be consigned to the scrap-heap of oblivion
on the morning of the third day. But Dobson’s
device bids fair to blazon forth his devotion to his
Queen for more years than anybody can remem-
ber. Overlooking Hobart, on a fine, clear hillside,
he had the letters V.R. over 60 deeply trenched .
into the green sward in conspicuous characters
30vds. long. This brand is clearly visible from !
every part of the town and port, and can’t be
rubbed out so long as Dobson holds the deeds of
the paddock. The idea was probably borrowed
from Mt. Wellington, where a symmetrical “S.T."
(formed by the deep snow lingering in the gullies
on the mountain-face) heralds the approach of
Spring Time as regularly as the swallows home-
ward fly.
A Nyah (Vic.) Fat man wrote to Melb.
Trades Hall Council the other day, stating that
there was plenty of profitable employment at
clearing land to be had in his district. He also
observed that the “dissipated unemployed ” had
“ extravagant notions about labor and hours,” and
referred to the need of working by moonlight to >
make good wages at clearing land. Moreover, he
assured the council that working by moonlight
was fine fun. It would certainly save a lot of
sleep. And what does alow, unemployed person
want with sleep, anyhow ?
N.S.W. Premier Reid’s brother Bob is a
pillar of Freetrade and piety in Melbourne. As
an advocate of religion and lots of it in State
schools he is excessively zealous. Witness : “ The
reading of the Bible is the birthright of every
Britisher. Our past history teems with instances
of heroism and nobility of character when this
privilege has been fought for in years gone by.”
Past history also teems with instances proving
that the Britisher considers it his birthright to
force the other fellow to read the Bible, as Reid
and his ilk are trying to do now. Nobody wants
to prevent Reid exerting his birthright and read-
ing the Bible till all’s blue, but it is when he
wants to force the other fellow to read, and to
read according to Reid’s lights, that the public
plants down its hoof. It now remains for the
Reid party to take another lesson from history,
and set up a pillory, a scavenger’s daughter, and
other little aids to true religion
Since Earth was young
The Bards have sung
The rapture keen and exquisite
The lover feels
As softly steals
His arm round Her in the white moonlight.
But keener far
His pleasures are
Who, when his day’s work’s finished quite,
Springs forth alert,
In pants and shirt,
To grub up stumps in the stilly night.
The Fatman will
Respect him still—
The Fatman doth his own kind know
For he, we wis,
Who’d slave like this,
A Fatman is—in embryo.

Mr. Button writes later:-“The Pills I had from you
were of great benefit to me and others. In one case,
when three hunters and trappers were travelling about
the ranges near my place on the Rose River, one was taken
very ill ; his mates intended taking him in a waggon to
Wangaratta (about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor.
I gave him a few of your Pills. I am pleased to inform
you that, having to pass their camp next day, I found
him up and pegging-out his skins and looking well. This
Bpeaks volumes for your valuable Remedies.”
