pRELIMINARY ANNOUNCEMENT.
Theatre Royal,
Brisbane.
Miss Elsie Lander (late leadintr lady Saunder-
son's National Company, U.S.A.) invites the
attention of the play-going public of Brisbane to
the fact that she has leasea the Theatre Royal,
Brisbane, for an extended season of high-class
sensational melodrama. Twelve startling and
realistic dramas, entirely new to Australia, have ,
been secured. Several of these have proved
phenomenal successes in America, and all are
good. They will be mounted in a style superior
to anything ever yet seen in Brisbane. Entirely
new and beautiful scenery is being painted ; new
and pretty costumes and wonderful mechanical
effects have been prepared. Miss Lander will be
supported by a strong company of the very best
favorite Australian artists. The stage is being
strengthened to carry the horses introduced in
some of the plays, and special precautions will be
taken to safeguard the Theatre during the ex-
plosions and fire-scenes which will be placed before
the public on a scale unrivalled throughout Aus-
tralia. There will be startlingly novel sensations
in every play. No larce theatres are available in
Sydney or Melbourne for some .months to come,
and Miss Lander is therefore beginning her Aus-
tralian tour at Brisbane. The season will com-
mence early in November; each drama will be
played for
SIX NIGHTS ONLY;
and there will be no revivals.

The first private benefaction received by
Sydney University consisted of .£5OO, from one
Solomon Levey. The money was originally in-
tended for orphans at the Grammar School.
Dear Bulletin, —ls there any solid reason
why people arrested on suspicion of being of un-
sound mind should be treated as drunks, thieves,
or burglars ? It is a standing disgrace that unfor-
tunate lunatics (and their friends) should be sub-
jected to the coarse indignity of being haled before
a police-court so that the Bench can order them to
a receiving-house for treatment and safety. Such
cases are always decided by the medical officer’s
testimony, and surely a plain double or treble cer-
tificate should be sufficient to consign the poor
patients to the care of the metropolitan authorities
without the formal public proceedings of a court.
—ltan.
Prussian War Office scientists assert, as
the result of numerous experiments on men who
didn’t know they were being so used, that the
consumption of small quantities of sugar gives
increased muscular power, and therefore increased
work value. A test series of similar experiments
with saccharin, for comparison with the sugar
series, showed that on the days when sugar was
used as the food-sweetener far more work was got
• through than on the saccharin days. The ex-
planation is that muscular exertion reduces the
amount of sugar in the blood below the normal,
hence the extra value of sugar (sweetener' and,
food) over saccharin (sweetener only).
When the machinery of the Sydney
Catholic Press was officially blessed, even the
clerical “glory av God and tin per cint.” share-
holders regarded the ceremony as “more fiscal
than Popery.” But it would seem that the
blessing, which, among other things, sanctioned
the sale of the paper at the church-doors on Sun-
day, has not worked in the right direction. In a
recent issue the holy organ prints a long Jeremiad,
of which this is a hunk :
Certain it is that the difficulties under which the
Catholic Press struggled into existence here in Sydney,
and the rather cold reception it received at its birth from
a certain class of Catholics who should have hailed it
with enthusiastic joy, are not calculated to impress us
with the idea that a new era for Catholic journalism has
arrived in Australia. We leave unnoticed the self-
sufficient, ignorant Catholics, of whom there are some
unfortunately in our midst, whose contribution to a great
and momentous work such as that in which we are
engaged consists of stupid, malignant criticism and im-
potent opposition.
A Boon to Sufferers. —A well-known stock-
dealer and storekeeper announces that, if written
to confidentially, he will reply and state the plan
he pursued by which he was restored to health
and vigor after years of suffering from Nervous
and Physical Weakness, Loss of Vigor, etc. He
writes : “I have no scheme to extort money from
anyone whomsoever. I was victimised by quacks
and empirics until I lost faith in mankind ; but,
thank heaven, I am now well, vigorous, and
: strong, and anxious to make this certain mean's of
j cure known to all. Having nothing to sell, I
i want no money.” Address—J. KILIAN, Store-
keeper, Daveyston, S.A.*
The late Barnato’s personal estate falls
short of the million. A good lie is told about
Barney’s start. When diamond-broking at Kim-
berley he was long suspected to be an illicit-buyer,
but was too clever to be caught. At last the De
Beers people set a trap for him. Giving one of
their Kaffir boy ’tecs, a big diamond, they sent
him to Barnato’s office to trade as if “on the
cross.” Barney agreed to buy the stone, but said
he must go “ over the road ” for the money, and
put the diamond in his safe till he came back.
Going straight to the De Beers compound, he
stated that a Kaffir had tried to sell him a stolen
stone, and compelled them to send an officer back
with him to ai’rest the illicit trafficker. Where-
upon the biter was bit. But the cream of the joke
came in the fact that when Barney was called
upon to • show the stone he opened his safe and
■ took out one worth about £2, and, as the De Beers
men couldn’t own up to the “plant,” the cute
little Hebrew cleared several thousand by the coup.

Sheep-owners and cattle-owners are just
now at loggerheads in Bananaland. The owners
of sheep find the dingoes a greater curse than mar-
supials ; the owners of cattle vice-versa. Conse-
quently, the latter would like to protect the
dingoes to some extent, arguing, likewise that
where rabbits are few in number the dingo keeps
them down. We may yet see a reserve proclaimed
for the cultivation and propagation of the wild
dog.
A Vic. goldfields town has a Chinese camp;
also a Chinese solicitor. One evening lately, as
the camp was busy driving out the devil, (an inter-
esting little propitiatory religious service), some
boys spoilt the proceedings, first by throwing
crackers into the flames, and then by stoning the
worshippers. Police-court proceedings followed,
and the Chinese lawyer was engaged for the
defence. Probably the first time, this, in an Aus-
tralian court, when a Chinese lawyer has been on
one side, appearing for Europeans, with Chinese
principals on the other side.

The Turning of the Tide .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Up the creaking stairs for the last time. There
was exultation in the thought. At length he
could make his quittance with a clear conscience.
He had done his best to keep in a world of which
he sickened. Though he hated it he had done his
He looked round his attic, the crowded lumber-
room whioh Mrs. Pankin let him occupy almost
for nothing. He was like a bit of broken-down
furnituro himself, with his bent back and sloping'
shoulders. His face was pallid, the skin coarsened
by poverty long drawn-out; but his eyes were
bright and clear, clearer than eyes should be.
The time was come for him to make his exit.
He saw that clearly. A little while and he would
drift past even the desire for death. He shud-
dered as he thought of such mental degradation.
An uncertain table stood by the window, and
the young twilight fell drearily upon a heap of old
magazines and newspapers thrown down upon it.
Clearing a space with his elbows, he sat down, laid
his head on the table and let his thoughts wander
backwards.
He was eager to go. He had the means ready
to hand; but he "must gather himself together
before he took the last little step. He was not
afraid of death in the ordinary sense; but he
feared the first few minutes after death. He
might be confused, and forget why he had come ;
he might not be able to explain, as he could now,
how impossible he had found it to live, how he
had applied, and sought, and begged for work for
weeks and months. He began recalling all the
people he had applied to ; he felt like making a
list of their names and handing it in at the Cate.
Of course there were one or two positions which
he was secretly glad had been refused him ; still,
he had applied for them ; he had done his duty.
There was that clerkship, for instance; 255. a
week. He had been definitely refused it to-day.
The manager had broken it to him gently; he had
left the office with a light heart. Of course if he
had got it, it would have been his duty to stay.
He would have had no right to emigrate to that
brave country where all his hopes were now fixed.
How glad he was he had not got if>!
He rose smiling, his last fear vanished. He
must remember to mention he had tried for that
clerkship. He felt, not gay, not excited, but
soberly, sensibly happy. He looked out of the
window and felt a throb of pity for the miserable
millions who were yet not miserable enough to
justify their emigrating. Then his thoughts leapt
forward to a future now full of interest. What
were they thinking of him in the other world ?
For, of course, they were aware of the new com-
rade journeying towards them. He took the
white packet in his hands and caressed it smil-
ingly. Perhaps they would not speak to him at
first. Doubtless suicides wt-ro the illegitimate
children of the spiritual world, come without
authority. But he would work off the bar sinis-
ter, never fear.
He untied the string. He was so nearly there
that he wondered he could hear the noises of this
earth so plainly ; Mrs. Pankin’s footstep below,
the opening and shutting of doors, the raucous
voices of his fellow lodgers. He wondered, more-
over, that no sound reached him from that far
shore to which he was hastening, no cheery voice
giving him welcome, no warning even, bidding
him to return. , TT
But he could not have returned. He was so
nearly there that had he thrown the packet out of
the window he felt as if he would be committing
suicide backward, and to commit suicide from
heaven to earth must surely be worse than from
earth to heaven. _ . . , , -jr
No, he was not afraid. He looked back with
wonder at a time, two months ago, when he had
secretly wept and prayed against this doom, then
the most miserable employment would have been
eagerly seized. Now his heart was uplifted with
thankfulness that his petition of to-day had been
refused. * .
A few minutes later, still smiling, he raised a
glass to his lips.
* * * *
“ The cruelty of it ! ” sobbed the girl-lodger.
The landlady had opened a letter, come by the
early morning’s post. It was addressed to the
dead man, and stated that Messrs. Rmgwood and
Co., having been disappointed in their choice re
the clerkship at twenty-five shillings, would be
glad to see Mr. John Roberts at ten o clock this
morning, with a view to, &c.
The girl held the letter in her hand, and her
tears blurred the written page.
“Todo it just as the tide was turning ! Oh lit
he had known . Perhaps he does know !
Perhaps he did, and was laughing at them.
Maoriland.
CONSTANCE CLYDE.
What We Eat.
[Desperate remedy is proverbially justified of desperate
disease, and concern for the public stomach—not desire
to experiment upon the public gorge—induces The Bul-
letin to print the following perfumed items in all their
beauty unadorned. “ Revolutions are not to be made
with rosewater.” —Ed. B.]
A correspondent sends The Bulletin a
cutting from a N.S. W. country paper recording the
death from phthisis of a man who had for the past
three years been employed in a. local creamery.
Considering the germ-receptivity of milk it
wouldn’t be easy to say how much phthisis-poison
may not have been diffused from that establish-
ment. It is also pointed out that, in the country,
when fowls become sick, they are generally packed
off as quickly as possible for sale—in town I—and1 —and
the same thing, it is alleged, is common in the
case of pigs. What a howling absurdity, in the
face of these pleasing little customs of rural inno-
cence, is the care exercised in the inspection of
dairy cattle!
J „ „ * *
There have been some truly awful dis-
closures of the horrors of certain branches of the
Melb. food-supply, but for picturesque beastliness
the report of a Board of Health officer, re a certain
suburban sausage and “small-goods’ factory just
unearthed, has not been equalled by the story of
any other offal-emporium. This establishment was
just a seething heap of old blood, rotting, sewage,
slaughter-house slops, fragments of cow, pig, skins,
hair, trotters, and maggots, and over the pile
streamed miles of ancient intestines, and the smell
offended thenostrilsof the inspector’s horse tethered
100yds. off. “The pits contained rotten, stinking,
coagulated blood.” “ The woodwork was bespat-
tered to the thickness of an inch. ’ “ The over-
flow ran on the floor some 15 inches deep. A
shed, used for the preparation of sausage-casings,
held “ a quantity of prepared and blown-out cas-
ings for polonies and German, ’ besides heaps of
decomposing trotters and wool-tags.” A shed,
where pickling was going on, contained terribly
offensive gut-tubs,” and the same unspeakable
stinks. The drainage of the whole place ran into
a hole adjacent to the sheds, and this hole was a
working* mass of green slime. So horrors on
horrors’ heads accumulate until we come to the
crowning horror of all on one of the upper floors
the bloated, putrid carcase of a horse ! The pre-
liminary dreadfulness is bad beyond all superla-
tives, but that horse— that dead, disgusting, irrele-
vant horse ! —simply shrieks for explanation, and,
to the writer,"the mystery of it will not, till the
day of his death, depart from the common sausage
of commerce, or the polony, or the German, or
the pallid sausage of pork.
* * * *
Thus Dr. Constantine, of Boston Health
Department: —
The next time you buy any fruit from a street-peddler,
it mioht be well to remember that it was probably
ripened in some filthy tenement-house bed. It is a com-
mon practice for Italian fruit-vendors to hasten the
ripening of bananas, oranges and lemons by placing them
in their beds, where the fruit receives the animal heat
from their bodies. Fruit subjected to this process will
ripen in an incredibly short time.
Verb. sap.
* * * *
Ed. Bulletin,— While the Sydney in-
spectors of nuisances are “lumbering” the city
Chow for dirty back-yards it would be well if they
(the inspectors) extended their labors to the
market-gardens. Yesterday I took a short-cut
through some Chow gardens on the Parramatta
River, and saw one of the Johns cutting fine,
healthy-looking watercress which was growing
in a gutter of coffee-colored water. About 30
yards away ran a cess-pit, used by the 12 or 14
yellow men who work the place, and it was flow-
ing into the gutter the watercress was being taken
from. And watercress is not cooked, but eaten
raw.—Welly NT.

From Q. Govt. Bacteriologist Pound’s
report on a Brisbane dairy
Quagmire of mud. . . . All the way from the water-
tank to the cow-bails the ground was in a filthy condi-
tion, but on arrival at the latter I am afraid that I could
not be explicit enough in describing their disgusting and
dilapidated condition. I am perfectly sure that the
manure had never been cleaned away since the dairy was
founded. . . . Cows . . . filthy condition, the
udders and teats being covered with a mixture of black
mud and manure . . . poor, miserable wasters .. •
noticeable chronic cough . . . would have reacted te
the tuberculin test.
Pound had the “healthiest” cow milked, the
milkman ordering his boy to wash the teats first;
boy did so with a quart of water in a milking-
bucket
As the mud softened, the boy roughly rubbed it off
with his already filthy hand into the bucket . . .
simply emptied out the dirty water, and commenced
milking straight away into the same bucket which con-
tained all the sediment, as mud, manure, &o. . . .
Milking tended very considerably to clean the teats of
the cow to the disadvantage of the milk. At the same
time, the filth which was removed naturally found its way
from the hands of the milker into the bucket ... an
eruptive disease of the teats and lower portion of the
udder . . . vesicular-looking sores, several of which
were suppurating . . . milk carried with it a quantity
of pus and blood from the ruptured sores.
By gorge ! What price Brisbane rumanmilk now !
And the Q. Beg. Council doesn’t want dairymen
nterfered with !
Yic. Government has gazetted a perpetual
close season for the old-man or large grey kanga-
roo (Macropus giganteus) and the great red kanga-
roo ( Macropus rufus), but has left the small grey
brush kangaroo outside the pale.

Life was a Burden.
TWENTY YEARS OF AGONY.
ACUTE INDIGESTION. LIVER AND KIDNEY TROUBLES.
FORCED TO ABANDON HIS WORK.
A GREAT VICTORY FOR GOULD’S TINY TONICS.
A reporter, hearing certain remarkable and sensational statements made concerning Mr. S. .
Edwards, wheelwright, of Summer-street, Orange, decided to pay the old gentleman an impartial
visit, and to personally attest the truth or otherwise of these startling rumors.
Mr. Edwards is 60 years of age, and came to the colony 32 years ago. He has lived in the town-
ship named for 20 years, where there are few people better known. . . j. ~ .
P “ It was this way,” said Mr, Edwards, in answer to inquiries For twenty tedious years—that
is, since 1877— I’ve suffered fearful agonies from acute indigestion, coupled with liver and kidney-
troubles Everything I ate, even the lightest food, would cause an ominous pain in my chest, and the
food would stay^then?like a leaden weight. Tired and languid, I always felt oppressed, whilst the
pains at times were absolutely unbearable; robbed of all my. wonted energy, m melancholy strain, I
consulted leading doctors both in Orange and Dubbo. Their ind To
ever, useless, and they did not seem to know from what affliction 1 suffered. And so
I became worse. Excruciating pains darted through my back and[legs and left£
also afflicting my hands, which at other times were often numb and lifeless, and
I have seen the time when I would have to rub my finger-joints to restore' all this 7
kidneys became disorganised, and gravel caused me heart-rending anguish. Ihen, on top ot all this,
cameTtouch“f sunsfroke, so that no one could credit the bitterness of my plight who had not them-
selves suffered similarly; and, indeed, there are many afflicted not much lea
severely than was I. Patent medicines got every trial from me, but no perma-
nent relief followed. One day, however, things reached a crisis. Suddenly,
whilst trying to work, I was taken with a sharp, tormenting torture, as it a keen
knife had been driven into my back. Almost collapsed, I was forced to abandon
my work and go home, which I reached with difficulty and in a completely
exhausted condition. I was forced to my bed, and there I lay, awake anc
weary, in utter and complete prostration, becoming weaker and weaker, and
looking hopelessly for the end, for hope had died, and I only thought of death .
“ Then a wonderful event happened !
“ I suddenly found a cure ! . , . ,
“ The way it happened was this. A pamphlet was left at the house,
and my wife brought it to me. There I read of a case exactly the same
as mine, in which a cure had been miraculously effected by Gould s
Tiny Tonic Liver Pills. My wife went across to the chemist, Mr.
De Beare, and at once I gave them a trial. This was the happiest day of my
life, for from the first the pills commenced to improve me. hor some tune I
continued to take them, and step by step I continued to advance in vigor,
health, and strength. My appetite returned, the shooting P» m ® ““g Jfjf®
and back and shoulder vanished away, and that dull, dead, tired fee, ng
which oppressed me was replaced by a bright and buoyant spirit, and a new
lease of cherry, happy life. I left my bed, my whole system rejuvenated by
twenty years, and here I am hale and hearty to-day, able to get about without
difficulty, and able to conduct a busy wheelwright s business. I took nothing
else but Gould’s Tiny Tonic Liver Pills, and to these alone I ascribe my
speedy and astounding recovery. For the last three years, prior to taking the
pills, I had not worked more than 18 months. My wife has also taken them
with great benefit to herself, and Mr. De Beare, the chemist, will also sub-
stantiate my case.”

The Dead Crusoe.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We stole, like creatures in a dream,
Upon a fair, far Southern sea,
Where wave and sky evolved supreme
And sense-subduing harmony.
A sapphire sky, a waste of blue,
A trance of silence everywhere,
And listlessly we passed, a crew
Of ghosts aboard a ship of air.
I thought these pure, lone waters ne’er
Had mirrored deep a gilded prow,
No carved canoes had drifted there,
No eyes beheld the grace till now.
We’d come through spume by tempest spun,
It seemed, to where a sea forgot
Lay spangled ’neath a. changeless sun,
And pain, and grief, and death were not.
At length an island slowly reared
Against the golden morn, afar;
A drear, dead land of rock, it seared
The face of Ocean like a scar.
But, creeping to a crescent bay,
We saw, with dim, adoring eyes,
All glistening in the early ray,
A paradise of palms arise.
We anchored there, and for a day
I loitered wherein wild excess
A small, deserted Eden lay
Begirt with sand and barrenness.
A breathless calm was over all,
No savage lurked, no beast broke through,
And ranked upon the brown sea-wall
The sea-birds drowsed above the blue.
But ere the sun had set I found,
Hid deep in ferns and plants unknown,
And vines that curled its rafters round,
A ruined hut of wood and stone.
My search disclosed two twisted shoes
Of hard, black hide, a wooden bowl,
A clasp-knife, such as sailors use—
A tally on a rotted pole.
From these I wove a common tale
Of some gaunt seaman rafted here,
And watching for the dawning sail
In solitude from year to year.
At last a boat rode to the sands,
He heard the cheery call of men,
And clinging to their hard, brown hands
Went out unto the world again.
Yet musing so upon the bluff
That overhung the dappled bay,
I thought this island fair enough
To charm its prisoner for aye.
Then past a rocky point I saw,
Against the cliff’s opposing brown,
A sight that chilled with sudden awe,
And tore my pleasant fabric down.
A staff that once had held on high
A signal to the desert sea
Stood broken ; from a fissure nigh
A skeleton was facing me ;
A rain-washed thing of dead, grey bone,
It sat, with cavemed eyes intent,
In spectral stateliness alone,
A grim and awful monument !
One arm the shattered post embraced,
One hand hung by its hollow hip;
It stared across the glittering wa.ste,_
And waited for a white- winged ship.
I read upon that grisly face _
A legend of supreme despair,
And, fiercely hating all the place,
Fled forth, and left it watching there.
EDWAD DYSON.
The early-closing provisions of the Vic.
Shops Act, after holding water for years, came
to grief in the Law-courts, the other day. Ihe
Act explains that a shop shall be considered
to be open as long as goods are “ offered for sale.’
Judge Holroyd suddenly decides that having the
windows and door open, and the gas lighted, and
the stock exposed, and assistants behind the
counter ready to serve, customers, is not offering
goods for sale ; they can only be offered for sale
if someone comes in and they are actually ten-
dered to him or her over the counter. In the
absence of this evidence, the law, as interpreted
by Holroyd, assumes that the shop is kept open
and the gas burned for fun and the moral improve-
ment of the staff, and that a customer would be
indignantly thrown out if he presented himself.
Rats!
As no one knows when or how he may get
into gaol, this is of general interest: —
Editor Bulletin,—Be bugs in the Saltpetriiire. The
upper corridors of E wing, Darlinghurst gaol, can show
similar swarms. The authorities don t seem to know any
wa}- of coping with them—a hint from the Parisian
experts might be of value-and, anyhow, their inspec-
tions are made in davtime when the carnivora have dis-
appeared. Thev breed, so I was told, in the timbers of
the roof, ciome down the outside of the walls, and through
the ventilating-holes. In the early morning stragglers
can be seen i^oing 1 back that track. A careful
smearing of the whole circumference of the ventilators
with a mixture of soft soap and sulphur (to be got strictly
sub rosa from the painter’s shop) is recommended by the
initiated as checking the influx. I Dever tried it. We
used to get lengths of cocoa-nut fibre from the mat shop
and fat from the meat rations and extemporise tapers.
Matches are non-contraband. We lit up at midnight,
when sleep was out of the question—after the warder’s
round —and smashed all before us. I have seen white-
washed walls of cells to which the doorposts of Israel
in Egypt at Passover couldn’t have been a circumstance
for blood-sprinkling. The lower corridors seem to be
freer. What it is like in summer Heaven only knows.—
One Who Was There in Winter.
The amiable N.S.W. District Court Judge
Backhouse lately lectured on the Advantages
of Impecunosity. The Judge is credited with
possessing a good private income in addition to
his oiricial pay of £l5OO a year with handsome
travelling expenses ; and as at 60, or on previous
disablement, a D.C. Judge has the right of re-
tiring on a pension of £750, he can, provided he
invests, say, £250 • annually in life - assurance,
always safely live, while he holds office, at the
rate of £lOOO a year. A N.S.W. D.C. Judge’s
billet is about the best, freest and safest post
under Govt. ; The Bulletin would prefer it to
any other. Considering the disadvantages under
which Judge Backhouse labors, he deserves great
credit for being so respectable and good-tem-
pered a man. The optimistic view taken of
poverty and first - offending solicitors by the
gilded Judge is no doubt partially due to ■ the
fact that he is renowned as a pedestrian and keeps
his liver in working order. D.C. Judge Gibson,
who walks less, is not so sound either in his law
or in his liver. Only the other day lie sent up a
man for three years’ hard for stealing a ninepenny
tin of baking powder.
Reremoa and the Pearly
Nautilus.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When a man wants to find something
there always are a hundred people to tell him
where to find it, but never a man who will go and
fetch it. It was so with Rossmatin and his pearly
nautilus. At Rotorua a man in the Priest’s Bath
told him to try the coast, north of Mangonui. He
had seen shoals of nautili there, and had them for
breakfast. So Rossmatin went and tried ; he had
a theory that his cephalopod spawned .most freely
in southern latitudes, though he had hardly hoped
to find it south of parallel 25. But Science is
nothing if you have no faith. So Rossmatin had
come north of Mangonui.
The man in the Priest’s Bath had recommended
him to “chip in” with the Maoris—they would
find his shell-fish for him, Rossmatin had
“ chipped hi ” with Reremoa.
Reremoa was pretty, 18, aboundingin hair black
as coal—a Maori sea-nymph.
Rossmatin was a zoologist, an entomologist, a
lover of bugs and butterflies, snakes and centi-
pedes ; a palseologist, an ichthyologist—a great
scientist. But his mania was for cephalopods,
curious sea denizens of the genus mollusca. He
could tell you all about dibranch iata and tetra-
branchiata —which are quite beyond the ken of
ordinary men —and would, if you let him, discourse
by the hour on the pearly nautilus, to search for
which he had devoted his life. He said this shell-
fish was unique; there was but one specimen
extant —the one brought home by the Challenger
Expedition, and preserved in a pickle-bottle. The
scarcity of this cephalopod had caused Rossmatin
much tribulation. It was his passion as well as
his despair. He lived to explain its life-history.
“For,” he would say, “it stands in an order all
by itself, the sole remaining representative of the
ancient ammonites of the Palaeozoic and Mesozoic
times; the chief of all molluscs ; the greatest of all
invertebrates; divided from all octopoda and
decapoda by a wide gap. ” Ah !
Rossmatin had visions of dead ammonites as
big as cart-wheels, but he would be content with a
real live, pearly nautilus the size of a half-crown.
But fate was hard on Rossmatin. For 20 years it
had cast his lot in London, whereas his cephalopod
lived somewhere in the South Seas. It had also
made him poor. But, after years of economy, he
had come to seek his heart’s desire.
» * * *
The morning was clear and still; the sea
was unruffled by a ripple—its surface was like
blue glass. Reremoa and Rossmatin were walk-
ing along the golden beach. Reremoa could speak
but little English. Rossmatin could speak no
Maori, yet he was explaining all about his
cephalopod. He told of his tireless search—how
at Ralum, in New Britain, he had dredged and
dived for a year in 60 fathoms of water, but all in
vain ; how he had explored the New Guinea
coast for six months, and had been nearly
drowned ; how the islanders of Guadalcanal: and
Ysabel, in the Solomons, had tried to spear him ;
how at Lilli, in the Loyalty group, he had fallen
ill with a strange disease, and the French, doctors
at Noumea had sent him south to Rotorua to try
the puias. Reremoa did not understand, but she
thought Rossmatin the handsomest pakeha she
had seen. Rossmatin told how he had read that
the natives of the New Hebrides caught his cepha-
lopod in their lobster-pots, and ate them with
relish. At Bogotu there had been shoals of
sharks ; at Vella Lavella he had been speared in
the left arm.
Reremoa wondered what this pupu fish could be.
But she listened with all ears, determined to do
all she could for the pakeha —she had the indulgent
Maori disposition.
Rossmatin explained the construction of his
mollusc ; how it had curved nurtures and plain septa.
Reremoa looked into his blue eyes, and wondered
where they got their color from. She thought a
yellow, close-clipped beard was the best sort of
beard she had ever seen. Rossmatin explained
that your true ammonite has complex septa and
nurtures that are zigzagged, foliated, or irregularly
lobed. Reremoa thought it would be delightful
to touch the soft, white throat just below where
the sunburn mark was, to see if the creaminess
rubbed off. And so they walked on ; Rossmatin
garrulous of turrilities, discoidal forms, and in-
volute types ; Reremoa as wise as a Maori girl
need be. At last they came to a reef that jutted
out into the sea.
Reremoa pointed down into the deep, still water.
“ Look,” she said, “ your pupu fish is down there.
I’ll dive down and get you one. ’ Rossmatin was
used to native ways. He did not demur —he
wanted his nautilus.
Reremoa dived decorously and left her single
garment floating on the tide. Rossmatin clutched
the floating smock, and waited.
Reremoa came to the surface ; there was nothing
in her hand. Down she went again. Again she
came to the surface with nothing.
“Reremoa!” shouted Rossmatin, “ there s
nothing down there. Come out-there may be
sharks about.” , .
A third time Reremoa dived ; a third time she
came to the surface. .
“Look!” she cried, “ kuku. I’ve got it!
Her face beamed with pleasure.
“Quick! Let me see, ” cried Rossmatin.
Reremoa swam to the rock he stood on and clam-
bered up beside him. Rossmatin clutched the
shell-fish that she held.
“Damn! It’s a common mussel.”
Reremoa’s face fell. All her joy vanished.
She looked pitifully at Rossmatin, as though she
had done wrong.
Rossmatin looked at Reremoa. Then Rossmatin
clutched Reremoa. . ,
He had found something better than his pearly
nautilus.
M.L.
